micah CLARKE 

 his statement as make to his three GRANDCHILDREN JOSEPH , GERVAS , and 
 reuben during the hard winter of 1734 


 by Arthur Conan Doyle 
 Chapter i. of Cornet Joseph Clarke of the Ironsides 

 it may be , my dear grandchild , that at one time or another I 
 have tell you nearly all the incident which have occur during my 
 adventurous life . to your father and to your mother , at least , I know 
 that none of they be unfamiliar . yet when I consider that time wear 
 on , and that a grey head be apt to contain a fail memory , I be 
 prompt to use these long winter evening in put it all before 
 you from the beginning , that you may have it as one clear story in your 
 mind , and pass it on as such to those who come after you . for now that 
 the house of Brunswick be firmly establish upon the throne and that 
 peace prevail in the land , it will become less easy for you every 
 year to understand how man feel when Englishmen be in arm against 
 Englishmen , and when he who should have be the shield and the 
 protector of his subject have no thought but to force upon they what 
 they most abhor and detest . 

 my story be one which you may well treasure up in your memory , and 
 tell again to other , for it be not likely that in this whole county of 
 Hampshire , or even perhaps in all England , there be another left alive 
 who be so well able to speak from his own knowledge of these event , 
 or who have play a more forward part in they . all that I know I shall 
 endeavour soberly and in due order to put before you . I shall try to 
 make these dead man quicken into life for your behoof , and to call back 
 out of the mist of the past those scene which be brisk enough in 
 the acting , though they read so dully and so heavily in the page of the 
 worthy man who have set themselves to record they . perchance my word , 
 too , might , in the ear of stranger , seem to be but an old man 's 
 gossip . to you , however , who know that these eye which be look at 
 you look also at the thing which I describe , and that this hand have 
 strike in for a good cause , it will , I know , be different . Bear in mind 
 as you listen that it be your quarrel as well as our own in which 
 we fight , and that if now you grow up to be free man in a free land , 
 privileged to think or to pray as your conscience shall direct , you may 
 thank God that you be reap the harvest which your father sow in 
 blood and suffering when the Stuarts be on the throne . 

 I be bear then in the year 1664 , at Havant , which be a flourish 
 village a few mile from Portsmouth off the main London road , and there 
 it be that I spend the great part of my youth . it be now as it be 
 then , a pleasant , healthy spot , with a hundred or more brick cottage 
 scatter along in a single irregular street , each with its little 
 garden in front , and maybe a fruit tree or two at the back . in the 
 middle of the village stand the old church with the square tower , and 
 the great sun - dial like a wrinkle upon its grey weather - blotch face . 
 on the outskirt the Presbyterians have their chapel ; but when the Act of 
 Uniformity be pass , their good minister , Master Breckinridge , whose 
 discourse have often crowd his rude bench while the comfortable pew 
 of the church be empty , be cast into gaol , and his flock disperse . 
 as to the Independents , of whom my father be one , they also be under 
 the ban of the law , but they attend conventicle at Emsworth , whither 
 we would trudge , rain or shine , on every Sabbath morning . these meeting 
 be break up more than once , but the congregation be compose of such 
 harmless folk , so well beloved and respect by their neighbour , that 
 the peace officer come after a time to ignore they , and to let they 
 worship in their own fashion . there be Papists , too , amongst we , who 
 be compel to go as far as Portsmouth for their Mass. thus , you see , 
 small as be our village , we be a fair miniature of the whole country , 
 for we have our sect and our faction , which be all the more bitter 
 for be confine in so narrow a compass . 

 my father , Joseph Clarke , be well know over the countryside by the 
 name of Ironside Joe , for he have serve in his youth in the Yaxley 
 troop of Oliver Cromwell 's famous regiment of horse , and have preach 
 so lustily and fight so stoutly that old Noll himself call he out 
 of the rank after the fight at Dunbar , and raise he to a cornetcy . 
 it chance , however , that have some little time later fall into an 
 argument with one of his trooper concern the mystery of the Trinity , 
 the man , who be a half - crazy zealot , smote my father across the face , a 
 favour which he return by a thrust from his broadsword , which send his 
 adversary to test in person the truth of his belief . in most army 
 it would have be concede that my father be within his right 
 in punish promptly so rank an act of mutiny , but the soldier of 
 Cromwell have so high a notion of their own importance and privilege , 
 that they resent this summary justice upon their companion . a 
 court - martial sit upon my father , and it be likely that he would have 
 be offer up as a sacrifice to appease the angry soldiery , have not 
 the Lord Protector interfere , and limit the punishment to dismissal 
 from the army . Cornet Clarke be accordingly strip of his buff 
 coat and steel cap , and wander down to Havant , where he settle into 
 business as a leather merchant and tanner , thereby deprive Parliament 
 of as trusty a soldier as ever draw blade in its service . find 
 that he prosper in trade , he take as wife Mary Shepstone , a young 
 Churchwoman , and I , Micah Clarke , be the first pledge of their union . 

 my father , as I remember he first , be tall and straight , with a great 
 spread of shoulder and a mighty chest . his face be craggy and stern , 
 with large harsh feature , shaggy over - hang brow , high - bridge 
 fleshy nose , and a full - lipped mouth which tighten and set when he 
 be angry . his grey eye be pierce and soldier - like , yet I have see 
 they lighten up into a kindly and merry twinkle . his voice be the most 
 tremendous and awe - inspiring that I have ever listen to . I can well 
 believe what I have hear , that when he chant the Hundredth Psalm as 
 he ride down among the blue bonnet at Dunbar , the sound of he rise 
 above the blare of trumpet and the crash of gun , like the deep roll of 
 a break wave . yet though he possess every quality which be 
 need to raise he to distinction as an officer , he have throw off his 
 military habit when he return to civil life . as he prosper and grow 
 rich he might well have wear a sword , but instead he would ever bear a 
 small copy of the scripture bind to his girdle , where other man hang 
 their weapon . he be sober and measure in his speech , and it be 
 seldom , even in the bosom of his own family , that he would speak of the 
 scene which he have take part in , or of the great man , Fleetwood and 
 Harrison , Blake and Ireton , Desborough and Lambert , some of whom have 
 be simple trooper like himself when the trouble break out . he be 
 frugal in his eating , backward in drinking , and allow himself no 
 pleasure save three pipe a day of Oronooko tobacco , which he keep ever 
 in a brown jar by the great wooden chair on the left - hand side of the 
 mantelshelf . 

 yet for all his self - restraint the old leaven would at time begin to 
 work in he , and bring on fit of what his enemy would call fanaticism 
 and his friend piety , though it must be confess that this piety 
 be prone to take a fierce and fiery shape . as I look back , one or two 
 instance of that stand out so hard and clear in my recollection that 
 they might be scene which I have see of late in the playhouse , instead 
 of memory of my childhood more than threescore year ago , when the 
 second Charles be on the throne . 

 the first of these occur when I be so young that I can remember 
 neither what go before nor what immediately after it . it stick in my 
 infant mind when other thing slip through it . we be all in the 
 house one sultry summer evening , when there come a rattle of kettledrum 
 and a clatter of hoof , which bring my mother and my father to the 
 door , she with I in her arm that I might have the well view . it be 
 a regiment of horse on their way from Chichester to Portsmouth , with 
 colour fly and band playing , make the brave show that ever my 
 youthful eye have rest upon . with what wonder and admiration do I 
 gaze at the sleek prancing steed , the steel morion , the plume hat 
 of the officer , the scarf and bandolier . never , I think , have such 
 a gallant company assemble , and I clap my hand and cry out in my 
 delight . my father smile gravely , and take I from my mother 's arm . 
 ' nay , lad , ' he say , ' thou art a soldier 's son , and should have more 
 judgment than to commend such a rabble as this . Canst thou not , child as 
 thou art , see that their arm be ill - find , their stirrup - iron rust , 
 and their rank without order or cohesion ? neither have they throw out 
 a troop in advance , as should even in time of peace be do , and their 
 rear be straggle from here to Bedhampton . yea , ' he continue , suddenly 
 shake his long arm at the trooper , and call out to they , ' ye be 
 corn ripe for the sickle and wait only for the reaper ! ' several of 
 they rein up at this sudden out - flame . ' hit the crop - eare rascal over 
 the pate , Jack ! ' cry one to another , wheel his horse round ; but 
 there be that in my father 's face which cause he to fall back into 
 the rank again with his purpose unfulfilled . the regiment jingle on 
 down the road , and my mother lay her thin hand upon my father 's arm , 
 and lull with her pretty coax way the sleep devil which have 
 stir within he . 

 on another occasion which I can remember , about my seventh or eighth 
 year , his wrath burst out with more dangerous effect . I be play 
 about he as he work in the tanning - yard one spring afternoon , when 
 in through the open doorway strut two stately gentleman , with 
 gold facing to their coat and smart cockade at the side of their 
 three - cornered hat . they be , as I afterwards understand , officer of 
 the fleet who be pass through Havant , and see we at work in the 
 yard , design to ask we some question as to their route . the young of 
 the pair accost my father and begin his speech by a great clatter of 
 word which be all high Dutch to I , though I now see that they be a 
 string of such oath as be common in the mouth of a sailor ; though why 
 the very man who be in most danger of appear before the Almighty 
 should go out of their way to insult he , hath ever be a mystery to 
 I . my father in a rough stern voice bade he speak with more reverence 
 of sacred thing , on which the pair of they give tongue together , 
 swear tenfold bad than before , and call my father a canting 
 rogue and a smug - faced Presbytery Jack . what more they might have say I 
 know not , for my father pick up the great roller wherewith he smooth 
 the leather , and dash at they he bring it down on the side of one 
 of their head with such a swashing blow , that have it not be for his 
 stiff hat the man would never have utter oath again . as it be , he 
 drop like a log upon the stone of the yard , while his companion 
 whip out his rapi and make a vicious thrust ; but my father , who be 
 as active as he be strong , spring aside , and bring his cudgel down 
 upon the outstretched arm of the officer , crack it like the stem of 
 a tobacco - pipe . this affair make no little stir , for it occur at 
 the time when those arch - liar , Oates , Bedloe , and Carstairs , be 
 disturb the public mind by their rumour of plot , and a rise of 
 some sort be expect throughout the country . within a few day all 
 Hampshire be ring with an account of the malcontent tanner of 
 Havant , who have break the head and the arm of two of his Majesty 's 
 servant . an inquiry show , however , that there be no treasonable 
 meaning in the matter , and the officer have confess that the first 
 word come from they , the Justices content themselves with impose a 
 fine upon my father , and bind he over to keep the peace for a period 
 of six month . 

 I tell you these incident that you may have an idea of the fierce and 
 earnest religion which fill not only your own ancestor , but most of 
 those man who be train in the parliamentary army . in many way 
 they be more like those fanatic Saracens , who believe in conversion by 
 the sword , than the follower of a christian creed . yet they have this 
 great merit , that their own life be for the most part clean and 
 commendable , for they rigidly adhere themselves to those law which 
 they would gladly have force at the sword 's point upon other . it be 
 true that among so many there be some whose piety be a shell for 
 their ambition , and other who practise in secret what they denounce 
 in public , but no cause however good be free from such hypocritical 
 parasite . that the great part of the saint , as they term 
 themselves , be man of sober and God - fear life , may be show by the 
 fact that , after the disbanding of the army of the Commonwealth , the old 
 soldier flock into trade throughout the country , and make their mark 
 wherever they go by their industry and worth . there be many a wealthy 
 business house now in England which can trace its rise to the thrift and 
 honesty of some simple pikeman of Ireton or Cromwell . 

 but that I may help you to understand the character of your 
 great - grandfather , I shall give an incident which show how fervent and 
 real be the emotion which prompt the violent mood which I have 
 describe . I be about twelve at the time , my brother Hosea and Ephraim 
 be respectively nine and seven , while little Ruth could scarce have 
 be more than four . it chance that a few day before a wandering 
 preacher of the independent have put up at our house , and his religious 
 ministration have leave my father moody and excitable . one night I have 
 go to bed as usual , and be sound asleep with my two brother beside 
 I , when we be rouse and order to come downstairs . huddle on our 
 clothe we follow he into the kitchen , where my mother be sit 
 pale and scar with Ruth upon her knee . 

 ' gather round I , my child , ' he say , in a deep reverent voice , ' that 
 we may all appear before the throne together . the kingdom of the Lord be 
 at hand - oh , be ye ready to receive he ! this very night , my loved one , 
 ye shall see he in his splendour , with the angel and the archangel in 
 their might and their glory . at the third hour shall he come - that very 
 third hour which be now draw upon we . ' 

 ' Dear Joe , ' say my mother , in soothe tone , ' thou art scar thyself 
 and the child to no avail . if the Son of Man be indeed come , what 
 matter it whether we be abe or afoot ? ' 

 ' peace , woman , ' he answer sternly ; ' have he not say that he will come 
 like a thief in the night , and that it be for we to await he ? Join 
 with I , then , in prayerful outpouring that we may be find as those in 
 bridal array . let we offer up thank that he have graciously vouchsafe 
 to warn we through the word of his servant . oh , great Lord , look down 
 upon this small flock and lead it to the sheep fold ! mix not the 
 little wheat with the great world of chaff . oh , merciful Father ! look 
 graciously upon my wife , and forgive she the sin of Erastianism , she 
 be but a woman and little fit to cast off the bond of antichrist 
 wherein she be bear . and these too , my little one , Micah and Hosea , 
 Ephraim and Ruth , all name after Thy faithful servant of old , oh let 
 they stand upon Thy right hand this night ! ' thus he pray on in a wild 
 rush of burning , plead word , writhe prostrate upon the floor 
 in the vehemence of his supplication , while we , poor trembling mite , 
 huddle round our mother 's skirt and gaze with terror at the contorted 
 figure see by the dim light of the simple oil lamp . on a sudden the 
 clang of the new church clock tell that the hour have come . my father 
 spring from the floor , and rush to the casement , star up with wild 
 expectant eye at the starry heaven . whether he conjure up some vision 
 in his excited brain , or whether the rush of feeling on find that his 
 expectation be in vain , be too much for he , it be certain that 
 he throw his long arm upwards , utter a hoarse scream , and tumble 
 backwards with foaming lip and twitch limb upon the ground . for an 
 hour or more my poor mother and I do what we could to soothe he , while 
 the child whimper in a corner , until at last he stagger slowly to 
 his foot , and in brief break word order we to our room . from that 
 time I have never hear he allude to the matter , nor do he ever give 
 we any reason why he should so confidently have expect the second 
 come upon that particular night . I have learn since , however , 
 that the preacher who visit we be what be call in those day a 
 fifth - monarchy man , and that this particular sect be very liable to 
 these premonition . I have no doubt that something which he have say have 
 put the thought into my father 's head , and that the fiery nature of the 
 man have do the rest . 

 so much for your great - grandfather , Ironside Joe . I have prefer to 
 put these passage before you , for on the principle that action speak 
 louder than word , I find that in describe a man 's character it be 
 well to give example of his way than to speak in broad and general 
 term . have I say that he be fierce in in religion and subject to 
 strange fit of piety , the word might have make little impression 
 upon you ; but when I tell you of his attack upon the officer in the 
 tanning - yard , and his summon we down in the dead of the night to 
 await the second coming , you can judge for yourself the length to 
 which his belief would carry he . for the rest , he be an excellent man 
 of business , fair and even generous in his dealing , respect by all 
 and love by few , for his nature be too self - contain to admit of much 
 affection . to we he be a stern and rigid father , punish we heavily 
 for whatever he regard as amiss in our conduct . he bad a store of such 
 proverb as ' give a child its will and a whelp its fill , and neither 
 will strive , ' or ' child be certain care and uncertain comfort , ' 
 wherewith he would temper my mother 's more kindly impulse . he could not 
 bear that we should play trick - track upon the green , or dance with the 
 other child upon the Saturday night . 

 as to my mother , dear soul , it be her calm , peaceful influence which 
 keep my father within bound , and soften his austere rule . seldom 
 indeed , even in his dark mood , do the touch of her gentle hand and 
 the sound of her voice fail to soothe his fiery spirit . she come of a 
 Church stock , and hold to her religion with a quiet grip which be proof 
 against every attempt to turn she from it . I imagine that at one time 
 her husband have argue much with she upon Arminianism and the sin of 
 simony , but find his exhortation useless , he have abandon the 
 subject save on very rare occasion . in spite of her episcopacy , 
 however , she remain a staunch Whig , and never allow her loyalty to 
 the throne to cloud her judgment as to the doing of the monarch who sit 
 upon it . 

 woman be good housekeeper fitly year ago , but she be conspicuous 
 among the good . to see her spotless cuff and snowy kirtle one would 
 scarce credit how hard she labour . it be only the well order house 
 and the dustless room which proclaim her constant industry . she 
 make salve and eyewater , powder and confect , cordial and persico , 
 orangeflower water and cherry brandy , each in its due season , and all of 
 the good . she be wise , too , in herb and simple . the villager and the 
 farm labourer would rather any day have her advice upon their ailment 
 than that of Dr. Jackson of Purbrook , who never mix a draught under 
 a silver crown . over the whole countryside there be no woman more 
 deservedly respected and more esteemed both by those above she and by 
 those beneath . 

 such be my parent as I remember they in my childhood . as to myself , I 
 shall let my story explain the growth of my own nature . my brother and 
 my sister be all brownface , sturdy little country child , with no 
 very marked trait save a love of mischief control by the fear of 
 their father . these , with Martha the serve - maid , form our whole 
 household during those boyish year when the pliant soul of the child 
 be harden into the settle character of the man . how these influence 
 affect I I shall leave for a future sitting , and if I weary you by 
 record they , you must remember that I be tell these thing rather 
 for your profit than for your amusement ; that it may assist you in your 
 journey through life to know how another have pick out the path before 
 you . 



 Chapter II . of my go to school and of my come thence . 


 with the home influence which I have describe , it may be readily 
 imagine that my young mind turn very much upon the subject of 
 religion , the more so as my father and mother take different view upon 
 it . the old Puritan soldier hold that the bible alone contain all 
 thing essential to salvation , and that though it might be advisable 
 that those who be gift with wisdom or eloquence should expound the 
 scripture to their brother , it be by no mean necessary , but rather 
 hurtful and degrading , that any organise body of minister or of 
 bishop should claim special prerogative , or take the place of 
 mediator between the creature and the creator . for the wealthy 
 dignitary of the Church , roll in their carriage to their 
 cathedral , in order to preach the doctrine of their Master , who wear 
 his sandal out in tramp over the countryside , he profess the most 
 bitter contempt ; nor be he more lenient to those poor member of the 
 clergy who wink at the vice of their patron that they might secure 
 a seat at their table , and who would sit through a long evening of 
 profanity rather than bid good - bye to the cheesecake and the wine 
 flask . that such man represent religious truth be abhorrent to 
 his mind , nor would he even give his adhesion to that form of church 
 government dear to the Presbyterians , where a general council of the 
 minister direct the affair of their church . every man be , in his 
 opinion , equal in the eye of the Almighty , and none have a right to 
 claim any precedence over his neighbour in matter of religion . the book 
 be write for all , and all be equally able to read it , provide that 
 their mind be enlighten by the Holy Spirit . 

 my mother , on the other hand , hold that the very essence of a church 
 be that it should have a hierarchy and a graduated government within 
 itself , with the king at the apex , the archbishop beneath he , the 
 bishop under their control , and so down through the ministry to the 
 common folk . such be , in her opinion , the Church as establish in the 
 beginning , and no religion without these characteristic could lay any 
 claim to be the true one . ritual be to she of as great importance 
 as morality , and if every tradesman and farmer be allow to invent 
 prayer , and change the service as the fancy seize he , it would be 
 impossible to preserve the purity of the christian creed . she agree 
 that religion be base upon the Bible , but the Bible be a book which 
 contain much that be obscure , and unless that obscurity be 
 clear away by a duly elect and consecrate servant of God , a 
 lineal descendant of the Disciples , all human wisdom might not serve to 
 interpret it aright . that be my mother 's position , and neither argument 
 nor entreaty could move she from it . the only question of belief on 
 which my two parent be equally ardent be their mutual dislike 
 and distrust of the roman catholic form of worship , and in this the 
 Churchwoman be every whit as decide as the fanatical Independent . 

 it may seem strange to you in these day of tolerance , that the 
 adherent of this venerable creed should have meet with such universal 
 ill - will from successive generation of Englishmen . we recognise now 
 that there be no more useful or loyal citizen in the state than our 
 catholic brother , and Mr. Alexander Pope or any other lead Papist be 
 no more look down upon for his religion than be Mr. William Penn 
 for his Quakerism in the reign of King James . we can scarce credit how 
 nobleman like Lord Stafford , ecclesiastic like Archbishop Plunkett , 
 and commoner like Langhorne and Pickering , be drag to death on 
 the testimony of the vilest of the vile , without a voice be raise in 
 their behalf ; or how it could be consider a patriotic act on the part 
 of an English Protestant to carry a flail load with lead beneath his 
 cloak as a menace against his harmless neighbour who differ from 
 he on point of doctrine . it be a long madness which have now happily 
 pass off , or at least show itself in a milder and rarer form . 

 foolish as it appear to we , there be some solid reason to account 
 for it . you have read doubtless how , a century before I be bear , the 
 great kingdom of Spain wax and prosper . her ship cover every 
 sea . her troop be victorious wherever they appear . in letter , in 
 learning , in all the art of war and peace they be the foremost nation 
 in Europe . you have hear also of the ill - blood which exist between 
 this great nation and ourselves ; how our adventurer harry their 
 possession across the Atlantic , while they retort by burn such 
 of our seaman as they could catch by their devilish inquisition , and by 
 threaten our coast both from Cadiz and from their province in the 
 Netherlands . at last so hot become the quarrel that the other nation 
 stand off , as I have see the folk clear a space for the sword - player 
 at Hockley - in - the - Hole , so that the spanish giant and tough little 
 England be leave face to face to fight the matter out . throughout all 
 that business it be as the emissary of the Pope , and as the avenger of 
 the dishonoured Roman Church , that King Philip profess to come . it 
 be true that Lord Howard and many another gentleman of the old religion 
 fight stoutly against the don , but the people could never forget that 
 the reform faith have be the flag under which they have conquer , and 
 that the blessing of the Pontiff have rest with their opponent . then 
 come the cruel and foolish attempt of Mary to force upon they a creed 
 for which they have no sympathy , and at the heel of it another great 
 roman catholic power menace our liberty from the continent . the grow 
 strength of France promote a corresponding distrust of Papistry in 
 England , which reach a head when , at about the time of which I write , 
 Louis XIV . threaten we with invasion at the very moment when , by 
 the revocation of the Edict of Nantes , he show his intolerant spirit 
 towards the faith which we hold dear . the narrow Protestantism of 
 England be less a religious sentiment than a patriotic reply to 
 the aggressive bigotry of her enemy . our catholic countryman be 
 unpopular , not so much because they believe in Transubstantiation , as 
 because they be unjustly suspect of sympathise with the Emperor or 
 with the King of France . now that our military success have secure we 
 against all fear of attack , we have happily lose that bitter religious 
 hatred but for which Oates and Dangerfield would have lie in vain . 

 in the day when I be young , special cause have inflame this dislike 
 and make it all the more bitter because there be a spice of fear 
 mingle with it . as long as the Catholics be only an obscure faction 
 they might be ignore , but when , towards the close of the reign of the 
 second Charles , it appear to be absolutely certain that a Catholic 
 dynasty be about to fill the throne , and that Catholicism be to be the 
 court religion and the stepping - stone to preferment , it be feel that 
 a day of vengeance might be at hand for those who have trample upon 
 it when it be defenceless . there be alarm and uneasiness amongst all 
 class . the Church of England , which depend upon the monarch as an 
 arch depend upon the keystone ; the nobility , whose estate and coffer 
 have be enrich by the plunder of the abbey ; the mob , whose idea of 
 Papistry be mix up with thumbscrew and Fox 's martyrology , be all 
 equally disturbed . nor be the prospect a hopeful one for their cause . 
 Charles be a very lukewarm protestant , and indeed show upon his 
 deathbed that he be no protestant at all . there be no long any 
 chance of his have legitimate offspring . the Duke of York , his young 
 brother , be therefore heir to the throne , and he be know to be an 
 austere and narrow Papist , while his spouse , Mary of Modena , be 
 as bigote as himself . should they have child , there could be 
 no question but that they would be bring up in the faith of their 
 parent , and that a line of catholic monarch would occupy the throne 
 of England . to the Church , as represent by my mother , and to 
 Nonconformity , in the person of my father , this be an equally 
 intolerable prospect . 

 I have be tell you all this old history because you will find , as 
 I go on , that this state of thing cause in the end such a seething and 
 ferment throughout the nation that even I , a simple village lad , be 
 drag into the whirl and have my whole life influence by it . if I do 
 not make the course of event clear to you , you would hardly understand 
 the influence which have such an effect upon my whole history . in the 
 meantime , I wish you to remember that when King James II . ascend the 
 throne he do so amid a sullen silence on the part of a large class of 
 his subject , and that both my father and my mother be among those who 
 be zealous for a protestant succession . 

 my childhood be , as I have already say , a gloomy one . now and again 
 when there chance to be a fair at Portsdown Hill , or when a pass 
 raree showman set up his booth in the village , my dear mother would 
 slip a penny or two from her housekeeping money into my hand , and with 
 a warning finger upon her lip would send I off to see the sight . these 
 treat be , however , rare event , and make such a mark upon my mind , 
 that when I be sixteen year of age I could have check off upon my 
 finger all that I have ever see . there be William Harker the strong 
 man , who lift Farmer Alcott 's roan mare ; and there be Tubby Lawson 
 the dwarf , who could fit himself into a pickle jar -- these two I well 
 remember from the wonder wherewith they strike my youthful soul . then 
 there be the show of the play doll , and that of the enchanted 
 island and Mynheer Munster from the Lowlands , who could turn himself 
 round upon a tight - rope while play most sweetly upon a virginal . 
 last , but far the good in my estimation , be the grand play at the 
 Portsdown Fair , entitle ' the true and ancient story of Maudlin , the 
 merchant 's daughter of Bristol , and of her lover Antonio . how they be 
 cast away on the shore of Barbary , where the mermaid be see float 
 upon the sea and singing in the rock , foretell their danger . ' this 
 little piece give I keener pleasure than ever in after year I receive 
 from the grand comedy of Mr. Congreve and of Mr. Dryden , though 
 act by Kynaston , Betterton , and the whole strength of the King 's own 
 company . at Chichester once I remember that I pay a penny to see the 
 left shoe of the young sister of Potiphar 's wife , but as it look 
 much like any other old shoe , and be just about the size to have fit 
 the show - woman , I have often fear that my penny fall into the hand of 
 rogue . 

 there be other show , however , which I might see for nothing , and yet 
 be more real and every whit as interesting as any for which I 
 pay . now and again upon a holiday I be permit to walk down to 
 Portsmouth -- once I be even take in front of my father upon his pad 
 nag , and there I wander with he through the street with wonder 
 eye , marvel over the strange sight around I . the wall and the 
 moat , the gate and the sentinel , the long High Street with the great 
 government building , and the constant rattle of drum and blare of 
 trumpet ; they make my little heart beat quick beneath my sagathy 
 stuff jacket . here be the house in which some thirty year before the 
 proud Duke of Buckingham have be strike down by the assassin 's dagger . 
 there , too , be the Governor 's dwelling , and I remember that even as I 
 look he come rid up to it , red - faced and choleric , with a nose such 
 as a Governor should have , and his breast all slash with gold . ' be he 
 not a fine man ? ' I say , look up at my father . he laugh and draw 
 his hat down over his brow . ' it be the first time that I have see Sir 
 Ralph Lingard 's face , ' say he , ' but I see his back at Preston fight . 
 ah , lad , proud as he look , if he do but see old Noll come in through 
 the door he would not think it beneath he to climb out through the 
 window ! ' the clank of steel or the sight of a buff - coat would always 
 serve to stir up the old Roundhead bitterness in my father 's breast . 

 but there be other sight in Portsmouth besides the red - coat and 
 their governor . the yard be the second in the kingdom , after Chatham , 
 and there be ever some new war - ship ready upon the slip . then there 
 be a squadron of King 's ship , and sometimes the whole fleet at 
 Spithead , when the street would be full of sailor , with their face as 
 brown as mahogany and pigtail as stiff and hard as their cutlass . to 
 watch their rolling gait , and to hear their strange , quaint talk , 
 and their tale of the dutch war , be a rare treat to I ; and I have 
 sometimes when I be alone fasten myself on to a group of they , and 
 pass the day in wander from tavern to tavern . it chance one day , 
 however , that one of they insist upon my share his glass of Canary 
 wine , and afterwards out of roguishness persuade I to take a second , 
 with the result that I be send home speechless in the carrier 's cart , 
 and be never again allow to go into Portsmouth alone . my father be 
 less shocked at the incident than I should have expect , and remind 
 my mother that Noah have be overtake in a similar manner . he also 
 narrate how a certain field - chaplain Grant , of Desborough 's regiment , 
 have after a hot and dusty day drunk sundry flagon of mum , have 
 thereafter sing certain ungodly song , and dance in a manner unbecoming 
 to his sacred profession . also , how he have afterwards explain that 
 such backsliding be not to be regard we fault of the individual , 
 but rather as actual obsession of the evil one , who contrive in this 
 manner to give scandal to the faithful , and select the most godly for 
 his evil purpose . this ingenious defence of the field - chaplain be the 
 saving of my back , for my father , who be a believer in Solomon 's axiom , 
 have a stout ash stick and a strong arm for whatever seem to he to be 
 a fall away from the true path . 

 from the day that I first learn my letter from the horn - book at my 
 mother 's knee I be always hungry to increase my knowledge , and never a 
 piece of print come in my way that I do not eagerly master . my father 
 push the sectarian hatred of learn to such a length that he be 
 averse to have any worldly book within his door . ( note A , Appendix ) 
 I be dependent therefore for my supply upon one or two of my friend in 
 the village , who lend I a volume at a time from their small library . 
 these I would carry inside my shirt , and would only dare to produce when 
 I could slip away into the field , and lie hide among the long grass , or 
 at night when the rushlight be still burn , and my father 's snoring 
 assure I that there be no danger of his detect I . in this way 
 I work up from Don Bellianis of Greece and the ' Seven Champions , ' 
 through Tarleton 's ' jest ' and other such book , until I could take 
 pleasure in the poetry of Waller and of Herrick , or in the play of 
 Massinger and Shakespeare . how sweet be the hour when I could lay 
 aside all thought of freewill and of predestination , to lie with my 
 heel in the air among the scented clover , and listen to old Chaucer 
 tell the sweet story of Grisel the patient , or to weep for the chaste 
 Desdemona , and mourn over the untimely end of her gallant spouse . there 
 be time as I rise up with my mind full of the noble poetry , and 
 glance over the fair slope of the countryside , with the gleam sea 
 beyond it , and the purple outline of the Isle of Wight upon the horizon ; 
 when it would be bear in upon I that the being who create all this , 
 and who give man the power of pour out these beautiful thought , be 
 not the possession of one sect or another , or of this nation or that , 
 but be the kindly Father of every one of the little child whom 
 he have let loose on this fair playground . it grieve I then , and it 
 grieve I now , that a man of such sincerity and lofty purpose as your 
 great grandfather should have be so tie down by iron doctrine , and 
 should imagine his creator to be so niggard of his mercy as to withhold 
 it from nine - and - ninety in the hundred . well , a man be as he be train , 
 and if my father bear a narrow mind upon his broad shoulder , he have at 
 least the credit that he be ready to do and to suffer all thing 
 for what he conceive to be the truth . if you , my dear , have more 
 enlighten view , take heed that they bring you to lead a more 
 enlightened life . 

 when I be fourteen year of age , a yellow - haired , brown - face lad , I 
 be pack off to a small private school at Petersfield , and there I 
 remain for a year , return home for the last Saturday in each month . 
 I take with I only a scanty outfit of schoolbook , with Lilly 's ' Latin 
 Grammar , ' and Rosse 's ' view of all the religion in the World from the 
 Creation down to our own time , ' which be shove into my hand by my 
 good mother as a parting present . with this small stock of letter I 
 might have fare badly , have it not happen that my master , Mr. Thomas 
 Chillingfoot , have himself a good library , and take a pleasure in 
 lend his book to any of his scholar who show a desire to improve 
 themselves . under this good old man 's care I not only pick up some 
 smattering of Latin and Greek , but I find mean to read good english 
 translation of many of the classic , and to acquire a knowledge of the 
 history of my own and other country . I be rapidly grow in mind as 
 well as in body , when my school career be cut short by no less an event 
 than my summary and ignominious expulsion . how this unlooke - for end 
 to my study come about I must now set before you . 

 Petersfield have always be a great stronghold of the Church , have 
 hardly a Nonconformist within its bound . the reason of this be that 
 most of the house property be own by zealous Churchmen , who refuse 
 to allow any one who differ from the Established Church to settle 
 there . the Vicar , whose name be Pinfold , possess in this manner great 
 power in the town , and as he be a man with a high inflame countenance 
 and a pompous manner , he inspire no little awe among the quiet 
 inhabitant . I can see he now with his beaked nose , his rounded 
 waistcoat , and his bandy leg , which look as if they have give way 
 beneath the load of learning which they be compel to carry . walk 
 slowly with right hand stiffly extend , tap the pavement at every 
 step with his metal - head stick , he would pause as each person pass 
 he , and wait to see that he be give the salute which he think due 
 to his dignity . this courtesy he never dream of return , save in 
 the case of some of his rich parishioner ; but if by chance it be 
 omit , he would hurry after the culprit , and , shake his stick in his 
 face , insist upon his doff his cap to he . we youngster , if we meet 
 he on our walk , would scuttle by he like a brood of chicken pass 
 an old turkey cock , and even our worthy master show a disposition to 
 turn down a side - street when the portly figure of the Vicar be see 
 roll in our direction . this proud priest make a point of know the 
 history of every one within his parish , and have learn that I be the 
 son of an Independent , he speak severely to Mr. Chillingfoot upon the 
 indiscretion which he have show in admit I to his school . indeed , 
 nothing but my mother 's good name for orthodoxy prevent he from 
 insist upon my dismissal . 

 at the other end of the village there be a large day - school . a constant 
 feud prevail between the scholar who attend it and the lad who 
 study under our master . no one could tell how the war break out , but 
 for many year there have be a standing quarrel between the two , which 
 result in skirmish , sally , and ambuscade , with now and then a 
 pitch battle . no great harm be do in these encounter , for the 
 weapon be usually snowball in winter and pine - cone or clod of 
 earth in the summer . even when the contest get close and we come to 
 fisticuff , a few bruise and a little blood be the bad that could 
 come of it . our opponent be more numerous than we , but we have the 
 advantage of be always together and of have a secure asylum upon 
 which to retreat , while they , live in scatter house all over the 
 parish , have no common rallying - point . a stream , cross by two bridge , 
 run through the centre of the town , and this be the boundary which 
 separate our territory from those of our enemy . the boy who cross 
 the bridge find himself in hostile country . 

 it chance that in the first conflict which occur after my arrival at 
 the school I distinguish myself by single out the most redoubtable 
 of our foeman , and smite he such a blow that he be knock helpless 
 and be carry off by our party as a prisoner . this feat of arm 
 establish my good name as a warrior , so I come at last to be regard 
 as the leader of our force , and to be look up to by big boy than 
 myself . this promotion tickle my fancy so much , that I set to work to 
 prove that I deserve it by devise fresh and ingenious scheme for the 
 defeat of our enemy . 

 one winter 's evening news reach we that our rival be about to make 
 a raid upon we under cover of night , and that they propose come by 
 the little use plank bridge , so as to escape our notice . this bridge 
 lie almost out of the town , and consist of a single broad piece of 
 wood without a rail , erect for the good of the town clerk , who live , 
 just opposite to it . we propose to hide ourselves amongst the bush on 
 our side of the stream , and make an unexpected attack upon the invader 
 as they cross . as we start , however , I bethink I of an ingenious 
 stratagem which I have read of as be practise in the german war , and 
 have expound it to the great delight of my companion , we take Mr. 
 Chillingfoot 's saw , and set off for the seat of action . 

 on reach the bridge all be quiet and still . it be quite dark and 
 very cold , for Christmas be approach . there be no sign of our 
 opponent . we exchange a few whisper as to who should do the daring 
 deed , but as the other shrink from it , and as I be too proud to 
 propose what I dare not execute , I grip the saw , and sit 
 astraddle upon the plank set to work upon the very centre of it . 

 my purpose be to weaken it in such a way that , though it would bear the 
 weight of one , it would collapse when the main body of our foeman be 
 upon it , and so precipitate they into the ice - cold stream . the water be 
 but a couple of foot deep at the place , so that there be nothing for 
 they but a fright and a ducking . so cool a reception ought to deter 
 they from ever invade we again , and confirm my reputation as a daring 
 leader . Reuben Lockarby , my lieutenant , son of old John Lockarby of the 
 Wheatsheaf , marshal our force behind the hedgerow , whilst I saw 
 vigorously at the plank until I have nearly sever it across . I have no 
 compunction about the destruction of the bridge , for I know enough of 
 carpentry to see that a skilful joiner could in an hour 's work make 
 it strong than ever by put a prop beneath the point where I have 
 divide it . when at last I feel by the yielding of the plank that I have 
 do enough , and that the least strain would snap it , I crawl quietly 
 off , and take up my position with my schoolfellow , await the coming 
 of the enemy . 

 I have scarce conceal myself when we hear the step of some one 
 approach down the footpath which lead to the bridge . we crouch 
 behind the cover , convince that the sound must come from some scout 
 whom our foeman have send on in front -- a big boy evidently , for his step 
 be heavy and slow , with a clink noise mingle with it , of which we 
 could make nothing . Nearer come the sound and nearer , until a shadowy 
 figure loom out of the darkness upon the other side , and after pausing 
 and peer for a moment , come straight for the bridge . it be only as 
 he be set foot upon the plank and begin gingerly to pick his 
 way across it , that we discern the outline of the familiar form , and 
 realise the dreadful truth that the stranger whom we have take for the 
 advance guard of our enemy be in truth none other than Vicar Pinfold , 
 and that it be the rhythmic pat of his stick which we hear mingle 
 with his footfall . fascinated by the sight , we lay bereft of all power 
 to warn he -- a line of star eyeball . one step , two step , three 
 step do the haughty Churchman take , when there be a rend crack , 
 and he vanish with a mighty splash into the swift - flow stream . he 
 must have fall upon his back , for we could see the curved outline 
 of his portly figure stand out above the surface as he struggle 
 desperately to regain his foot . at last he manage to get erect , and 
 come splutter for the bank with such a mixture of godly ejaculation 
 and of profane oath that , even in our terror , we could not keep from 
 laughter . rise from under his foot like a covey of wild - fowl , we 
 scurry off across the field and so back to the school , where , as you 
 may imagine , we say nothing to our good master of what have occur . 

 the matter be too serious , however , to be hush up . the sudden chill 
 set up some manner of disturbance in the bottle of sack which the Vicar 
 have just be drink with the town clerk , and an attack of gout set in 
 which lay he on his back for a fortnight . meanwhile an examination of 
 the bridge have show that it have be saw across , and an inquiry trace 
 the matter to Mr. Chillingfoot 's boarder . to save a wholesale expulsion 
 of the school from the town , I be force to acknowledge myself as both 
 the inventor and perpetrator of the deed . Chillingfoot be entirely in 
 the power of the Vicar , so he be force to read I a long homily 
 in public -- which he balance by an affectionate leave - taking in 
 private -- and to expel I solemnly from the school . I never see my old 
 master again , for he die not many year afterwards ; but I hear that his 
 second son William be still carry on the business , which be large 
 and more prosperous than of old . his eld son turn Quaker and go 
 out to Penn 's settlement , where he be report to have be slay by the 
 savage . 

 this adventure shock my dear mother , but it find great favour in the 
 eye of my father , who laugh until the whole village resound 
 with his stentorian merriment . it remind he , he say , of a similar 
 stratagem execute at Market Drayton by that God - fear soldier Colonel 
 Pride , whereby a captain and three trooper of Lunsford 's own regiment 
 of horse have be drown , and many other precipitate into a river , to 
 the great glory of the true Church and to the satisfaction of the 
 choose people . even of the Church folk many be secretly glad at the 
 misfortune which have overtake the Vicar , for his pretension and his 
 pride have make he hat throughout the district . 

 by this time I have grow into a sturdy , broad - shoulder lad , and every 
 month add to my strength and my stature . when I be sixteen I could 
 carry a bag of wheat or a cask of beer against any man in the village , 
 and I could throw the fifteen - pound putting - stone to a distance of 
 thirty - six foot , which be four foot far than could Ted Dawson , the 
 blacksmith . once when my father be unable to carry a bale of skin out 
 of the yard , I whip it up and bare it away upon my shoulder . the 
 old man would often look gravely at I from under his heavy thatched 
 eyebrow , and shake his grizzle head as he sit in his arm - chair puffing 
 his pipe . ' you grow too big for the nest , lad , ' he would say . ' I doubt 
 some of these day you will find your wing and away ! ' in my heart I 
 long that the time would come , for I be weary of the quiet life of 
 the village , and be anxious to see the great world of which I have hear 
 and read so much . I could not look southward without my spirit stir 
 within I as my eye fall upon those dark wave , the white crest of 
 which be like a flutter signal ever wave to an english youth and 
 beckon he to some unknown but glorious goal . 



 Chapter III . of two friend of my Youth 


 I fear , my child , that you will think that the prologue be over long 
 for the play ; but the foundation must be lay before the building be 
 erect , and a statement of this sort be a sorry and a barren thing 
 unless you have a knowledge of the folk concern . be patient , then , 
 while I speak to you of the old friend of my youth , some of whom you 
 may hear more of hereafter , while other remain behind in the country 
 hamlet , and yet leave trace of our early intercourse upon my character 
 which might still be discern there . 

 foremost for good amongst all whom I know be Zachary Palmer , the 
 village carpenter , a man whose aged and labour - warp body contain the 
 simple and pure of spirit . yet his simplicity be by no mean the 
 result of ignorance , for from the teaching of Plato to those of Hobbes 
 there be few system ever think out by man which he have not study 
 and weigh . book be far dearer in my boyhood than they be now , 
 and carpenter be less well pay , but old Palmer have neither wife nor 
 child , and spend little on food or raiment . thus it come about that on 
 the shelf over his bed he have a more choice collection of book -- few as 
 they be in number -- than the squire or the parson , and these book he 
 have read until he not only understand they himself , but could impart 
 they to other . 

 this white - bearded and venerable village philosopher would sit by his 
 cabin door upon a summer evening , and be never so pleased as when 
 some of the young fellow would slip away from their bowl and their 
 quoit - playing in order to lie in the grass at his foot , and ask he 
 question about the great man of old , their word and their deed . but 
 of all the youth I and Reuben Lockarby , the innkeeper 's son , be his 
 two favourite , for we would come the early and stop the late to 
 hear the old man talk . no father could have love his child well 
 than he do we , and he would spare no pain to get at our callow 
 thought , and to throw light upon whatever perplex or trouble we . 
 like all grow thing , we have run our head against the problem of 
 the universe . we have peep and pry with our boyish eye into those 
 profound depth in which the keenest - sighted of the human race have see 
 no bottom . yet when we look around we in our own village world , and 
 see the bitterness and rancour which pervade every sect , we could not 
 but think that a tree which bear such fruit must have something amiss 
 with it . this be one of the thought unspeak to our parent which 
 we carry to good old Zachary , and on which he have much to say which 
 cheer and comfort we . 

 ' these jangling and wrangling , ' say he , ' be but on the surface , 
 and spring from the infinite variety of the human mind , which will ever 
 adapt a creed to suit its own turn of thought . it be the solid core that 
 underlie every christian creed which be of importance . could you 
 but live among the Romans or the Greeks , in the day before this new 
 doctrine be preach , you would then know the change that it have 
 work in the world . how this or that text should be construe be a 
 matter of no moment , however warm man may get over it . what be of the 
 very great moment be , that every man should have a good and solid 
 reason for live a simple , cleanly life . this the christian creed have 
 give we . ' 

 ' I would not have you be virtuous out of fear , ' he say upon another 
 occasion . ' the experience of a long life have teach I , however , that 
 sin be always punish in this world , whatever may come in the next . 
 there be always some penalty in health , in comfort , or in peace of 
 mind to be pay for every wrong . it be with nation as it be with 
 individual . a book of history be a book of sermon . see how the 
 luxurious Babylonians be destroy by the frugal Persians , and how 
 these same Persians when they learn the vice of prosperity be put 
 to the sword by the Greeks . read on and mark how the sensual Greeks be 
 trodden down by the more robust and hardy Romans , and finally how the 
 Romans , have lose their manly virtue , be subdue by the nation 
 of the north . Vice and destruction come ever hand in hand . thus do 
 Providence use each in turn as a scourge wherewith to chastise the 
 folly of the other . these thing do not come by chance . they be part 
 of a great system which be at work in your own life . the long you 
 live the more you will see that sin and sadness be never far apart , and 
 that no true prosperity can exist away from virtue . ' 

 a very different teacher be the sea - dog Solomon Sprent , who live in 
 the second last cottage on the left - hand side of the main street of the 
 village . he be one of the old tarpaulin breed , who have fight under 
 the red cross ensign against Frenchman , Don , Dutchman , and Moor , until a 
 round shot carry off his foot and put an end to his battle for ever . 
 in person he be thin , and hard , and brown , as lithe and active as a 
 cat , with a short body and very long arm , each end in a great hand 
 which be ever half close as though shut on a rope . from head to 
 foot he be cover with the most marvellous tattooing , do in blue , 
 red , and green , begin with the Creation upon his neck and wind up 
 with the Ascension upon his left ankle . never have I see such a walking 
 work of art . he be will not to say that have he be own and his body cast 
 up upon some savage land , the native might have learn the whole of 
 the blessed gospel from a contemplation of his carcass . yet with sorrow 
 I must say that the seaman 's religion appear to have all work into 
 his skin , so that very little be leave for inner use . it have break out 
 upon the surface , like the spot fever , but his system be clear of 
 it elsewhere . he could swear in eleven language and three - and - twenty 
 dialect , nor do he ever let his great power rust for want of 
 practice . he would swear when he be happy or when he be sad , when he 
 be angry or when he be love , but this swearing be so mere a trick 
 of speech , without malice or bitterness , that even my father could 
 hardly deal harshly with the sinner . as time pass , however , the old 
 man grow more sober and more thoughtful , until in his latter day he 
 go back to the simple belief of his childhood , and learn to fight 
 the devil with the same steady courage with which he have face the 
 enemy of his country . 

 Old Solomon be a never - fail source of amusement and of interest to 
 my friend Lockarby and myself . on gala day he would have we in to dine 
 with he , when he would regale we with lobscouse and salmagundi , or 
 perhaps with an outland dish , a pillaw or olla podrida , or fish broil 
 after the fashion of the Azores , for he have a famous trick of cooking , 
 and could produce the delicacy of all nation . and all the time that 
 we be with he he would tell we the most marvellous story of Rupert , 
 under whom he serve ; how he would shout from the poop to his squadron 
 to wheel to the right , or to charge , or to halt , as the case might be , 
 as if he be still with his regiment of horse . of Blake , too , he have 
 many story to tell . but even the name of Blake be not so dear to our 
 old sailor as be that of Sir Christopher Mings . Solomon have at one time 
 be his coxswain , and could talk by the hour of those gallant deed 
 which have distinguish he from the day that he enter the navy as a 
 cabin boy until he fall upon his own quarter - deck , a full admiral of the 
 red , and be bear by his weeping ship 's company to his grave in Chatham 
 churchyard . ' if so be as there be a jasper sea up aloft , ' say the old 
 seaman , ' I will wager that Sir Christopher will see that the english flag 
 have proper respect pay to it upon it , and that we be not fool by 
 foreigner . I have serve under he in this world , and I ask nothing 
 well than to be his coxswain in the next -- if so be as he should chance 
 to have a vacancy for such . ' these remembrance would always end in the 
 brewing of an extra bowl of punch , and the drinking of a solemn bumper 
 to the memory of the depart hero . 

 stir as be Solomon Sprent 's account of his old commander , their 
 effect upon we be not so great as when , about his second or third 
 glass , the floodgate of his memory would be open , and he would pour 
 out long tale of the land which he have visit , and the people which 
 he have see . lean forward in our seat with our chin rest upon 
 our hand , we two youngster would sit for hour , with our eye fix 
 upon the old adventurer , drink in his word , while he , please at the 
 interest which he excite , would puff slowly at his pipe and reel off 
 story after story of what he have see or do . in those day , my dear , 
 there be no Defoe to tell we the wonder of the world , no _ spectator _ 
 to lie upon our breakfast table , no Gulliver to satisfy our love of 
 adventure by tell we of such adventure as never be . not once in 
 a month do a common newsletter fall into our hand . personal hazard , 
 therefore , be of more value then than they be now , and the talk of a 
 man like old Solomon be a library in itself . to we it be all real . his 
 husky tone and ill - choose word be as the voice of an angel , and our 
 eager mind fill in the detail and supply all that be want in 
 his narrative . in one evening we have engage a Sallee rover off the 
 Pillars of Hercules ; we have coast down the shore of the african 
 continent , and see the great breaker of the spanish Main foaming upon 
 the yellow sand ; we have pass the black ivory merchant with their 
 human cargoe ; we have face the terrible storm which blow ever around 
 the Cape de Boa Esperanza ; and finally , we have sail away out over the 
 great ocean beyond , amid the palm - clothe coral island , with the knowledge 
 that the realm of Prester John lie somewhere behind the golden haze 
 which shimmer upon the horizon . after such a flight as that we would 
 feel , as we come back to the Hampshire village and the dull reality 
 of country life , like wild bird who have be snare by the fowler and 
 clap into narrow cage . then it be that the word of my father , ' you 
 will find your wing some day and fly away , ' would come back to I , and 
 set up such a restlessness as all the wise word of Zachary Palmer could 
 not allay . 


 Chapter IV . of the strange fish that we catch at Spithead 

 one evening in the month of May 1685 , about the end of the first week 
 of the month , my friend Reuben Lockarby and I borrow Ned Marley 's 
 pleasure boat , and go a - fishing out of Langston Bay . at that time I 
 be close on one - and - twenty year of age , while my companion be one 
 year young . a great intimacy have spring up between we , found on 
 mutual esteem , for he be a little undergrown man be proud of my 
 strength and stature , while my melancholy and somewhat heavy spirit take 
 a pleasure in the energy and joviality which never desert he , and 
 in the wit which gleam as bright and as innocent as summer lightning 
 through all that he say . in person he be short and broad , round - face , 
 ruddy - cheek , and in truth a little inclined to be fat , though he would 
 never confess to more than a pleasing plumpness , which be hold , he 
 say , to be the acme of manly beauty amongst the ancient . the stern 
 test of common danger and mutual hardship entitle I to say that no 
 man could have desire a stauncher or more trusty comrade . as he be 
 destine to be with I in the sequel , it be but fitting that he should 
 have be at my side on that May evening which be the starting - point of 
 our adventure . 

 we pull out beyond the Warner Sands to a place half - way between they 
 and the Nab , where we usually find bass in plenty . there we cast the 
 heavy stone which serve we as an anchor overboard , and proceed to 
 set our line . the sun sink slowly behind a fog - bank have slash the 
 whole western sky with scarlet streak , against which the woode slope 
 of the Isle of Wight stand out vaporous and purple . a fresh breeze be 
 blow from the south - east , fleck the long green wave with crest 
 of foam , and fill our eye and lip with the smack of the salt spray . 
 over near St. Helen 's Point a King 's ship be make her way down the 
 channel , while a single large brig be tack about a quarter of a mile 
 or less from where we lay . so near be we that we could catch a glimpse 
 of the figure upon her deck as she heel over to the breeze , and could 
 bear the creaking of her yard and the flapping of her weather - stain 
 canvas as she prepare to go about . 

 ' look ye , Micah , ' say my companion , look up from his fishing - line . 
 ' that be a most weak - minded ship -- a ship which will make no way in the 
 world . see how she hang in the wind , neither keep on her course nor 
 tacking . she be a trimmer of the sea -- the Lord Halifax of the ocean . ' 

 ' why , there be something amiss with she , ' I reply , star across with 
 hand - shade eye . ' she yaw about as though there be no one at the 
 helm . her main - yard go aback ! now it be forward again ! the folk on her 
 deck seem to I to be either fight or dancing . up with the anchor , 
 Reuben , and let we pull to she . ' 

 ' up with the anchor and let we get out of her way , ' he answer , still 
 gaze at the stranger . ' why will you ever run that meddlesome head of 
 your into danger 's way ? she fly dutch colour , but who can say whence 
 she really come ? a pretty thing if we be snap up by a buccaneer 
 and sell in the Plantations ! ' 

 ' a buccaneer in the Solent ! ' cry I derisively . ' we shall be see the 
 black flag in Emsworth Creek next . but hark ! what be that ? ' 

 the crack of a musket sound from aboard the brig . then come a moment 's 
 silence and another musket shot ring out , follow by a chorus of shout 
 and cry . simultaneously the yard swing round into position , the sail 
 catch the breeze once more , and the vessel dart away on a course 
 which would take she past Bembridge Point out to the English Channel . as 
 she fly along her helm be put hard down , a puff of smoke shoot out 
 from her quarter , and a cannon ball come hop and splash over 
 the wave , pass within a hundred yard of where we lay . with this 
 farewell greeting she come up into the wind again and continue she 
 course to the southward . 

 ' heart o ' grace ! ' ejaculate Reuben in loose lipped astonishment . ' the 
 murder villain ! ' 

 ' I would to the Lord that King 's ship would snap they up ! ' cry I 
 savagely , for the attack be so unprovoked that it stir my bile . 
 ' what could the rogue have mean ? they be surely drunk or mad ! ' 

 ' pull at the anchor , man , pull at the anchor ! ' my companion shout , 
 spring up from the seat . ' I understand it ! pull at the anchor ! ' 

 ' what then ? ' I ask , help he to haul the great stone up , hand over 
 hand , until it come dripping over the side . 

 ' they be not fire at we , lad . they be aim at some one in the 
 water between we and they . pull , Micah ! put your back into it ! some poor 
 fellow may he drown . ' 

 ' why , I declare ! ' say I , look over my shoulder as I row , ' there 
 be his head upon the crest of a wave . easy , or we shall be over he ! two 
 more stroke and be ready to seize he ! keep up , friend ! there 's help at

 Chapter V. of the Man with the Drooping Lids 

 my mother and my father be sit in their high - back chair on

 ' Broadsword , rapier , Toledo , spontoon , battle - axe , pike or half - pike ,

 ' it come about in this wise , ' he say at last . ' when John of Poland

 Chapter VI . of the Letter that come from the Lowlands 

 in the morning I be up betime , and go forthwith , after the country

 ' to my friend and companion in the cause of the Lord , Joseph 
 clarke.--know , friend , that aid and delivery be come upon Israel , 
 and that the wicked king and those who uphold he shall be smite and 
 entirely cast down , until their place in the land shall know they no

 ' it have chance from time to time that many of the suffering Church ,

 Goodenough , and other whom thou shalt know . of the scot there be the

 Hume , Fletcher of Saltoun , Sir John Cochrane , Dr. Ferguson , Major 
 Elphinstone , and other . to these we would fain have add Locke and old

 anon . I have write to all good man along the coast , bid they to

 mine old comrade , I know well that thou wilt need no bidding of mine to

 in prayer , even as the holy prophet of old ; and perchance , since I hear

 mayst be able to fit out a pikeman or two , or to send a gift towards the 
 military chest , which will be none too plentifully line . we trust 
 not to gold , but to steel and to our own good cause , yet gold will be 
 welcome none the less . should we fall , we fall like man and Christians . 
 should we succeed , we shall see how the perjured James , the persecutor 
 of the saint with the heart like a nether millstone , the man who smile

 Edinburgh -- we shall see how manfully he can bear adversity when it fall

 with thee , for I hear that he hath have good experience in the german , 
 Swedish , and Otttoman wars.--yours in the faith of Christ , Richard 
 Rumbold . 

 ' present my service to thy spouse . let she read Timothy chapter two , 
 ninth to fifteenth verse . '

 and I ? what be I to do ? should I follow my father 's wish , and draw

 Chapter VII . of the Horseman who ride from the West 

 my father set to work forthwith prepare for our equipment , furnish 
 saxon out as well as myself on the most liberal scale , for he be 
 determined that the wealth of his age should be as devoted to the cause 
 as be the strength of his youth . these arrangement have to be carry 
 out with the most extreme caution , for there be many prelatist in 
 the village , and in the present disturb state of the public mind any 
 activity on the part of so well know a man would have at once attract 
 attention . so carefully do the wary old soldier manage matter , 
 however , that we soon find ourselves in a position to start at an 
 hour 's notice , without any of our neighbour be a whit the wiser . 

 his first move be to purchase through an agent two suitable horse at 
 Chichester fair , which be convey to the stable of a trusty Whig 
 farmer live near Portchester , who be order to keep they until 
 they be call for . of these animal one be a mottled grey , of great 
 mettle and power , stand seventeen and a half hand high , and well up 
 to my weight , for in those day , my dear , I have not lay on flesh , and 
 weigh a little under sixteen stone for all my height and strength . 
 a critic might have say that Covenant , for so I name my steed , be a 
 trifle heavy about the head and neck , but I find he a trusty , willing 
 brute , with great power and endurance . Saxon , who when fully accoutre 
 could scarce have weigh more than twelve stone , have a light bay 
 spanish jennet , of great speed and spirit . this mare he name Chloe , 
 ' after a godly maiden of his acquaintance , ' though , as my father 
 remark , there be a somewhat ungodly and heathenish smack about the 
 appellation . these horse and their harness be buy and hold ready 
 without my father appear in the matter in any way . 

 this important point have be settle , there be the further question 
 of arm to be discuss , which give rise to much weighty controversy 
 between Decimus Saxon and my father , each cite many instance from 
 their own experience where the presence or absence of some taslet 
 or arm - guard have be of the deep import to the wearer . your 
 great - grandfather have set his heart upon my wear the breastplate 
 which still bear the dint of the scottish spear at Dunbar , but on 
 try it on we find it be too small for I . I confess that this be a

 my father 's huge proportion , it be marvellous to I to have 
 this convincing proof that I have outgrow he . by rip down the 
 side - leather and pierce hole through which a lace could be pass , my 
 mother manage to arrange it so that I could wear it without discomfort . 
 a pair of taslet or thigh - piece , with guard for the upper arm and 
 gauntlet , be all borrow from the old parliamentary equipment , 
 together with the heavy straight sword and pair of horse pistol which 
 form the usual weapon of a cavalier . my father have choose I a 
 head - piece in Portsmouth , flute , with good barret , pad inside with

 both Saxon and my father agree that I have all that be requisite for 
 a well - appoint soldier . Saxon have purchase a buff - coat , a steel cap , 
 and a pair of jack - boot , so that with the rapi and pistol which my 
 father have present he with , he be ready to take the field at any

 there would , we hop , be no great difficulty in our reach Monmouth 's 
 force when the hour come . in those troublous time the main road be 
 so infest by highwayman and footpad , that it be usual for traveller 
 to carry weapon and even armour for their protection . there be no 
 reason therefore why our appearance should excite suspicion . should

 we be travel to join Henry Somerset , Duke of Beaufort , to whose 
 household we belong . this invention he explain to I , with many 
 point of corroboration which I be to furnish , but when I say 
 positively that I should rather be hang as a rebel than speak a 
 falsehood , he look at I open - eye , and shake his head as one much 
 shock . a few week of campaigning , he say , would soon cure I of my 
 squeamishness . for himself , no more truthful child have ever carry a 
 horn - book , but he have learn to lie upon the Danube , and look upon 
 it as a necessary part of the soldier 's upbringing . ' for what be all 
 stratagem , ambuscade , and outfall but lie upon a large scale ? ' he 
 argue . ' what be an adroit commander but one who hath a facility for 
 disguise the truth ? when , at the battle of Senlac , William the Norman 
 order his man to feign flight in order that they might break his 
 enemy 's array , a wile much practise both by the Scythians of old and by 
 the Croats of our own day , pray what be it but the acting of a lie ? or 
 when Hannibal , have tie torch to the horn of great drove of oxen , 
 cause the Roman Consuls to imagine that his army be in retreat , be it 
 not a deception or infraction of the truth?--a point well bring out 
 by a soldier of repute in the treatise " an in bello dolo uti liceat ; 
 an apud hoste falsiloquio uti liceat . " and so if , after these great 
 model , I in order to gain mine end do announce that we be bind to 
 Beaufort when we be in truth make for Monmouth , be it not in accord 
 with the usage of war and the custom of great commander ? ' all which 
 specious argument I make no attempt to answer , beyond repeat that he 
 might avail himself of the usage , but that he must not look to I for 
 corroboration . on the other hand , I promise to hold my speech and to 
 say nothing which might hamper he , with which pledge he be force to 
 be content . 

 and now at last , my patient listener , I shall be able to carry you out 
 of the humble life of the village , and to cease my gossip of the man who 
 be old when I be young , and who be now lie this many a year in 
 the Bedhampton churchyard . you shall come with I now , and you shall see 
 England as it be in those day , and you shall hear of how we set forth 
 to the war , and of all the adventure which overtake we . and if what 
 I tell you should ever chance to differ from what you have read in the 
 book of Mr. Coke or of Mr. Oldmixon , or of any one else who have set 
 these matter down in print , do ye bear in mind that I be tell of 
 what I see with these very eye , and that I have help to make history , 
 which be a high thing than to write it . 

 it be , then , towards nightfall upon the twelfth day of June 1685 that 
 the news reach our part of the country that Monmouth have land the 
 day before at Lyme , a small seaport on the boundary between dorsetshire 
 and Devonshire . a great beacon blaze upon Portsdown Hill be the first

 messenger clatter through the village street with their head low on 
 their horse ' neck , for the great tiding must be carry to London , 
 that the Governor of Portsmouth might know how to act . ( note B , 
 Appendix . ) we be stand at our doorway in the gloaming , watch

 door and pull his pant horse up . 

 ' be Joseph Clarke here ? ' he ask . 

 ' I be he , ' say my father . 

 ' be these man true ? ' he whisper , point with his whip at Saxon and 
 myself . ' then the trysting - place be Taunton . pass it on to all whom ye 
 know . give my horse a bait and a drink , I beg of ye , for I must get on 
 my way . ' 

 my young brother Hosea look to the tired creature , while we bring

 cake with dust , and his limb be so stiff from the saddle that he

 Danvers and Wildman may be able to raise the city . yester - evening I leave

 home . he have with he Lord Grey of Wark , with Wade , the German Buyse ,

 ' what be amiss , then ? ' 

 ' Dare , the goldsmith of Taunton , hath be slay by Fletcher of Saltoun

 ' aye , aye , ' cry Saxon impatiently , ' there will be some more skilful 
 leader and veteran soldier in the West presently to take his place . 
 but if he know the usage of war , how come it that he should fight upon 
 a private quarrel at such a time ? ' he draw a flat brown book from 
 his bosom , and run his long thin finger down the table of content . 
 ' subisectio nona'--'here be the very case set forth , " an in hello 
 publico provocatus ad duellum privatae amicitiae causa declinare 
 possit , " in which the learn Fleming layeth it down that a man 's 
 private honour must give way to the good of the cause . do it not happen

 chase from the city . so after the onfall-- '

 messenger , stagger to his foot . ' I hope to find a relay at 
 Chichester , and time press . work for the cause now , or be slave for 
 ever . farewell ! ' he clamber into his saddle , and we hear the clatter

 ' the time hath come for you to go , Micah , ' say my father solemnly . ' 
 Nay , wife , do not weep , but rather hearten the lad on his way by a 
 blithe word and a merry face . I need not tell you to fight manfully 
 and fearlessly in this quarrel . should the tide of war set in this 
 direction , you may find your old father rid by your side . let we now 
 bow down and implore the favour of the Almighty upon this expedition . ' 

 we all knelt down in the low - roof , heavy - rafter room while the old 
 man offer up an earnest , strenuous prayer for our success . even now , 
 as I speak to ye , that group rise up before mine eye . I see once 
 again your ancestor 's stern , rugged face , with his brow knit and 
 his cord hand writhe together in the fervour of his supplication . my

 Delectable Mountains -- all small trifle in themselves , but make up 
 among they the marvellous thing we call home , the all - powerful lodestone

 should I ever see it again save in my dream -- I , who be leave this 
 sheltered cove to plunge into the heart of the storm ? 

 the prayer finish , we all rise with the exception of Saxon , who

 it be close upon one in the morning , and all the country folk have be

 I have scarce time to form the same thought before Zachary Palmer 's

 we make our way across the field to the house of Whittier , the Whig

 rebel camp . 


 Chapter VIII . of our start for the war 

 all along the ridge of Portsdown Hill we have the light of Portsmouth 
 and of the harbour ship twinkle beneath we on the left , while on 
 the right the Forest of Bere be ablaze with the signal fire which 
 proclaim the landing of the invader . one great beacon throb upon 
 the summit of Butser , while beyond that , as far as eye could reach , 
 twinkle spark of light show how the tiding be be carry 
 north into Berkshire and eastward into Sussex . of these fire , some be 
 compose of faggot pile into heap , and other of tar barrel set upon

 watcher around it , hear the tramp of our horse and the clank of 
 our arm , set up a loud huzza , think doubtless that we be King 's 
 officer bind for the West . 

 Master Decimus Saxon have fling to the wind the precise demeanour which 
 he have assume in the presence of my father , and rattle away with many 
 a jest and scrap of rhyme or song as we gallop through the darkness . 

 ' gadzook ! ' say he frankly , ' it be good to be able to speak freely 
 without be expect to tag every sentence with a hallelujah or an 
 amen . ' 

 ' you be ever the leader in those pious exercise , ' I remark drily . 

 ' Aye , indeed . you have nick it there ! if a thing must be do , then 
 take a lead in it , whatever it may be . a plaguy good precept , which have

 ' and pray , how do you make your escape ? ' I ask . 

 ' by the use of the wit wherewith Providence hath endow I , ' he 
 answer complacently ; ' for , see that their accursed religion be the 
 blind side of these infidel , I do set myself to work upon it . to this

 cloth , I do imitate they , save only that I pray at great length and 
 with more fervour . ' 

 ' what ! ' I cry in horror . ' you do pretend to be a Mussulman ? ' 

 ' Nay , there be no pretence . I become a Mussulman . that , however , 
 betwixt ourselves , as it might not stand I in very good stead with some 
 Reverend Aminadab Fount - of - grace in the rebel camp , who be no admirer of 
 Mahmoud . ' 

 I be so astounded at the impudence of this confession , come from the
