 family , that I be fairly bereft of speech . Decimus Saxon whistle a few 
 bar of a sprightly tune , and then continued-- 

 ' my perseverance in these exercise soon lead to my being single out 
 from among the other prisoner , until I so prevail upon my gaoler 
 that the door be open for I , and I be allow out on condition 
 of present myself at the prison gate once a day . what use , think ye , 
 do I make of my freedom ? ' 

 ' Nay , you be capable of anything , ' say I. 

 ' I set off forthwith to their chief mosque -- that of St. Sophia . when the 
 door open and the muezzin call , I be ever the first to hurry into 
 devotion and the last to leave they . do I see a Mussulman strike his 
 head upon the pavement , I would strike mine twice . do I see he bend 
 and bow , I be ready to prostrate myself . in this way ere long the piety 
 of the converted Giaour become the talk of the city , and I be provide 
 with a hut in which to make my sacred meditation . here I might have 
 do well , and indeed I have well - nigh make up my mind to set up as 
 a prophet and write an extra chapter to the Koran , when some foolish 
 trifle make the faithful suspicious of my honesty . it be but some 
 nonsense of a wench be find in my hut by some who come to consult

 wagging ; so I think it wisest to give they the slip in a Levantine 
 coaster and leave the Koran uncomplete . it be perhaps as well , for it 
 would be a sore trial to have to give up christian woman and pork , for 
 their garlic - breathing houris and accurse kybobs of sheep 's flesh . ' 

 we have pass through Fareham and Botley during this conversation , and 
 be now make our way down the Bishopstoke road . the soil change 
 about here from chalk to sand , so that our horse ' hoof do but make 
 a dull subdue rattle , which be no bar to our talk -- or rather to my 
 companion 's , for I do little more than listen . in truth , my mind be so

 home behind , that I be in no humour for sprightly chatter . the sky be 
 somewhat clouded , but the moon glint out between the rift , show

 scatter house with garden slop down toward the road . the heavy ,

 ' Hast ever slay a man in anger ? ' ask Saxon , as we gallop along . 

 ' never , ' I answer . 

 ' ha ! you will find that when you hear the clink of steel against steel , 
 and see your foeman 's eye , you will straightway forget all rule , 
 maxim , and precept of the fence which your father or other may have 
 teach you . ' 

 ' I have learn little of the sort , ' say I. my father do but teach 
 I to strike an honest downright blow . this sword can shear through a 
 square inch of iron bar . ' 

 ' Scanderbeg 's sword must have Scanderbeg 's arm , ' he remark . ' I 
 have observe that it be a fine piece of steel . one of the real old 
 text - compeller and psalm - expounder which the faithful draw in the day 
 of yore , when they would : 

           " prove their religion orthodox , 
            by apostolic blow and knock . " 

 you have not fence much , then ? ' 

 ' scarce at all , ' say I. 

 ' it be as well . with an old and tried swordsman like myself , knowledge 
 of the use of his weapon be everything ; but with a young Hotspur of your 
 temper , strength and energy go for much . I have oft remark that those 
 who be most skilled at the shooting of the popinjay , the cleaving of 
 the Turk 's head , and other such sport , be ever laggard in the field . 
 have the popinjay a crossbow as well , and an arrow on the string , or 
 have the Turk a fist as well as a head , our young gallant 's nerve would 
 scarce be as steady over the business . I make no doubt , Master Clarke , 
 that we shall make trusty comrade . what saith old Butler ? 

           " never do trusty squire with knight , 
            or knight with squire ere jump more right . " 

 I have scarce dare to quote " hudibra " for these week past , lest I 
 should set the Covenant ferment in the old man 's vein . '

 speak with more reverence and less flippancy of my father , who would 
 assuredly never have harbour you have he hear the tale which you have 
 tell I even now . ' 

 ' belike not , ' the adventurer answer , chuckle to himself . ' it be a 
 long stride from a mosque to a conventicle . but be not so hot - headed , 
 my friend . you lack that repose of character which will come to you ,

 ' do you bear the same in mind , ' I answer hotly ; ' my temper be 
 peaceful , but covert threat and veiled menace I shall not abide . ' 

 ' Odd 's mercy ! ' he cry . ' I see that you will start carve I anon , and

 enow without fall out among ourselves . what house be those on the

 ' the village of Swathling , ' I reply . ' the light of Bishopstoke lie to 
 the right , in the hollow . ' 

 ' then we be fifteen mile on our way , and methink there be already

 a dark blur which I have remark upon the roadway in front of we have

 he spring down from his saddle , and turn the figure over upon his

 ' how be he slay ! ' I ask in horror , look down at the poor vacant 
 face , the empty house from which the tenant have depart . 

 ' a stab from behind and a tap on the head from the butt of a pistol .

 ' it would indeed , ' say I heartily . ' what great privilege than to 
 execute justice upon such cowardly murderer ! ' 

 ' Pooh ! pooh ! ' he cry . ' justice be a slippery dame , and hath a 
 two - edge sword in her hand . we may have enough of justice in our 
 character as rebel to give we a surfeit of it . I would fain overtake 
 these robber that we may relieve they of their _ spolia opima _ , together 
 with any other wealth which they may have unlawfully amass . my learn 
 friend the Fleming layeth it down that it be no robbery to rob a robber . 
 but where shall we conceal this body ? ' 

 ' wherefore should we conceal it ? ' I ask . 

 ' why , man , unused to war or the precaution of a warrior , you must yet 
 see that should this body be find here , there will be a hue and cry 
 through the country , and that stranger like ourselves will be arrest 
 on suspicion . should we clear ourselves , which be no very easy matter , 
 the justice will at least want to know whence we come and whither we 
 go , which may lead to inquiry that may bode we little good . I shall 
 therefore take the liberty , mine unknown and silent friend , of drag 
 you into yon bush , where for a day or two at least you be like to lie 
 unobserve , and so bring no harm upon honest man . ' 

 ' for God 's sake do not treat it so unkindly , ' I cry , spring down

 need to trail it in so unseemly a fashion . if it must be move hence , I 
 shall carry it with all due reverence . ' so say , I pick the body up 
 in my arm , and bear it to a wayside clump of yellow gorse bush , I 
 lay it solemnly down and draw the branch over it to conceal it . 

 ' you have the thews of an ox and the heart of a woman , ' mutter my 
 companion . ' by the Mass , that old white - head psalm - singer be right ; 
 for if my memory serve I , he say word to that effect . a few handful 
 of dust will hide the stain . now we may jog upon our way without any 
 fear of be call upon to answer for another man 's sin . let I but 
 get my girth tighten and we may soon be out of danger 's way . ' 

 ' I have have to do , ' say Saxon , as we ride onwards , ' with many gentry 
 of this sort , with albanian brigand , the bandit of Piedmont , the 
 Lanzknechte and Freiritter of the Rhine , algerine picaroon , and other

 ' nor do that end all , ' I remark . 

 ' no . there be Tophet behind and the flame of hell . so our good friend 
 the parson tell we . well , if a man be to make no money in this world , 
 be hang at the end of it , and finally burn for ever , he hath assuredly 
 wander on to a thorny track . if , on the other hand , one could always

 ' but what can the well - fill purse do for they ? ' say I. ' what will

 ' true , ' say Saxon dryly ; ' they may , however , prove useful in the 
 meantime . this you say be Bishopstoke . what be the light over yonder ? ' 

 ' they come , I think , from Bishop 's Waltham , ' I answer . 

 ' we must press on , for I would fain be in Salisbury before it be broad 
 day . there we shall put our horse up until evening and have some rest , 
 for there be nothing gain by man or beast come jade to the war . 
 all this day the western road will be crowd with courier , and mayhap 
 patrol by cavalry as well , so that we can not show our face upon it

 and push on at dusk , keep off the main road and make our way across 
 Salisbury Plain and the Somersetshire down , we shall be less likely to 
 come to harm . ' 

 ' but what if Monmouth be engage before we come up to he ? ' I ask . 

 ' then we shall have miss a chance of get our throat cut . why , 
 man , suppose that he have be rout and entirely disperse , would

 yeoman , who have ride all the way from Hampshire to strike in against 
 the King 's enemy ? we might chance to get some reward in money or in

 horse by walk they up this hill . my jennet be as fresh as when we

 the patch of light in the east have increase and broaden , and the sky 
 be mottle with little pink feather of cloud . as we pass over

 shilling , it matter little to I who be King of England . give I a

 from Dean to Salisbury be all straight road with moor , morass , and 
 fenland on either side , break only by the single hamlet of Aldersbury , 
 just over the Wiltshire border . our horse , refresh by the short rest , 
 step out gallantly , and the brisk motion , with the sunlight and the

 either side of the road at the sound of the horse ' hoof , and once a

 ' Anent Monmouth , ' he remark , come back suddenly to the reality of 
 our position . ' it be unlikely that he can take the field for some day , 
 though much depend upon his strike a blow soon , and so raise the 
 courage of his follower before the King 's troop can come down upon

 which be like to prove a more difficult matter . suppose he can raise 
 five thousand man -- and he can not stir with less -- he will not have one 
 musket in five , so the rest must do as they can with pike and bill , or 
 such other rude arm as they can find . all this take time , and though 
 there may be skirmish , there can scarce be any engagement of import

 ' he will have be land three or four day ere we reach he , ' say I. 

 ' hardly time for he with his small staff of officer to enrol his man 
 and divide they into regiment . I scarce expect to find he at Taunton , 
 though we be so directed . Hast ever hear whether there be any rich 
 Papists in those part ? ' 

 ' I know not , ' I reply . 

 ' if so there might be plate chest and silver charger , to say nothing 
 of my lady 's jewel and other such trifle to reward a faithful soldier . 
 what would war be without plunder ! a bottle without the wine -- a shell 
 without the oyster . see the house yonder that peep through the tree . 
 I warrant there be a store of all good thing under that roof , which you 
 and I might have for the asking , do we but ask with our sword in our 
 grip . you be my witness that your father do give and not lend I this 
 horse . ' 

 ' why say you that , then ? ' 

 ' lest he claim a half of whatever booty I may chance to gain . what saith 
 my learn fleming under the heading " an qui militi equum praebuit , 
 praedae ab eo captae particep esse debeat ? " which signifieth " whether 
 he who lendeth a horse hath a claim on the plunder of he who borroweth 
 it . " in this discourse he cite a case wherein a spanish commander 
 have lend a steed to one of his captain , and the say captain have 
 capture the general of the enemy , the commander do sue he for a 
 half share of the twenty thousand crown which form the ransom of the 
 prisoner . a like case be note by the famous Petrinus Bellus in his book

 chapter IX . of a passage of arm at the Blue Boar

 ' instruction from Mistress Mary Clarke to her son Micah , on the twelfth

 ' and oh , Micah , my own bright boy , come back safe to your mother , or my

 chapter X. of our Perilous Adventure on the Plain 

 we be not half a mile from the town before the roll of kettledrum and

 ' we be off the main road now , ' I remark ; ' even should they pursue we ,

 Chapter XI . of the Lonely Man and the Gold Chest

 Chapter XII . of certain passage upon the Moor 

 in the morning , after a breakfast furnish by the remain of our

 both helmet and body - plate be of the fine Milan steel , richly

 on my bill as long as their finger could hold a pen , but slit I if I 
 like bleed my own companion . they might have find a place for I , 
 too , have I consent to play second - fiddle where I have be use to lead 
 the band . I ' faith , I care not what I turn my hand to amongst stranger , 
 but I would fain leave my memory sweet in town . ' 

 ' as to what you propose , of serve we as a valet , ' say I , ' it be not 
 to be think of . we be , in spite of my friend 's waggishness , but two 
 plain blunt countryman , and have no more need of a valet than one of 
 those poet which you have speak of . on the other hand , if you should 
 care to attach yourself to our party , we shall take you where you 
 will see service which shall be more to your taste than the curling of 
 periwig or the brushing of eyebrow . ' 

 ' Nay , nay , my friend . speak not with unseemly levity of the mystery 
 of the toilet , ' he cry . ' ye would yourself be none the bad for 
 a touch of mine ivory comb , and a close acquaintance with the famous 
 skin - purify wash of Murphy which I be myself in the habit of use . ' 

 ' I be beholden to you , sir , ' say Reuben , ' but the famous spring water 
 wash by Providence be quite good enough for the purpose . ' 

 ' and Dame Nature hath place a wig of her own upon I , ' I add , ' which 
 I should be very loth to change . ' 

 ' goth ! Perfect Goths ! ' cry the exquisite , throw up his white 
 hand . ' but here come a heavy tread and the clink of armour in the 
 passage . ' tis our friend the knight of the wrathful countenance , if I 
 mistake not . ' 

 it be indeed Saxon , who stride into the room to tell we that our horse 
 be at the door , and that all be ready for our departure . take 
 he aside I explain to he in a whisper what have pass between the 
 stranger and ourselves , with the circumstance which have lead I to 
 suggest that he should join our party . the old soldier frown at the 
 news . 

 ' what have we to do with such a coxcomb ? ' he say . ' we have hard fare 
 and hard blow before we . he be not fit for the work . ' 

 ' you say yourself that Monmouth will be weak in horse , ' I answer . 
 ' here be a well - appoint cavalier , who be to all appearance a desperate 
 man and ready for anything . why should we not enrol he ? ' 

 ' I fear , ' say Saxon , ' that his body may prove to be like the bran of 
 a fine cushion , of value only for what it have around it . however , it be 
 perhaps for the good . the handle to his name may make he welcome in the 
 camp , for from what I hear there be some dissatisfaction at the way in 
 which the gentry stand aloof from the enterprise . ' 

 ' I have fear , ' I remark , still speak in a whisper , ' that we be 
 about to lose one of our party instead of gain one in this Bruton 
 inn . ' 

 ' I have think well of it , ' he answer , with a smile . ' Nay , I will 
 tell you of it anon . well , Sir Gervas Jerome , ' he add aloud , turn 
 to our new associate , ' I hear that you be come with we . for a day you 
 must be content to follow without question or remark . be that agree ! ' 

 ' with all my heart , ' cry Sir Gervas . 

 ' then here be a bumper to our well acquaintance , ' cry Saxon , raise 
 his glass . 

 ' I pledge ye all , ' quoth the gallant . ' here be to a fair fight , and may 
 the good man win . ' 

 ' Donnerblitz , man ! ' say Saxon . ' I believe there be mettle in you for all 
 your gay plume . I do conceive a liking for you . give I your hand ! ' 

 the soldier of fortune 's great brown grip enclose the delicate hand 
 of our new friend in a pledge of comradeship . then , have pay our 
 reckoning and bade a cordial adieu to Dame Hobson , who glance methought 
 somewhat reproachfully or expectantly at Saxon , we spring on our steed 
 and continue our journey amidst a crowd of star villager , who 
 huzzae lustily as we ride out from amongst they . 



 Chapter XIV . of the Stiff - legged Parson and his Flock 

 our road lie through Castle Carey and Somerton , which be small town 
 lie in the midst of a most beautiful pastoral country , well woode and 
 water by many stream . the valley along the centre of which the road 
 lie be rich and luxuriant , shelter from the wind by long roll 
 hill , which be themselves highly cultivate . here and there we pass 
 the ivy - clothe turret of an old castle or the peaked gable of a ramble 
 country house , protrude from amongst the tree and mark the country 
 seat of some family of repute . More than once , when these mansion be 
 not far from the road , we be able to perceive the unrepaire dint and 
 fracture on the wall receive during the stormy period of the civil 
 trouble . fairfax it seem have be down that way , and have leave abundant 
 trace of his visit . I have no doubt that my father would have have much 
 to say of these sign of Puritan wrath have he be rid at our side . 

 the road be crowd with peasant who be travel in two strong 
 current , the one set from east to west , and the other from west to 
 east . the latter consist principally of aged people and of child , 
 who be be send out of harm 's way to reside in the less disturbed 
 county until the trouble should be over . many of these poor folk be 
 push barrow in front of they , in which a few bedclothe and some 
 crack utensil represent the whole of their worldly good . other 
 more prosperous have small cart , draw by the wild shaggy colt which 
 be breed on the Somerset moor . what with the spirit of the half - tame 
 beast and the feebleness of the driver , accident be not uncommon , 
 and we pass several unhappy group who have be tumble with their 
 property into a ditch , or who be stand in anxious debate round a 
 crack shaft or a broken axle . 

 the countryman who be make for the West be upon the other hand 
 man in the prime of life , with little or no baggage . their brown face , 
 heavy boot , and smockfrock proclaim most of they to be mere hind , 
 though here and there we overtook man who , by their top - boot and 
 corduroy , may have be small farmer or yeoman . these fellow walk 
 in gang , and be arm for the most part with stout oak cudgel , 
 which be carry as an aid to their journey , but which in the hand of 
 powerful man might become formidable weapon . from time to time one 
 of these traveller would strike up a psalm tune , when all the other 
 within earshot would join in , until the melody ripple away down the 
 road . as we pass some scowl angrily at we , while other whisper 
 together and shake their head , in evident doubt as to our character and 
 aim . now and again among the people we mark the tall broad - brim 
 hat and Geneva mantle which be the badge of the Puritan clergy . 

 ' we be in Monmouth 's country at last , ' say Saxon to I , for Reuben 
 Lockarby and Sir Gervas Jerome have ride on ahead . ' this be the raw 
 material which we shall have to lick into soldiership . ' 

 ' and no bad material either , ' I reply , take note of the sturdy 
 figure and bold hearty face of the man . ' think ye that they be bind 
 for Monmouth 's camp , then ? ' 

 ' aye , be they . see you yon long - limbe parson on the left -- he with the 
 pen - house hat . markest thou not the stiffness wherewith he move his 
 left leg ! ' 

 ' why , yes ; he be travel - wear doubtless . ' 

 ' Ho ! ho ! ' laugh my companion . ' I have see such a stiffness before 
 now . the man hath a straight sword within he leg of his breech . a 
 regular parliamentary tuck , I will warrant . when he be on safe ground he 
 will produce it , aye , and use it too , but until he be out of all danger 
 of fall in with the King 's horse he be shy of strap it to his 
 belt . he be one of the old breed by his cut , who : 

           " call fire and sword and desolation , 
            a godly thorough reformation . " 

 Old Samuel hath they to a penstroke ! there be another ahead of he 
 there , with the head of a scythe inside his smock . can you not see the 
 outline ? I warrant there be not one of the rascal but hath a pike - head 
 or sickle - blade conceal somewhere about he . I begin to feel the 
 breath of war once more , and to grow young with it . Hark ye , lad ! I be 
 glad that I do not tarry at the inn . ' 

 ' you seem to be in two mind about it , ' say I. 

 ' aye , aye . she be a fine woman , and the quarter be comfortable . I do 
 not gainsay it . but marriage , d'ye see , be a citadel that it be plaguy 
 easy to find one 's way into , but once in old Tilly himself could not 
 bring one out again with credit , I have know such a device on the 
 Danube , where at the first onfall the Mamelukes have abandon the 
 breach for the very purpose of ensnare the imperial troop in the 
 narrow street beyond , from which few ever return . old bird be not 
 catch with such wile . I do succeed in gain the ear of one of the 
 gossip , and ask he what he could tell I of the good dame and her 
 inn . it seemeth that she be somewhat of a shrew upon occasion , and that 
 her tongue have more to do with her husband 's death than the dropsy which 
 the leech put it down to . again , a new inn hath be start in the 
 village , which be well - manage , and be like to draw the custom from 
 she . it be , too , as you have say , a dull sleepy spot . all these reason 
 weigh with I , and I decide that it would be good to raise my siege 
 of the widow , and to retreat whilst I could yet do so with the credit 
 and honour of war . ' 

 '' tis good so , ' say I ; ' you could not have settle down to a life of 
 toping and ease . but our new comrade , what think you of he ? ' 

 ' faith ! ' Saxon answer , ' we shall extend into a troop of horse if we 
 add to our number every gallant who be in want of a job . as to this Sir 
 Gervas , however , I think , as I say at the inn , that he hath more mettle 
 in he than one would judge at first sight . these young sprig of the 
 gentry will always fight , but I doubt if he be harden enough or 
 hath constancy enough for such a campaign as this be like to be . his

 though Monmouth be a man of easy virtue , the saint be like to have the

 showy grey stallion and gaze back at we . mark his riding - hat tilt 
 over his eye , his open bosom , his whip dangle from his button - hole ,

 the peasant beside he . he will have to change his style if he be to 
 fight by the side of the fanatic . but hark ! I be much mistaken if they 
 have not already get themselves into trouble . ' 

 our friend have pull up their horse to await our coming . they have

 ' a flask or two of scent amongst they would not be amiss , ' he remark ;

 ' stand on your guard , but do not draw , ' cry Saxon . ' what the henker 
 hath come over the chaw - bacon ? they mean mischief . how now , friend , 
 why this uproar ? ' 

 this question instead of allay the tumult appear to make it tenfold 
 bad . all round we twenty deep be savage face and angry eye ,

 a stone or two have already whistle past our ear , and we have be 
 force in self - defence to draw our sword , when the tall minister whom

 ' what ! he hath come to the title already ! ' exclaim Saxon . ' know then

 ' he lie , good Master Pettigrue , he lie most foully , ' shout a burly

 ' what proof have we that ye be not in the pay of the usurper , and on 
 your way to oppress the faithful ? ' ask the Puritan divine . 

 ' I tell you , man , ' say Saxon impatiently , ' that we have travel all 
 the way from Hampshire to fight against James Stuart . we will ride with 
 ye to Monmouth 's camp , and what well proof could ye desire than that ? ' 

 ' it may be that ye do but seek an opportunity of escape from our 
 bondage , ' the minister observe , after confer with one or two of the 
 lead peasant . ' it be our opinion , therefore , that before come 
 with we ye must deliver unto we your sword , pistol , and other carnal 
 weapon . ' 

 ' nay , good sir , that can not be , ' our leader answer . ' a cavalier may 
 not with honour surrender his blade or his liberty in the manner ye 
 demand . keep close to my bridle - arm , Clarke , and strike home at any 
 rogue who lay hand on you . ' 

 a hum of anger rise from the crowd , and a score of stick and 
 scythe - blade be raise against we , when the minister again interpose

 ' do I hear aright ? ' he ask . ' be your name Clarke ? ' 

 ' it be , ' I answer . 

 ' your christian name ? ' 

 ' Micah . ' 

 ' live at ? ' 

 ' Havant . ' 

 the clergyman confer for a few moment with a grizzly - beard , 
 harsh - faced man dress in black buckram who stand at his elbow .

 to tell we the name of an old soldier , skilled in the german war , who 
 be to have come with ye to the camp of the faithful . ' 

 ' why , this be he , ' I answer ; ' Decimus Saxon be his name . ' 

 ' aye , aye , Master Pettigrue , ' cry the old man . ' the very name give by

 ' right glad I be to see ye , then , ' say the stalwart minister heartily . 
 ' friend , I can answer for these gentleman that they favour the honest 
 folk and the old cause . ' 

 at these word the rage of the mob turn in an instant into the most

 ' Joshua Pettigrue be my name , gentleman , ' say he ; ' I be an unworthy

 ' truly , sir , it be a wise resolution , ' say Saxon grimly , ' for if a 
 troop of horse come down upon these good people the pastor would find

 ' Nay , that could never be ! ' cry Master Pettigrue with fervour . ' say 
 rather that pastor , flock , and all would find their way along the thorny

 ' over Salisbury Plain and so through Bruton . ' 

 ' and see ye or meet ye any of our people upon the way ? ' 

 ' none , ' Saxon answer . ' we leave the Blue Guards at Salisbury , however , 
 and we see either they or some other horse regiment near this side of

 ' ah , there be a gathering of the eagle , ' cry Master Joshua Pettigrue ,

 utterly in his wrath , and they shall be destroy . '

 ' ah , Brother Sandcroft , ' cry the pastor , ' there be much sweetness and

 we not lighten it by a song of praise ? where be Brother Thistlethwaite , 
 whose voice be as the cymbal , the tabor , and the dulcimer ? ' 

 ' Lo , most pious Master Pettigrue , ' say Saxon , ' I have myself at time 
 venture to lift up my voice before the Lord . ' without any further 
 apology he break out in stentorian tone into the follow hymn , the 
 refrain of which be catch up by pastor and congregation . 

           the Lord he be a morion 
            that guard I from all wound ; 
           the Lord he be a coat of mail 
            that circle I all round . 
           who then fear to draw the sword , 
            and fight the battle of the Lord ? 

           the Lord he be the buckler true 
            that swing on my left arm ; 
           the Lord he be the plate of proof 
            that shieldeth I from harm . 
           who then fear to draw the sword , 
            and fight the battle of the Lord ? 

           who then dread the violent , 
            or fear the man of pride ? 
           or shall I flee from two or three 
            if he be by my side ? 
           who then fear to draw the sword , 
            and fight the battle of the Lord ! 

           my faith be like a citadel 
            Girt round with moat and wall , 
           no mine , or sap , or breach , or gap 
            can ere prevail at all . 
           who then fear to draw the sword , 
            and fight the battle of the Lord ? 

 Saxon cease , but the Reverend Joshua Pettigrue wave his long arm and 
 repeat the refrain , which be take up again and again by the long 
 column of march peasant . 

 ' it be a godly hymn , ' say our companion , who have , to my disgust and 
 to the evident astonishment of Reuben and Sir Gervas , resume the 
 snuffling , whine voice which he have use in the presence of my father . 
 ' it hath avail much on the field of battle . '

 Chapter XV . of our Brush with the King 's dragoon 

 some little distance from we a branch road run into that along which we 
 and our motley assemblage of companion - in - arm be travel . this 
 road curve down the side of a well - woode hill , and then over the level 
 for a quarter of a mile or so before opening on the other . just at the 
 brow of the rise ground there stand a thick bristle of tree , amid the 
 trunk of which there come and go a bright shimmer of sparkle steel , 
 which proclaim the presence of armed man . far back , where the road 
 take a sudden turn and run along the ridge of the hill , several horseman 
 could be plainly see outline against the evening sky . so peaceful , 
 however , be the long sweep of countryside , mellow by the golden light 
 of the set sun , with a score of village steeple and manor - house 
 peep out from amongst the wood , that it be hard to think that the 
 thundercloud of war be really lower over that fair valley , and that 
 at any instant the lightning might break from it . 

 the country folk , however , appear to have no difficulty at all in 
 understand the danger to which they be expose . the fugitive from 
 the West give a yell of consternation , and run wildly down the road or 
 whip up their beast of burden in the endeavour to place as safe a 
 distance as possible between themselves and the threaten attack . the 
 chorus of shrill cry and shout , with the cracking of whip , creak 
 of wheel , and the occasional crash when some cart load of good come to 
 grief , make up a most deafening uproar , above which our leader 's voice 
 resound in sharp , eager exhortation and command . when , however , the 
 loud brazen shriek from a bugle break from the wood , and the head of 
 a troop of horse begin to descend the slope , the panic become great 
 still , and it be difficult for we to preserve any order at all amidst 
 the wild rush of the terrify fugitive . 

 ' stop that cart , Clarke , ' cry Saxon vehemently , point with his 
 sword to an old waggon , pile high with furniture and bedding , which be 
 lumber along draw by two raw - boned colt . at the same moment I see 
 he drive his horse into the crowd and catch at the rein of another 
 similar one . 

 give Covenant 's bridle a shake I be soon abreast of the cart which 
 he have indicate , and manage to bring the furious young horse to a 
 stand - still . 

 ' bring it up ! ' cry our leader , work with the coolness which only a 
 long apprenticeship to war can give . ' now , friend , cut the trace ! ' 
 a dozen knife be at work in a moment , and the kicking , struggle 
 animal scamper off , leave their burden behind they . Saxon sprang 
 off his horse and set the example in drag the waggon across the 
 roadway , while some of the peasant , under the direction of Reuben 
 Lockarby and of Master Joshua Pettigrue , arrange a couple of other 
 cart to block the way fifty yard far down . the latter precaution 
 be to guard against the chance of the royal horse rid through 
 the field and attack we from behind . so speedily be the scheme 
 conceive and carry out , that within a very few minute of the first 
 alarm we find ourselves protect front and rear by a lofty barricade , 
 while within this improvise fortress be a garrison of a hundred and 
 fifty man . 

 ' what firearm have we amongst we ? ' ask Saxon hurriedly . 

 ' a dozen pistol at the most , ' reply the elderly Puritan , who be 
 address by his companion as Hope - above Williams . ' John Rodway , 
 the coachman , hath his blunderbuss . there be also two godly man from 
 Hungerford , who be keeper of game , and who have bring their piece 
 with they . ' 

 ' they be here , sir , ' cry another , point to two stout , bearded 
 fellow , who be ram charge into their long - barrel musket . 
 ' their name be Wat and Nat Millman . ' 

 ' two who can hit their mark be worth a battalion who shoot wide , ' our 
 leader remark , ' get under the waggon , my friend , and rest your piece 
 upon the spoke . never draw trigger until the son of Belial be within 
 three pike ' length of ye . ' 

 ' my brother and I , ' quoth one of they , ' can hit a running doe at two 
 hundred pace . our life be in the hand of the Lord , but two , at 
 least , of these hire butcher we shall send before we . ' 

 ' as gladly as ever we slew stoat or wild - cat , ' cry the other , slip 
 under the waggon . ' we be keep the Lord 's preserve now , brother Wat , 
 and truly these be some of the vermin that infest they . ' 

 ' let all who have pistol line the waggon , ' say Saxon , tie his mare 
 to the hedge -- an example which we all follow . ' Clarke , do you take 
 charge upon the right with Sir Gervas , while Lockarby assist Master 
 Pettigrue upon the left . ye other shall stand behind with stone . 
 should they break through our barricade , slash at the horse with your 
 scythe . once down , the rider be no match for ye . ' 

 a low sullen murmur of determine resolution rise from the peasant , 
 mingle with pious ejaculation and little scrap of hymn or of prayer . 
 they have all produce from under their smock rustic weapon of some 
 sort . ten or twelve have petronel , which , from their antique look and 
 rusty condition , threaten to be more dangerous to their possessor 
 than to the enemy . other have sickle , scythe - blade , flail , 
 half - pike , or hammer , while the remainder carry long knife and 
 oaken club . simple as be these weapon , history have prove that in 
 the hand of man who be deeply stir by religious fanaticism they be 
 by no mean to be despise . one have but to look at the stern , set face 
 of our follower , and the gleam of exultation and expectancy which shine 
 from their eye , to see that they be not the man to quail , either from 
 superior number or equipment . 

 ' by the Mass ! ' whisper Sir Gervas , ' it be magnificent ! an hour of this 
 be worth a year in the Mall . the old Puritan bull be fairly at bay . let 
 we see what sort of sport the bull - pup make in the baiting of he ! I will 
 lay five piece to four on the chaw - bacon ! ' 

 ' Nay , it be no matter for idle betting , ' say I shortly , for his 
 light - hearted chatter annoy I at so solemn a moment . 

 ' five to four on the soldier , then ! ' he persist . ' it be too good a 
 match not to have a stake on it one way or the other . ' 

 ' our life be the stake , ' say I. 

 ' faith , I have forget it ! ' he reply , still mumble his toothpick . ' " to 
 be or not to be ? " as Will of Stratford say . Kynaston be great on the 
 passage . but here be the bell that ring the curtain up . ' 

 whilst we have be make our disposition the troop of horse -- for there 
 appear to be but one -- have trot down the cross - road , and have draw 
 up across the main highway . they number , as far as I could judge , 
 about ninety trooper , and it be evident from their three - cornered 
 hat , steel plate , red sleeve , and bandolier , that they be dragoon 
 of the regular army . the main body halt a quarter of a mile from we , 
 while three officer ride to the front and hold a short consultation , 
 which end in one of they set spur to his horse and canter down 
 in our direction . a bugler follow a few pace behind he , wave a 
 white kerchief and blow an occasional blast upon his trumpet . 

 ' here come an envoy , ' cry Saxon , who be stand up in the waggon . 
 ' now , my brother , we have neither kettle - drum nor tinkling brass , but 
 we have the instrument wherewith Providence hath endow we . let we show 
 the redcoat that we know how to use it . 

           " who then dread the violent , 
            or fear the man of pride ? 
            or shall I flee from two or three 
            if he be by my side ? " ' 

 seven score voice break in , in a hoarse roar , upon the chorus-- 

           ' who then fear to draw the sword , 
            and fight the battle of the Lord ? ' 

 I could well believe at that moment that the Spartans have find the lame 
 singer Tyrtaeus the most successful of their general , for the sound of 
 their own voice increase the confidence of the country folk , while the 
 martial word of the old hymn rouse the dogged spirit in their breast . 
 so high do their courage run that they break off their song with a 
 loud warlike shout , wave their weapon above their head , and ready I 
 verily believe to march out from their barricade and make straight for 
 the horseman . in the midst of this clamour and turmoil the young dragoon 
 officer , a handsome , olive - face lad , ride fearlessly up to the barrier , 
 and pull up his beautiful roan steed , hold up his hand with an 
 imperious gesture which demand silence . 

 ' who be the leader of this conventicle ? ' he ask . 

 ' address your message to I , sir , ' say our leader from the top of 
 the waggon , ' but understand that your white flag will only protect you 
 whilst you use such language as may come from one courteous adversary to 
 another . say your say or retire . ' 

 ' courtesy and honour , ' say the officer , with a sneer , ' be not extend 
 to rebel who be in arm against their lawful sovereign . if you be the 
 leader of this rabble , I warn you if they be not disperse within five 
 minute by this watch'--he pull out an elegant gold time - piece--'we 
 shall ride down upon they and cut they to piece . ' 

 ' the Lord can protect his own , ' Saxon answer , amid a fierce hum of 
 approval from the crowd . ' be this all thy message ? ' 

 ' it be all , and you will find it enough , you presbyterian traitor , ' 
 cry the dragoon cornet . ' listen to I , misguided fool , ' he continue , 
 stand up upon his stirrup and speak to the peasant at the 
 other side of the waggon . ' what chance have ye with your whittle and 
 cheese - scraper ? Ye may yet save your skin if ye will but deliver up 
 your leader , throw down what ye be pleased to call your arm , and 
 trust to the King 's mercy . ' 

 ' this exceedeth the limitation of your privilege , ' say Saxon , draw 
 a pistol from his belt and cock it . ' if you say another word to 
 seduce these people from their allegiance , I fire . ' 

 ' hope not to benefit Monmouth , ' cry the young officer , disregard 
 the threat , and still address his word to the peasant . ' the whole 
 royal army be draw round he and-- ' 

 ' have a care ! ' shout our leader , in a deep harsh voice . 

 ' his head within a month shall roll upon the scaffold . ' 

 ' but you shall never live to see it , ' say Saxon , and stoop over 
 he fire straight at the cornet 's head . at the flash of the pistol the 
 trumpeter wheel round and gallop for his life , while the roan horse 
 turn and follow with its master still seat firmly in the saddle . 

 ' verily you have miss the Midianite ! ' cry Hope - above Williams . 

 ' he be dead , ' say our leader , pour a fresh charge into his pistol . 
 ' it be the law of war , Clarke , ' he add , look round at I . ' he hath 
 choose to break it , and must pay forfeit . ' 

 as he speak I see the young officer lean gradually over in his saddle , 
 until , when about half - way back to his friend , he lose his balance and 
 fall heavily in the roadway , turn over two or three time with 
 the force of his fall , and lie at last still and motionless , a 
 dust - colour heap . a loud yell of rage break from the trooper at 
 the sight , which be answer by a shout of defiance from the Puritan 
 peasantry . 

 ' down on your face ! ' cry Saxon ; ' they be about to fire . ' 

 the crackle of musketry and a storm of bullet , ping on the hard 
 ground , or cut twig from the hedge on either side of we , lent 
 emphasis to our leader 's order . many of the peasant crouch behind the 
 feather bed and table which have be pull out of the cart . some lay 
 in the waggon itself , and some shelter themselves behind or underneath 
 it . other again line the ditch on either side or lie flat upon the 
 roadway , while a few show their belief in the working of Providence 
 by stand upright without flinch from the bullet . amongst these 
 latter be Saxon and Sir Gervas , the former to set an example to his 
 raw troop , and the latter out of pure laziness and indifference . 
 Reuben and I sit together in the ditch , and I can assure you , my dear 
 grandchild , that we feel very much inclined to bob our head when we 
 hear the bullet pip all around they . if any soldier ever tell you 
 that he do not the first time that he be under fire , then that soldier 
 be not a man to trust . after sit rigid and silent , however , as if 
 we have both stiff neck , for a very few minute , the feeling pass 
 completely away , and from that day to this it have never return to I . 
 you see familiarity breed contempt with bullet as with other thing , 
 and though it be no easy matter to come to like they , like the King of 
 Sweden or my Lord Cutts , it be not so very hard to become indifferent to 
 they . 

 the cornet 's death do not remain long unavenged . a little old man with 
 a sickle , who have be stand near Sir Gervas , give a sudden sharp 
 cry , and spring up into the air with a loud ' glory to God ! ' fall flat 
 upon his face dead . a bullet have strike he just over the right eye . 
 almost at the same moment one of the peasant in the waggon be shoot 
 through the chest , and sit up cough blood all over the wheel . I see

 bed under his head , so that he lie breathe heavily and pattering

 fierce carbine fire he walk boldly up and down , with a drawn rapier in 
 his left hand -- for he be a left - handed man -- and his Bible in the other . 
 ' this be what you be die for , dear brother , ' he cry continually , 
 hold the brown volume up in the air ; ' be ye not ready to die for 
 this ? ' and every time he ask the question a low eager murmur of assent 
 rise from the ditch , the waggon , and the road . 

 ' they aim like yokel at a Wappenschaw , ' say Saxon , seat himself 
 on the side of the waggon . ' like all young soldier they fire too high . 
 when I be an adjutant it be my custom to press down the barrel of the 
 musket until my eye tell I that they be level . these rogue think 
 that they have do their part if they do but let the gun off , though 
 they be as like to hit the plover above we as ourselves . ' 

 ' five of the faithful have fall , ' say Hope - above Williams . ' shall we 
 not sally forth and do battle with the child of antichrist ? be we to 
 lie here like so many popinjay at a fair for the trooper to practise 
 upon ? ' 

 ' there be a stone barn over yonder on the hill - side , ' I remark . ' if 
 we who have horse , and a few other , be to keep the dragoon in 
 play , the people might be able to reach it , and so be shelter from the 
 fire . ' 

 ' at least let my brother and I have a shot or two back at they , ' cry 
 one of the marksman beside the wheel . 

 to all our entreaty and suggestion , however , our leader only reply 
 by a shake of the head , and continue to swing his long leg over the 
 side of the waggon with his eye fix intently upon the horseman , many

 of their charger . 

 ' this can not go on , sir , ' say the pastor , in a low earnest voice ; ' two 
 more man have just be hit . ' 

 ' if fifty more man be hit we must wait until they charge , ' Saxon 
 answer . ' what would you do , man ? if you leave this shelter you will

 I have do , you will learn to put up quietly with what be not to be

 nachhut of the imperial troop be follow by Croats , who be in

 we shall not have to wait long now . ' 

 the dragoon be indeed climb into their saddle again , and form

 at the same time about thirty man detach themselves from the main body

 oath under his breath as he observe they . 

 ' they have some knowledge of warfare after all , ' say he . ' they mean to 
 charge we flank and front . Master Joshua , see that your scythesman 
 line the quickset hedge upon the right . stand well up , my brother , and 
 flinch not from the horse . you man with the sickle , lie in the ditch 
 there , and cut at the leg of the brute . a line of stone thrower 
 behind that . a heavy stone be as sure as a bullet at close quarter . if 
 ye would see your wife and child again , make that hedge good against 
 the horseman . now for the front attack . let the man who carry petronel 
 come into the waggon . two of your , Clarke , and two of your , Lockarby . 
 I can spare one also . that make five . now here be ten other of a sort 
 and three musket . twenty shot in all . have you no pistol , Sir Gervas ? 

 ' no , but I can get a pair , ' say our companion , and spring upon his 
 horse he force his way through the ditch , past the barrier , and so down 
 the road in the direction of the dragoon . 

 the movement be so sudden and so unexpected that there be a dead 
 silence for a few second , which be break by a general howl of hatred

 Amalekite ! ' they shriek . ' he hath go to join his kind ! he hath

 horseman , who be still form up for a charge and wait for the 
 flank party to get into position , they sit still and silent , not

 towards they . 

 we be not leave long in doubt , however . he have no soon reach the 
 spot where the cornet have fall than he spring from his horse and

 contain his powder and ball . mount at his leisure , amid a shower of 
 bullet which puff up the white dust all around he , he ride onwards

 bad for his adventure , though a ball have graze his horse 's fetlock

 peasant raise a shout of jubilation as he ride in , and from that day 
 forward our friend be permit to wear his gay trapping and to bear 
 himself as he would , without be suspect of have mount the 
 livery of Satan or of be want in zeal for the cause of the saint . 

 ' they be come , ' cry Saxon . ' let no man draw trigger until he see 
 I shoot . if any do , I shall send a bullet through he , though it be 
 my last shot and the trooper be amongst we . ' 

 as our leader utter this threat and look grimly round upon we with 
 an evident intention of execute it , a shrill blare of a bugle burst 
 from the horseman in front of we , and be answer by those upon our 
 flank . at the signal both body set spur to their horse and dash 
 down upon we at the top of their speed . those in the field be delay 
 for a few moment , and throw into some disorder , by find that the 
 ground immediately in front of they be soft and boggy , but have 
 make their way through it they re - form upon the other side and ride 
 gallantly at the hedge . our own opponent , have a clear course before 
 they , never slacken for an instant , but come thunder down with a 
 jingling of harness and a tempest of oath upon our rude barricade . 

 ah , my child ! when a man in his age try to describe such thing as 
 these , and to make other see what he have see , it be only then that he 
 understand what a small stock of language a plain man keep by he for 
 his ordinary use in the world , and how unfit it be to meet any call 
 upon it . for though at this very moment I can myself see that white 
 Somersetshire road , with the wild whirling charge of the horseman , the 
 red angry face of the man , and the gap nostril of the horse all 
 wreathe and frame in cloud of dust , I can not hope to make it clear 
 to your young eye , which never have look , and , I trust , never shall 
 look , upon such a scene . when , too , I think of the sound , a mere rattle 
 and jingle at first , but grow in strength and volume with every step , 
 until it come upon we with a thunderous rush and roar which give the 
 impression of irresistible power , I feel that that too be beyond the 
 power of my feeble word to express . to inexperienced soldier like 
 ourselves it seem impossible that our frail defence and our feeble 
 weapon could check for an instant the impetus and weight of the 
 dragoon . to right and leave I see white set face , open - eyed and rigid , 
 unflinche , with a stubbornness which rise less from hope than from 
 despair . all round rise exclamation and prayer . ' Lord , save Thy 
 people ! ' ' Mercy , Lord , mercy ! ' ' be with we this day ! ' ' receive our 
 soul , o merciful Father ! ' Saxon lie across the waggon with his eye 
 glinting like diamond and his petronel present at the full length 
 of his rigid arm . follow his example we all take aim as steadily as 
 possible at the first rank of the enemy . our only hope of safety lay 
 in make that one discharge so deadly that our opponent should be too 
 much shake to continue their attack . 

 would the man never fire ? they could not be more than ten pace from we . 
 I could see the buckle of the man 's plate and the powder charge in 
 their bandolier . one more stride yet , and at last our leader 's pistol 
 flash and we pour in a close volley , support by a shower of heavy 
 stone from the sturdy peasant behind . I could hear they splinter 
 against casque and cuirass like hail upon a casement . the cloud of smoke 
 veil for an instant the line of gallop steed and gallant rider 
 drift slowly aside to show a very different scene . a dozen man and 
 horse be roll in one wild blood - spurt heap , the unwounde 
 fall over those whom our ball and stone have bring down . 
 struggle , snort charger , iron - shod foot , staggering figure rise 
 and fall , wild , hatless , bewildered man half stun by a fall , and 
 not know which way to turn -- that be the foreground of the picture , 
 while behind they the remainder of the troop be rid furiously back , 
 wound and hale , all drive by the one desire of get to a place of 
 safety where they might rally their shattered formation . a great shout 
 of praise and thanksgiving rise from the delighted peasant , and 
 surge over the barricade they strike down or secure the few uninjured 
 trooper who have boon unable or unwilling to join their companion in 
 their flight . the carbine , sword , and bandolier be eagerly pounce 
 upon by the victor , some of whom have serve in the militia , and know 
 well how to handle the weapon which they have win . 

 the victory , however , be by no mean complete . the flanking squadron 
 have ride boldly at the hedge , and a dozen or more have force their way 
 through , in spite of the shower of stone and the desperate thrust of 
 the pikeman and scytheman . once amongst the peasant , the long sword 
 and the armour of the dragoon give they a great advantage , and though 
 the sickle bring several of the horse to the ground the soldier 
 continue to lay about they freely , and to beat back the fierce but 
 ill - armed resistance of their opponent . a dragoon sergeant , a man of 
 great resolution and of prodigious strength , appear to be the leader 
 of the party , and encourage his follower both by word and example . 
 a stab from a half - pike bring his horse to the ground , but he 
 spring from the saddle as it fall , and avenge its death by a sweeping 
 back - handed cut from his broadsword . wave his hat in his left hand he 
 continue to rally his man , and to strike down every Puritan who come 
 against he , until a blow from a hatchet bring he on his knee and 
 a flail stroke break his sword close by the hilt . at the fall of their 
 leader his comrade turn and flee through the hedge , but the gallant 
 fellow , wound and bleeding , still show fight , and would assuredly 
 have be knock upon the head for his pain have I not pick he up 
 and throw he into the waggon , where he have the good sense to lie quiet 
 until the skirmish be at an end . of the dozen who break through , not 
 more than four escape , and several other lay dead or wound upon the 
 other side of the hedge , impale by scythe - blade or knock off 
 their horse by stone . altogether nine of the dragoon be slay 
 and fourteen wound , while we retain seven unscathed prisoner , ten 
 horse fit for service , and a score or so of carbine , with good store 
 of match , powder , and ball . the remainder of the troop fire a 
 single , straggling , irregular volley , and then gallop away down the 
 cross - road , disappear amongst the tree from which they have emerge . 

 all this , however , have not be accomplish without severe loss upon 
 our side . three man have be kill and six wound , one of they 
 very seriously , by the musketry fire . five have be cut down when 
 the flank party break their way in , and only one of these could be 
 expect to recover . in addition to this , one man have lose his life 
 through the bursting of an ancient petronel , and another have his arm 
 break by the kick of a horse . our total loss , therefore , be eight 
 kill and the same wound , which could not but be regard as a very 
 moderate number when we consider the fierceness of the skirmish , and the 
 superiority of our enemy both in discipline and in equipment . 

 so elated be the peasant by their victory , that those who have secure 
 horse be clamorous to be allow to follow the dragoon , the more so 
 as Sir Gervas Jerome and Reuben be both eager to lead they . Decimus 
 Saxon refuse , however , to listen to any such scheme , nor do he show 
 more favour to the Reverend Joshua Pettigrue 's proposal , that he should 
 in his capacity as pastor mount immediately upon the waggon , and improve 
 the occasion by a few word of healing and unction . 

 ' it be true , good Master Pettigrue , that we owe much praise and much 
 outpouring , and much sweet and holy contending , for this blessing which 
 hath come upon Israel , ' say he , ' but the time hath not yet arrive . 
 there be an hour for prayer and an hour for labour . Hark ye , friend'--to 
 one of the prisoners--'to what regiment do you belong ? ' 

 ' it be not for I to reply to your question , ' the man answer sulkily . 

 Nay , then , we will try if a string round your scalp and a few twist of a 
 drumstick will make you find your tongue , ' say Saxon , push his face 
 up to that of the prisoner , and star into his eye with so savage an 
 expression that the man shrink away affrighted . 

 ' it be a troop of the second dragoon regiment , ' he say . 

 ' where be the regiment itself ? ' 

 ' we leave it on the Ilchester and Langport road . ' 

 ' you hear , ' say our leader . ' we have not a moment to spare , or we may 
 have the whole crew about our ear . put our dead and wound in the 
 cart , and we can harness two of these charger to they . we shall not be 
 in safety until we be in Taunton town . ' 

 even Master Joshua see that the matter be too press to permit of 
 any spiritual exercise . the wounded man be lift into the waggon and 
 lay upon the bedding , while our dead be place in the cart which have 
 defend our rear . the peasant who own these , far from make any 
 objection to this disposal of their property , assist we in every way , 
 tighten girth and buckling trace . within an hour of the ending of 
 the skirmish we find ourselves pursue our way once more , and look 
 back through the twilight at the scatter black dot upon the white 
 road , where the body of the dragoon mark the scene of our victory . 




 Chapter XVI . of our come to Taunton 

 the purple shadow of evening have fall over the countryside , and the 
 sun have sink behind the distant Quantock and Brendon Hills , as our rude 
 column of rustic infantry plod through Curry Rivell , Wrantage , and 
 Henlade . at every wayside cottage and red - tile farmhouse the people 
 swarm out we we pass , with jug full of milk or beer , shake hand 
 with our yokel , and press food and drink upon they . in the little 
 village old and young come buzz to greet we , and cheer long and 
 loud for King Monmouth and the protestant cause . the stay - at - home be 
 mostly elderly folk and child , but here and there a young labourer , 
 whom hesitation or duty have keep back , be so carry away by our 
 martial appearance , and by the visible trophy of our victory , that he 
 snatch up a weapon and join our rank . 

 the skirmish have reduce our number , but it have do much to turn our 
 rabble of peasant into a real military force . the leadership of Saxon , 
 and his stern , short word of praise or of censure have do even more . 
 the man keep some sort of formation , and step together briskly in a 
 compact body . the old soldier and I ride at the head of the column , with 
 Master Pettigrue still walk between we . then come the cartful of 
 our dead , whom we be carry with we to insure their decent burial . 
 behind this walk two score of scythe and sickle man , with their rude 
 weapon over their shoulder , precede the waggon in which the wound 
 be carry . this be follow by the main body of the peasant , 
 and the rear be bring up by ten or twelve man under the command of 
 Lockarby and Sir Gervas , mount upon capture charger , and wear the 
 breastplate , sword , and carbine of the dragoon . 

 I observe that Saxon ride with his chin upon his shoulder , cast 
 continual uneasy glance behind he , and halt at every piece of 
 rise ground to make sure that there be no pursuer at our heel . it 
 be not until , after many weary mile of marching , the light of Taunton 
 could be see twinkle far off in the valley beneath we that he at last 
 heave a deep sigh of relief , and express his belief that all danger 
 be over . 

 ' I be not prone to be fearful upon small occasion , ' he remark , ' but 
 hamper as we be with wounded man and prisoner , it might have puzzle 
 Petrinus himself to know what we should have do have the cavalry 
 overtake we . I can now , Master Pettigrue , smoke my pipe in peace , 
 without prick up my ear at every chance rumble of a wheel or shout 
 of a village roisterer . ' 

 ' even have they pursue we , ' say the minister stoutly , ' as long as the 
 hand of the Lord shall shield we , why should we fear they ? ' 

 ' aye , aye ! ' Saxon answer impatiently , ' but the devil prevaileth at 
 time . be not the choose people themselves overthrow and lead into 
 captivity ? how say you , Clarke ? ' 

 ' one such skirmish be enough for a day , ' I remark . ' faith ! if instead 
 of charge we they have continue that carbine fire , we must either have 
 come forth or be shoot where we lay . ' 

 ' for that reason I forbid our friend with the musket to answer it , ' 
 say Saxon . ' our silence lead they to think that we have but a pistol or 
 two among we , and so bring they to charge we . thus our volley become 
 the more terrifying since it be unexpected . I will wager there be not a 
 man amongst they who do not feel that he have be lead into a trap . Mark 
 you how the rogue wheel and flee with one accord , as though it have 
 be part of their daily drill ! ' 

 ' the peasant stand to it like man , ' I remark . 

 ' there be nothing like a tincture of Calvinism for stiffen a line of 
 battle , ' say Saxon . ' look at the swede when he be at home . what more 
 honest , simple - hearted fellow could you find , with no single soldierly 
 virtue , save that he could put away more spruce beer than you would care 
 to pay for . yet if you do but cram he with a few strong , homely text , 
 place a pike in his hand , and give he a Gustavus to lead he , there be 
 no infantry in the world that can stand against he . on the other hand , 
 I have see young Turks , untraine to arm , strike in on behalf of the 
 Koran as lustily as these brave fellow behind we do for the Bible 
 which Master Pettigrue hold up in front of they . ' 

 ' I trust , sir , ' say the minister gravely , ' that you do not , by 
 these remark , intend to institute any comparison between our sacred 
 scripture and the writing of the impostor Mahomet , or to infer that 
 there be any similarity between the devil - inspire fury of the infidel 
 Saracens and the christian fortitude of the struggle faithful ! ' 

 ' by no mean , ' Saxon answer , grin at I over the minister 's head . 
 ' I be but show how closely the Evil One can imitate the working of 
 the Spirit . ' 

 ' too true , Master Saxon , too true ! ' the clergyman answer sadly . ' amid 
 the conflict and discord it be hard to pick out the true path . but 
 I marvel much that amidst the snare and temptation that beset a 
 soldier 's life you have keep yourself unsullied , with your heart still 
 set upon the true faith . ' 

 ' it be through no strength of mine own , ' say Saxon piously . 

 ' in very truth , such man as you be much need in Monmouth 's army , ' 
 Master Joshua exclaim . ' they have there several , as I understand , from 
 Holland , Brandenburg , and Scotland , who have be train in arm , but 
 who care so little for the cause which we uphold that they curse and 
 swear in a manner that affright the peasant , and threaten to call 
 down a judgment upon the army . other there be who cle close to the 
 true faith , and have be bear again among the righteous ; but alas ! they 
 have have no experience of camp and field . our blessed Master can work 
 by mean of weak instrument , yet the fact remain that a man may be 
 a choose light in a pulpit , and yet be of little avail in an onslaught 
 such as we have see this day . I can myself arrange my discourse to the 
 satisfaction of my flock , so that they grieve when the sand be run out ; 
 ( Note E. Appendix ) but I be aware that this power would stand I in 
 little stead when it come to the raising of barricade and the use of 
 carnal weapon . in this way it come about , in the army of the faithful , 
 that those who be fit to lead be hateful to the people , while those to 
 whose word the people will hearken know little of war . now we have this 
 day see that you be ready of head and of hand , of much experience of 
 battle , and yet of demure and sober life , full of yearning after the 
 word , and striving against Apollyon . I therefore repeat that you shall 
 be as a very Joshua amongst they , or as a Samson , destine to tear 
 down the twin pillar of Prelacy and Popery , so as to bury this corrupt 
 government in its fall . ' 

 Decimus Saxon 's only reply to this eulogy be one of those groan which 
 be suppose , among the zealot , to be the symbol of intense inner 
 conflict and emotion . so austere and holy be his expression , so solemn 
 his demeanour , and so frequent the upturning of his eye , clasp 
 of his hand , and other sign which mark the extreme sectary , that 
 I could not but marvel at the depth and completeness of the hypocrisy 
 which have cast so complete a cloak over his rapacious self . for very 
 mischief 's sake I could not refrain from remind he that there be 
 one at least who value his profession at their real value . 

 ' have you tell the worthy minister , ' say I , ' of your captivity amongst 
 the Mussulmans , and of the noble way in which you do uphold the 
 christian faith at Stamboul ? ' 

 ' Nay , ' cry our companion , ' I would fain hear the tale . I marvel much 
 that one so faithful and unbending as thyself be ever let loose by the 
 unclean and bloodthirsty follower of Mahomet . ' 

 ' it do not become I to tell the tale , ' Saxon answer with great 
 presence of mind , cast at the same time a most venomous sidelong 
 glance at I . ' it be for my comrade in misfortune and not for I to 
 describe what I endure for the faith . I have little doubt , Master 
 Pettigrue , that you would have do as much have you be there . the town 
 of Taunton lie very quiet beneath we , and there be few light for so 
 early an hour , see that it have not yet go ten . it be clear that 
 Monmouth 's force have not reach it yet , else have there be some show 
 of camp - fire in the valley ; for though it be warm enough to lie out in 
 the open , the man must have fire to cook their victual . ' 

 ' the army could scarce have come so far , ' say the pastor . ' they have , 
 I hear , be much delay by the want of arm and by the need of 
 discipline . bethink ye , it be on the eleventh day of the month that 
 Monmouth land at Lyme , and it be now but the night of the fourteenth . 
 there be much to be do in the time . ' 

 ' four whole day ! ' growl the old soldier . ' yet I expect no well , 
 see that they have , so far as I can hear , no try soldier amongst 
 they . by my sword , Tilly or Wallenstein would not have take four day 
 to come from Lyme to Taunton , though all James Stuart 's cavalry bar 
 the way . great enterprise be not push through in this halting 
 fashion . the blow should be sharp and sudden . but tell I , worthy sir , 
 all that you know about the matter , for we have hear little upon the 
 road save rumour and surmise . be there not some fashion of onfall at 
 Bridport ? ' 

 ' there be indeed some shedding of blood at that place . the first two 
 day be consume , as I understand , in the enrolling of the faithful 
 and the search for arm wherewith to equip they . you may well shake your 
 head , for the hour be precious . at last five hundred man be break 
 into some sort of order , and march along the coast under command of 
 Lord Grey of Wark and Wade the lawyer . at Bridport they be oppose 
 by the red Dorset militia and part of Portman 's yellow coat . if all 
 be true that be say , neither side have much to boast of . Grey and his 
 cavalry never tighten bridle until they be back in Lyme once more , 
 though it be say their flight have more to do with the hard mouth 
 of their horse than with the soft heart of the rider . Wade and his 
 footman do bravely , and have the good of it against the king 's troop . 
 there be much outcry against Grey in the camp , but Monmouth can 
 scarce afford to be severe upon the only nobleman who hath join his 
 standard . ' 

 ' Pshaw ! ' cry Saxon peevishly . ' there be no great stock of nobleman in 
 Cromwell 's army , I trow , and yet they hold their own against the king , 
 who have as many lord by he as there be haw in a thicket . if ye have 
 the people on your side , why should ye crave for these bewigge fine 
 gentleman , whose white hand and delicate rapier be of as much service 
 as so many lady ' bodkin ? ' 

 ' faith ! ' say I , ' if all the fop be as careless for their life as our 
 friend Sir Gervas , I could wish no well comrade in the field . ' 

 ' in good sooth , yes ! ' cry Master Pettigrue heartily . ' what though he 
 be clothe in a Joseph 's coat of many colour , and hath strange turn

 ' very little , save that the peasant have flock in in such number

 whilst this conversation have be proceed we have be walk our

 the footpath and causeway be black with throng of the townsfolk ,

 chapter XVII . of the Gathering in the market - square 

 the fair town in which we now find ourselves be , although Monmouth 
 have not yet reach it , the real centre of the rebellion . it be a 
 prosperous place , with a great woollen and kersey trade , which give 
 occupation to as many as seven thousand inhabitant . it stand high , 
 therefore , amongst english borough , be inferior only to Bristol , 
 Norwich , Bath , Exeter , York , Worcester , and Nottingham amongst the 
 country town . Taunton have long be famous not only for its own 
 resource and for the spirit of its inhabitant , but also for the 
 beautiful and highly cultivate country which spread around it , and give 
 rise to a gallant breed of yeoman . from time immemorial the town have 
 be a rallying - point for the party of liberty , and for many year it 
 have lean to the side of Republicanism in politic and of Puritanism 
 in religion . no place in the kingdom have fight more stoutly for 
 the Parliament , and though it have be twice besiege by Goring , the 
 burgher , head by the brave Robert Blake , have fight so desperately , 
 that the Royalists have be compel each time to retire discomfited . 
 on the second occasion the garrison have be reduce to dog's - flesh and 
 horse - flesh , but no word of surrender have come either from they or 
 their heroic commander , who be the same Blake under whom the old seaman 
 Solomon Sprent have fight against the Dutch . after the Restoration the 
 Privy Council have show their recollection of the part play by the 
 Somersetshire town , by issue a special order that the battlement 
 which fence round the maiden stronghold should be destroy . thus , 
 at the time of which I speak , nothing but a line of ruin and a few

 who have serve in the past , and be not averse to see the flash of 
 gun and hear the screech of shot once more . 

 Charles 's Council might destroy the battlement which his soldier have 
 be unable to take , but no royal edict could do away with the resolute

 Lunsford 's babe - eater be hurl down the main breach by the strong

 this flame be fan by the unwearied ministering of a choose band

 no city value so highly those liberty and that creed which be in

 a large body of the burgher have already set out to join the rebel army ,

 ' they have vow that none other should lead they , ' say the minister , 
 stand by Saxon 's stirrup . 

 ' I could not wish to lead stouter fellow , ' say he . ' let they deploy 
 into double line in front of the town - hall . so , so , smartly there , rear

 you . so -- as hard and as straight as an Andrea Ferrara . I prythee ,

 there be much in this magistrate 's appearance to attract attention , for

 ' friend in the faith , ' he say , ' I thank the Lord that I have be 
 spar in my old age to look down upon this goodly assembly . for we of

 a deep irrepressible hum of approval burst from the close rank of

 Chapter XVIII . of Master Stephen Timewell , Mayor of Taunton 

 within the town - hall all be bustle and turmoil . at one side behind a 
 low table cover with green baize sit two scrivener with great roll 
 of paper in front of they . a long line of citizen pass slowly before

 the town clerk stand by the table give forth the voucher for each

 ' Abraham Willis , ' he shout as we enter ; ' put he down twenty - six 
 pound and ten shilling . you shall receive ten per centum upon this

 ' a strange adventure truly , ' exclaim Saxon . ' how fare it with you for

 ' but scurvily , for Charles 's face , which be black enough at all

 Chapter XIX . of a Brawl in the Night 

 Decimus Saxon refuse to avail himself of Master Timewell 's house and 
 table for the reason , as I afterwards learn , that , the Mayor be a 
 firm Presbyterian , he think it might stand he in ill stead with the 
 Independents and other zealot be he to allow too great an intimacy 
 to spring up between they . indeed , my dear , from this time onward this 
 cunning man frame his whole life and action in such a way as to make 
 friend of the sectary , and to cause they to look upon he as their 
 leader . for he have a firm belief that in all such outbreak as that in 
 which we be engage , the most extreme party be sure in the end to gain 
 the upper hand . ' Fanatics , ' he say to I one day , ' mean fervour , and 
 fervour mean hard work , and hard work mean power . ' that be the centre 
 point of all his plotting and scheming . 

 and first of all he set himself to show how excellent a soldier he be , 
 and he spar neither time nor work to make this apparent . from morn 
 till midday , and from afternoon till night , we drill and drill until 
 in very truth the shouting of the order and the clatter of the arm 
 become wearisome to our ear . the good burgher may well have 
 think that Colonel Saxon 's Wiltshire foot be as much part of the 
 market - place as the town cross or the parish stock . there be much to 
 be do in very little time , so much that many would have think it 
 hopeless to attempt it . not only be there the general muster of the 
 regiment , but we have each to practise our own company in their several 
 drill , and to learn as good we could the name and the want of the 
 man . yet our work be make easy to we by the assurance that it be not 
 throw away , for at every gathering our bumpkin stand more erect , 
 and handle their weapon more deftly . from cock - crow to sun - down the 
 street resound with ' poise your musket ! order your musket ! rest 
 your musket ! handle your primer ! ' and all the other order of the old 
 manual exercise . 

 as we become more soldierly we increase in number , for our smart 
 appearance draw the pick of the new - comer into our rank . my own 
 company swell until it have to be divide , and other enlarge in 
 proportion . the baronet 's musqueteer muster a full hundred , skilled 
 for the most part in the use of the gun . altogether we spring from 
 three hundred to four hundred and fifty , and our drill improve until we 
 receive praise from all side on the state of our man . 

 late in the evening I be rid slowly back to the house of Master 
 Timewell when Reuben clatter after I , and besought I to turn back 
 with he to see a noteworthy sight . though feel little in the mood 
 for such thing , I turn Covenant and ride with he down the length of 
 High Street , and into the suburb which be know as Shuttern , where my 
 companion pull up at a bare barn - like building , and bid I look in 
 through the window . 

 the interior , which consist of a single great hall , the empty 
 warehouse in which wool have use to be store , be all alight with lamp 
 and candle . a great throng of man , whom I recognise as belong to 
 my own company , or that of my companion , lie about on either side , some 
 smoking , some pray , and some burnish their arm . down the middle a 
 line of bench have be draw up , on which there be seat astraddle 
 the whole hundred of the baronet 's musqueteer , each engage in plait 
 into a queue the hair of the man who sit in front of he . a boy walk 
 up and down with a pot of grease , by the aid of which with some whipcord 
 the work be go forward merrily . Sir Gervas himself with a great 
 flour dredger sit perch upon a bale of wool at the head of the line , 
 and as quickly as any queue be finish he examine it through his 
 quizzing glass , and if it find favour in his eye , daintily powder it 
 from his dredger , with as much care and reverence as though it be some 
 service of the Church . no cook season a dish could have add 
 his spice with more nicety of judgment than our friend display in 
 whiten the pate of his company . glance up from his labour he see 
 our two smile face look in at he through the window , but his work 
 be too engross to allow he to leave it , and we ride off at last 
 without have speech with he . 

 by this time the town be very quiet and still , for the folk in those 
 part be early bed - goer , save when some special occasion keep they 
 afoot . we ride slowly together through the silent street , our horse ' 
 hoof ring out sharp against the cobble stone , talk about such 
 light matter as engage the mind of youth . the moon be shin very 
 brightly above we , silver the broad street , and cast a fretwork 
 of shadow from the peak and pinnacle of the church . at Master 
 Timewell 's courtyard I spring from my saddle , but Reuben , attract by 
 the peace and beauty of the scene , ride onwards with the intention of 
 go as far as the town gate . 

 I be still at work upon my girth buckle , undo my harness , when of 
 a sudden there come from the street a shouting and a rushing , with the 
 clinking of blade , and my comrade 's voice call upon I for help . 
 draw my sword I run out . some little way down there be a clear 
 space , white with the moonshine , in the centre of which I catch a 
 glimpse of the sturdy figure of my friend spring about with an 
 activity for which I have never give he credit , and exchange sword 
 thrust with three or four man who be press he closely . on the 
 ground there lie a dark figure , and behind the struggle group Reuben 's 
 mare rear and plunge in sympathy with her master 's peril . as I rush 
 down , shout and wave my sword , the assailant take flight down 
 a side street , save one , a tall sinewy swordsman , who rush in upon 
 Reuben , stab furiously at he , and curse he the while for a 
 spoil - sport . to my horror I see , as I run , the fellow 's blade slip 
 inside my friend 's guard , who throw up his arm and fall prostrate , 
 while the other with a final thrust dash off down one of the narrow 
 winding lane which lead from East Street to the bank of the Tone . 

 ' for Heaven 's sake where be you hurt ? ' I cry , throw myself upon my 
 knee beside his prostrate body . ' where be your injury , Reuben ? ' 

 ' in the wind , mostly , ' quoth he , blow like a smithy bellow ; 
 ' likewise on the back of my pate . give I your hand , I pray . ' 

 ' and be you indeed scathless ? ' I cry , with a great lightening of 
 the heart as I help he to his foot . ' I think that the villain have 
 stab you . ' 

 ' as well stab a Warsash crab with a bodkin , ' say he . ' thank to good 
 Sir Jacob Clancing , once of Snellaby Hall and now of Salisbury Plain , 
 their rapier do no more than scratch my plate of proof . but how be it 
 with the maid ? ' 

 ' the maid ? ' say I. 

 ' aye , it be to save she that I draw . she be beset by these night 
 walker . see , she rise ! they throw she down when I set upon they . '

 ' good lack ! it be Mistress Timewell ! ' I cry , in amazement .

 alarm , and there will be a rabble collect anon . let we escape from

 Windows have indeed begin to clatter up in every direction , and loud

 ' I trust , sir , that you have really meet with no hurt , ' say the maiden

 ' Nay , nay , ' say she , with uplifted finger , ' you shall not follow the

 State , and who strike at he through I . but ye have both be so kind

 we protest that we could not , with our hand upon our sword - hilt .
