 action . ' all that I ask be that ye will say nothing if this matter to my 
 grandsire . he be choleric , and a little matter doth set he in a flame , 
 so old as he be . I would not have his mind turn from the public need 
 to a private trifle of this sort . have I your promise ? ' 

 ' mine , ' say I , bow . 

 ' and mine , ' say Lockarby . 

 ' thank , good friend . alack ! I have drop my gauntlet in the street . 
 but it be of no import . I thank God that no harm have come to any one . my 
 thank once more , and may pleasant dream await ye . ' she spring up the 
 step and be go in an instant . 

 Reuben and I unharness our horse and see they care for in silence . 
 we then enter the house and ascend to our chamber , still without a 
 word . outside his room door my friend pause . 

 ' I have hear that long man 's voice before , Micah , ' say he . 

 ' and so have I , ' I answer . ' the old man must beware of his ' prentice . 
 I have half a mind to go back for the little maiden 's gauntlet . ' 

 a merry twinkle shoot through the cloud which hide gather on Reuben 's 
 brow . he open his left hand and show I the doe - skin glove crumple 
 up in his palm . 

 ' I would not barter it for all the gold in her grandsire 's coffer , ' 
 say he , with a sudden outflame , and then half - laugh , half - blushing 
 at his own heat , he whisk in and leave I to my thought . 

 and so I learn for the first time , my dear , that my good comrade have 
 be strike by the little god 's arrow . when a man 's year number one 
 score , love spring up in he , as the gourd grow in the scripture , in a 
 single night . I have tell my story ill if I have not make you understand 
 that my friend be a frank , warm - hearted lad of impulse , whose reason 
 seldom stand sentry over his inclination . such a man can no more draw 
 away from a winning maid than the needle can shun the magnet . he love 
 as the mavis sing or the kitten play . now , a slow - witte , heavy fellow 
 like myself , in whose vein the blood have always flow somewhat coolly 
 and temperately , may go into love as a horse go into a shelving 
 stream , step by step , but a man like Reuben be kick his heel upon 
 the bank one moment , and be over ear in the deep pool the nest . 

 Heaven only know what match it be that have set the tow alight . I can 
 but say that from that day on my comrade be sad and cloudy one hour , 
 gay and blithesome the next . his even flow of good spirit have desert 
 he , and he become as dismal as a moulting chicken , which have ever 
 seem to I to be one of the strange outcome of what poet have 
 call the joyous state of love . but , indeed , pain and pleasure be so 
 very nearly akin in this world , that it be as if they be tether 
 in neighbouring stall , and a kick would at any time bring down the 
 partition . here be a man who be as full of sigh as a grenade be of 
 powder , his face be sad , his brow be downcast , his wit be wander ; 
 yet if you remark to he that it be an ill thing that he should be 
 in this state , he will answer you , as like as not , that he would not 
 exchange it for all the power and principality . tear to he be 
 golden , and laughter be but base coin . well , my dear , it be useless 
 for I to expound to you that which I can not myself understand . if , as I 
 have hear , it be impossible to get the thumb - mark of any two man to 
 be alike , how can we expect their inmost thought and feeling to tally ? 
 yet this I can say with all truth , that when I ask your grandmother 's 
 hand I do not demean myself as if I be chief mourner at a funeral . 
 she will bear I out that I walk up to she with a smile upon my face , 
 though mayhap there be a little flutter at my heart , and I take she 
 hand and I say -- but , lack - a - day , whither have I wander ? what have all 
 this to do with Taunton town and the rise of 1685 ? 

 on the night of Wednesday , June 17 , we learn that the king , as 
 Monmouth be call throughout the West , be lie less than ten mile 
 off with his force , and that he would make his entry into the loyal 
 town of Taunton the next morning . every effort be make , as ye may well 
 guess , to give he a welcome which should be worthy of the most whiggish 
 and protestant town in England . an arch of evergreen have already 
 be build up at the western gate , bear the motto , ' welcome to King 
 Monmouth ! ' and another span the entrance to the market - place from the 
 upper window of the White Hart Inn , with ' hail to the Protestant Chief ! ' 
 in great scarlet letter . a third , if I remember right , bridge the 
 entrance to the Castle yard , but the motto on it have escape I . the 
 cloth and wool industry be , as I have tell you , the staple trade of 
 the town , and the merchant have no mercy on their ware , but use they 
 freely to beautify the street . rich tapestry , glossy velvet , and 
 costly brocade flutter from the window or line the balcony . East 
 Street , High Street , and Fore Street be drape from garret to basement 
 with rare and beautiful fabric , while gay flag hang from the roof 
 on either side , or flutter in long festoon from house to house . 
 the royal banner of England float from the lofty tower of St. Mary 
 Magdalene , while the blue ensign of Monmouth wave from the sister 
 turret of St. James . late into the night there be plan and 
 hammering , work and devising , until when the sun rise upon Thursday , 
 June 18 , it shine on as brave a show of bunting and evergreen as ever 
 grace a town . Taunton have change as by magic from a city into a flower

 Master Stephen Timewell have busy himself in these preparation , but he 
 have bear in mind at the same time that the most welcome sight which 
 he could present to Monmouth 's eye be the large body of armed man who 
 be prepared to follow his fortune . there be sixteen hundred in the

 these be dispose in such a way that the King should pass they in his

 dorsetshire , and Frome peasant be draw up on either side of the

 ' my order be , ' say Saxon , rid up to we as we sit our horse reside 
 our companion , ' that I and my captain should fall in with the King 's 
 escort as he pass , and so accompany he to the market - place . your man 
 shall present arm , and shall then stand their ground until we return . ' 

 we all three draw our sword and salute . 

 ' if ye will come with I , gentleman , and take position to the right of 
 the gate here , ' say he , ' I may be able to tell ye something of these 
 folk as they pass . thirty year of war in many clime should give I the 
 master craftsman 's right to expound to his apprentice . ' 

 we all very gladly follow his advice , and pass out through the gate , 
 which be now nothing more than a broad gap amongst the mound which 
 mark the line of the old wall . ' there be no sign of they yet , ' I 
 remark , as we pull up upon a convenient hillock . ' I suppose that 
 they must come by this road which wind through the valley before we . ' 

 ' there be two sort of bad general , ' quoth Saxon , ' the man who be too 
 fast and the man who be too slow . his Majesty 's adviser will never be 
 accuse of the former failing , whatever other mistake they may fall 
 into . there be old Marshal Grunberg , with whom I do twenty - six month ' 
 soldiering in Bohemia . he would fly through the country pell - mell , 
 horse , foot , and artillery , as if the devil be at his heel . he might 
 make fifty blunder , but the enemy have never time to take advantage . I 
 call to mind a raid which we make into Silesia , when , after two day or 
 so of mountain road , his Oberhauptmann of the staff tell he that it 
 be impossible for the artillery to keep up . " lass es hinter ! " say he . 
 so the gun be leave , and by the evening of the next day the foot be 
 dead - beat . " they can not walk another mile ! " say the Oberhauptmann . 
 " Lassen Sie hinter ! " say he . so on we go with the horse -- I be in his 
 Pandour regiment , bad luck ! but after a skirmish or two , what with the 
 road and what with the enemy , our horse be foundered and useless . 
 " the horse be use up ! " say the Oberhauptmann . " Lassen Sie hinter ! " 
 he cry ; and I warrant that he would have push on to Prague with his

 that . ' 

 ' a dash commander , too , ' cry Sir Gervas . ' I would fain have serve 
 under he . ' 

 ' Aye , and he have a way of knock his recruit into shape which would 
 scarce be relish by our good friend here in the west country , ' say 
 Saxon . ' I remember that after the leaguer of Salzburg , when we have take

 upon the wall at they , kill three score and strike great panic 
 into the other . " the rogue must get use to stand fire soon 
 or later , " say he , " so they may as well commence their education at 
 once . " ' 

 ' he be a rough schoolmaster , ' I remark . ' he might have leave that part 
 of the drill to the enemy . ' 

 ' yet his soldier love he , ' say Saxon . ' he be not a man , when a city 
 have be force , to inquire into every squawk of a woman , or give ear to 
 every burgess who chance to find his strong - box a trifle the light . 
 but as to the slow commander , I have know none to equal Brigadier 
 Baumgarten , also of the imperial service . he would break up his 
 winter - quarter and sit down before some place of strength , where he 
 would raise a sconce here , and sink a sap there , until his soldier be 
 sick of the very sight of the place . so he would play with it , as a cat

 as like as not , he would raise the leaguer and march back into his 
 winter - quarter . I serve two campaign under he without honour , sack , 
 plunder , or emolument , save a beggarly stipend of three gulden a day , 
 pay in clipped money , six month in arrear . but mark ye the folk upon 
 yonder tower ! they be wave their kerchief as though something be 
 visible to they . ' 

 ' I can see nothing , ' I answer , shade my eye and gaze down the 
 tree - sprinkle valley which rise slowly in green upland to the grassy 
 Blackdown hill . 

 ' those on the housetop be wave and point , ' say Reuben . ' methink 
 I can myself see the flash of steel among yonder wood . ' 

 ' there it be , ' cry Saxon , extend his gauntlete hand , ' on the 
 western bank of the Tone , hard by the wooden bridge . follow my finger , 
 Clarke , and see if you can not distinguish it . ' 

 ' yes , truly , ' I exclaim , ' I see a bright shimmer come and go . and 
 there to the left , where the road curve over the hill , mark you that 
 dense mass of man ! ha ! the head of the column begin to emerge from the 
 tree . ' 

 there be not a cloud in the sky , but the great heat have cause a haze 
 to overlie the valley , gather thickly along the winding course of 
 the river , and hang in little spray and feather over the woodland 
 which clothe its bank . through this filmy vapour there break from time 
 to time fierce sparkle of brilliant light as the sun 's ray fall upon 
 breastplate or headpiece . now and again the gentle summer breeze 
 waft up sudden pulse of martial music to our ear , with the blare of 
 trumpet and the long deep snarl of the drum . as we gaze , the van of 
 the army begin to roll out from the cover of the tree and to darken the 
 white dusty road . the long line slowly extend itself , writhe out of 
 the forest land like a dark snake with sparkling scale , until the whole 
 rebel army -- horse , foot , and ordnance -- be visible beneath we . the 
 gleam of the weapon , the waving of numerous banner , the plume of the 
 leader , and the deep column of march man , make up a picture which 
 stir the very heart of the citizen , who , from the housetop and 
 from the ruinous summit of the dismantled wall , be enable to gaze 
 down upon the champion of their faith . if the mere sight of a pass 
 regiment will cause a thrill in your bosom , you can fancy how it be 
 when the soldier upon whom you look be in actual arm for your own 
 dearest and most cherished interest , and have just come out victorious 
 from a bloody struggle . if every other man 's hand be against we , these 
 at least be on our side , and our heart go out to they as to friend 
 and brother . of all the tie that unite man in this world , that of a 
 common danger be the strong . 

 it all appear to be most warlike and most impose to my inexperienced 
 eye , and I think as I look at the long array that our cause be as 
 good as win . to my surprise , however , Saxon pishe and pshawe under his 
 breath , until at last , unable to contain his impatience , he break out in 
 hot discontent . 

 ' do but look at that vanguard as they breast the slope , ' he cry . 
 ' where be the advance party , or Vorreiter , as the Germans call they ? 
 where , too , be the space which should be leave between the fore - guard and 
 the main battle ? by the sword of Scanderbeg , they remind I more of a 
 drove of pilgrim , as I have see they approach the shrine of St. 
 Sebaldus of Nurnberg with their banner and streamer . there in 
 the centre , amid that cavalcade of cavalier , rid our new monarch 
 doubtless . pity he hath not a man by he who can put this swarm of 
 peasant into something like campaign order . now do but look at those 
 four piece of ordnance trail along like lame sheep behind the flock . 
 Caracco , I would that I be a young king 's officer with a troop of 
 light horse on the ridge yonder ! my faith , how I should sweep down yon 
 cross road like a kestrel on a brood of young plover ! then heh for cut 
 and thrust , down with the skulk cannonier , a carbine fire to cover 
 we , round with the horse , and away go the rebel gun in a cloud of 
 dust ! how be that , Sir Gervas ? ' 

 ' good sport , Colonel , ' say the baronet , with a touch of colour in his 
 white cheek . ' I warrant that you do keep your pandour on the trot . ' 

 ' Aye , the rogue have to work or hang -- one or t'other . but methink our 
 friend here be scarce as numerous as report . I reckon they to be a 
 thousand horse , and mayhap five thousand two hundred foot . I have be 
 think a good tally - man on such occasion . with fifteen hundred in the 
 town that would bring we to close on eight thousand man , which be no 
 great force to invade a kingdom and dispute a crown . ' 

 ' if the West can give eight thousand , how many can all the county of 
 England afford ? ' I ask . ' be not that the fair way to look at it ? ' 

 ' Monmouth 's popularity lie mostly in the West , ' Saxon answer . ' it 
 be the memory of that which prompt he to raise his standard in these 
 county . ' 

 ' his standard , rather , ' quoth Reuben . ' why , it look as though they have 
 hang their linen up to dry all down the line . ' 

 ' true ! they have more ensign than ever I see with so small a force , ' 
 Saxon answer , rise in his stirrup . ' one or two be blue , and the 
 rest , as far as I can see for the sun shin upon they , be white , with 
 some motto or device . ' 

 whilst we have be converse , the body of horse which form the 
 vanguard of the protestant army have approach within a quarter of a 
 mile or less of the town , when a loud , clear bugle - call bring they to 
 a halt . in each successive regiment or squadron the signal be repeat , 
 so that the sound pass swiftly down the long array until it die away 
 in the distance . as the coil of man form up upon the white road , with

 its likeness to a giant serpent occur again to my mind . 

 ' I could fancy it a great boa , ' I remark , ' which be draw its coil 
 round the town . ' 

 ' a rattlesnake , rather , ' say Reuben , point to the gun in the rear . 
 ' it keep all its noise in its tail . ' 

 ' here come its head , if I mistake not , ' quoth Saxon . ' it be good 
 perhaps that we stand at the side of the gate . ' 

 as he speak a group of gaily dress cavalier break away from the main 
 body and ride straight for the town . their leader be a tall , slim , 
 elegant young man , who sit his horse with the grace of a skilled rider , 
 and who be remarkable amongst those around he for the gallantry of his

 gate a roar of welcome burst from the assemble multitude , which be 
 take up and prolong by the crowd behind , who , though unable to see 
 what be go forward , gather from the shouting that the King be 
 approach . 



 Chapter XX . of the Muster of the Men of the West 

 Monmouth be at that time in his thirty - sixth year , and be remarkable 
 for those superficial grace which please the multitude and fit a man to 
 lead in a popular cause . he be young , well - speak , witty , and skille 
 in all martial and manly exercise . on his progress in the West he have 
 not think it beneath he to kiss the village maiden , to offer prize 
 at the rural sport , and to run race in his boot against the fleet 
 of the barefooted countryman . ( note G. , Appendix ) his nature be vain 
 and prodigal , but he excel in that showy magnificence and careless 
 generosity which win the heart of the people . both on the continent 
 and at Bothwell Bridge , in Scotland , he have lead army with success , and 
 his kindness and mercy to the Covenanters after his victory have cause 
 he to be as much esteem amongst the whig as Dalzell and Claverhouse 
 be hat . as he rein up his beautiful black horse at the gate of the 
 city , and raise his plume montero cap to the shouting crowd , the grace 
 and dignity of his bearing be such as might befit the knight - errant 
 in a Romance who be fight at long odd for a crown which a tyrant have 
 filch from he . 

 he be reckon well - favour , but I can not say that I find he so . his 
 face be , I think , too long and white for comeliness , yet his feature 
 be high and noble , with well - mark nose and clear , search eye . in 
 his mouth might perchance be notice some trace of that weakness which 
 mar his character , though the expression be sweet and amiable . he 
 wear a dark purple roquelaure riding - jacket , face and lapelle with 
 gold lace , through the open front of which shine a silver breastplate . 
 a velvet suit of a light shade than the jacket , a pair of high yellow 
 Cordovan boot , with a gold - hilte rapier on one side , and a poniard 
 of Parma on the other , each hang from the morocco - leather sword - belt , 
 complete his attire . a broad collar of Mechlin lace flow over his 
 shoulder , while wristband of the same costly material dangle from his 
 sleeve . again and again he raise his cap and bent to the saddle - bow in 
 response to the storm of cheer . ' a Monmouth ! a Monmouth ! ' cry 
 the people ; ' hail to the protestant chief ! ' ' long live the noble King 
 Monmouth ! ' while from every window , and roof , and balcony flutter 
 kerchief or wave hat brighten the joyous scene . the rebel van catch 
 fire at the sight and raise a great deep - cheste shout , which be take 
 up again and again by the rest of the army , until the whole countryside 
 be sonorous . 

 in the meanwhile the city elder , head by our friend the Mayor , 
 advanced from the gate in all the dignity of silk and fur to pay homage 
 to the king . sink upon one knee by Monmouth 's stirrup , he kiss the 
 hand which be graciously extend to he . 

 ' nay , good Master Mayor , ' say the King , in a clear , strong voice , ' it 
 be for my enemy to sink before I , and not for my friend . Prythee , 
 what be this scroll which you do unroll ? ' 

 ' it be an address of welcome and of allegiance , your Majesty , from your 
 loyal town of Taunton . ' 

 ' I need no such address , ' say King Monmouth , look round . ' it be 
 write all around I in fairer character than ever find themselves 
 upon parchment . my good friend have make I feel that I be welcome 
 without the aid of clerk or scrivener . your name , good Master Mayor , be 
 Stephen Timewell , as I understand ? ' 

 ' the same , your Majesty . ' 

 ' too curt a name for so trusty a man , ' say the King , draw his sword 
 and touch he upon the shoulder with it . ' I shall make it long by 
 three letter . rise up , Sir Stephen , and may I find that there be many 
 other knight in my dominion as loyal and as stout . ' 

 amidst the huzzah which break out afresh at this honour do to the 
 town , the Mayor withdraw with the councilman to the left side of the 
 gate , whilst Monmouth with his staff gather upon the right . at a 
 signal a trumpeter blow a fanfare , the drum strike up a point of war , 
 and the insurgent army , with serried rank and wave banner , resume 
 its advance upon the town . as it approach , Saxon point out to we the 
 various leader and man of note who surround the king , give we their 
 name and some few word as to their character . 

 ' that be Lord Grey of Wark , ' say he ; ' the little middle - aged lean man 
 at the King 's bridle arm . he hath be in the Tower once for treason . 
 ' twas he who flee with the Lady Henrietta Berkeley , his wife 's sister . a 
 fine leader truly for a godly cause ! the man upon his left , with the 
 red swollen face and the white feather in his cap , be Colonel Holmes . 
 I trust that he will never show the white feather save on his head . the 
 other upon the high chestnut horse be a lawyer , though , by my soul , he 
 be a well man at order a battalion than at draw a bill of cost . 
 he be the republican Wade who lead the foot at the skirmish at Bridport , 
 and bring they off with safety . the tall heavy - faced soldier in the 
 steel bonnet be Anthony Buyse , the Brandenburger , a soldado of fortune , 
 and a man of high heart , as be most of his countryman . I have fight 
 both with he and against he ere now . ' 

 ' Mark ye the long thin man behind he ? ' cry Reuben . ' he hath draw his 
 sword , and wave it over his head . ' tis a strange time and place for the 
 broadsword exercise . he be surely mad . ' 

 ' perhaps you be not far amiss , ' say Saxon . ' yet , by my hilt , be it 
 not for that man there would be no protestant army advance upon we 
 down yonder road . ' tis he who by dangle the crown before Monmouth 's 
 eye beguile he away from his snug retreat in Brabant . there be not 
 one of these man whom he hath not tempt into this affair by some bait 
 or other . with Grey it be a dukedom , with Wade the woolsack , with Buyse 
 the plunder of Cheapside . every one hath his own motive , but the clue 
 to they all be in the hand of yonder crazy fanatic , who make the 
 puppet dance as he will . he hath plot more , lie more , and suffer 
 less than any Whig in the party . ' 

 ' it must be that Dr. Robert Ferguson of whom I have hear my father 
 speak , ' say I. 

 ' you be right . ' tis he . I have but see he once in Amsterdam , and yet 
 I know he by his shock wig and crooked shoulder . it be whisper 
 that of late his overweene conceit hath unseat his reason . see , the 
 german place his hand upon his shoulder and persuade he to sheathe 
 his weapon . King Monmouth glance round too , and smile as though he 
 be the Court buffoon with a Geneva cloak instead of the motley . but 
 the van be upon we . to your company , and mind that ye raise your 
 sword to the salute while the colour of each troop go by . ' 

 whilst our companion have be talk , the whole protestant army have 
 be stream towards the town , and the head of the fore - guard be 
 abreast with the gateway . four troop of horse lead the way , badly 
 equip and mount , with rope instead of bridle , and in some case 
 square of sack in place of saddle . the man be armed for the most 
 part with sword and pistol , while a few have the buff - coat , plate , and 
 headpiece take at Axminster , still stain sometimes with the blood of 
 the last wearer . in the midst of they ride a banner - bearer , who carry 
 a great square ensign hang upon a pole , which be support upon a 
 socket let into the side of the girth . upon it be print in golden 
 letter the legend , ' Pro libertate et religione nostra . ' these 
 horse - soldier be make up of yeoman 's and farmer ' son , unused to 
 discipline , and have a high regard for themselves as volunteer , which 
 cause they to cavil and argue over every order . for this cause , though 
 not want in natural courage , they do little service during the war , 
 and be a hindrance rather than a help to the army . 

 behind the horse come the foot , walk six abreast , divide into 
 company of vary size , each company bear a banner which give the 
 name of the town or village from which it have be raise . this manner 
 of arrange the troop have be choose because it have be find to be 
 impossible to separate man who be akin and neighbour to each other . 
 they would fight , they say , side by side , or they would not fight at 
 all . for my own part , I think that it be no bad plan , for when it come 
 to push of pike , a man stand all the fast when he know that he 
 hath old and try friend on either side of he . many of these country 
 place I come to know afterwards from the talk of the man , and many 
 other I have travel through , so that the name upon the banner have 
 come to have a real meaning with I . Homer hath , I remember , a chapter 
 or book wherein he record the name of all the grecian chief and 
 whence they come , and how many man they bring to the common muster . it 
 be pity that there be not some Western Homer who could record the name 
 of these brave peasant and artisan , and recount what each do or 
 suffer in uphold a noble though disastrous cause . their place of 
 birth at least shall not be lose as far as mine own feeble memory can 
 carry I . 

 the first foot regiment , if so rudely form a band could be so call , 
 consist of man of the sea , fisher and coastman , clothe in the 
 heavy blue jerkin and rude garb of their class . they be bronze , 
 weather - beat tarpaulin , with hard mahogany face , variously arm 
 with birding piece , cutlass , or pistol . I have a notion that it 
 be not the first time that those weapon have be turn against 
 King James 's servant , for the Somerset and Devon coast be famous 
 breeding - place for smuggler , and many a saucy lugger be doubtless 
 lie up in creek or in bay whilst her crew have go a - soldiering to 
 Taunton . as to discipline , they have no notion of it , but roll along in 
 true blue - water style , with many a shout and halloo to each other or to 
 the crowd . from Star Point to Portland Roads there would be few net 
 for many week to come , and fish would swim the narrow sea which should 
 have be heap on Lyme Cobb or expose for sale in Plymouth market . 
 each group , or band , of these man of the sea bore with it its own 
 banner , that of Lyme in the front , follow by Topsham , Colyford , 
 Bridport , Sidmouth , Otterton , Abbotsbury , and Charmouth , all southern 
 town , which be on or near the coast . so they troop past we , rough 
 and careless , with cap cock , and the reek of their tobacco rise 
 up from they like the steam from a tired horse . in number they may have 
 be four hundred or thereabout . 

 the peasant of Rockbere , with flail and scythe , lead the next column , 
 follow by the banner of Honiton , which be support by two hundred 
 stout lacemaker from the bank of the Otter . these man show by the 
 colour of their face that their work keep they within four wall , yet 
 they excel their peasant companion in their alert and soldierly 
 bear . indeed , with all the troop , we observe that , though the 
 countryman be the stouter and hearty , the craftsman be the most 
 ready to catch the air and spirit of the camp . behind the man of Honiton 
 come the Puritan clothworker of Wellington , with their mayor upon 
 a white horse beside their standard - bearer , and a band of twenty 
 instrument before he . Grim - visage , thoughtful , sober man , they be 
 for the most part clothe in grey suit and wear broad - brim hat . 
 ' for God and faith ' be the motto of a streamer which float from 
 amongst they . the clothworker form three strong company , and the 
 whole regiment may have number close on six hundred man . 

 the third regiment be head by five hundred foot from Taunton , man 
 of peaceful and industrious life , but deeply imbue with those great 
 principle of civil and religious liberty which be three year later 
 to carry all before they in England . as they pass the gate they be 
 greet by a thunderous welcome from their townsman upon the wall and 
 at the window . their steady , solid rank , and broad , honest burgher 
 face , seem to I to smack of discipline and of work well do . behind 
 they come the muster of Winterbourne , Ilminster , Chard , Yeovil , and 
 Collumpton , a hundred or more pikesman to each , bring the tally of 
 the regiment to a thousand man . 

 a squadron of horse trot by , closely follow by the fourth regiment , 
 bear in its van the standard of Beaminster , Crewkerne , Langport , 
 and Chidiock , all quiet Somersetshire village , which have send out their 
 manhood to strike a blow for the old cause . puritan minister , with 
 their steeple hat and Geneva gown , once black , but now white with 
 dust , march sturdily along beside their flock . then come a strong 
 company of wild half - arm shepherd from the great plain which extend 
 from the Blackdowns on the south to the Mendips on the north -- very 
 different fellow , I promise you , from the Corydons and Strephons of 
 Master Waller or Master Dryden , who have depict the shepherd as ever 
 shed tear of love , and tootle upon a plaintive pipe . I fear that 
 Chloe or Phyllis would have meet with rough wooing at the hand of these 
 western savage . behind they be musqueteer from Dorchester , pikemen 
 from Newton Poppleford , and a body of stout infantry from among the 
 serge worker of Ottery St. Mary . this fourth regiment number rather 
 well than eight hundred , but be inferior in arm and in discipline to 
 that which precede it . 

 the fifth regiment be head by a column of fen man from the dreary 
 march which stretch round Athelney . these man , in their sad and sordid 
 dwelling , have retain the same free and bold spirit which have make 
 they in past day the last resource of the good King Alfred and the 
 protector of the western shire from the inroad of the Danes , who 
 be never able to force their way into their watery stronghold . two 
 company of they , towsy - head and bare - legged , but loud in hymn and 
 prayer , have come out from their fastness to help the protestant cause . 
 at their heel come the woodman and lumberer of Bishop 's Lidiard , big , 
 sturdy man in green jerkin , and the white - smock villager of Huish 
 Champflower . the rear of the regiment be form by four hundred man in 
 scarlet coat , with white cross - belt and well - burnish musket . 
 these be deserter from the Devonshire Militia , who have march with 
 Albemarle from Exeter , and who have come over to Monmouth on the field 
 at Axminster . these keep together in a body , but there be many other 
 militiaman , both in red and in yellow coat , amongst the various body 
 which I have set forth . this regiment may have number seven hundred 
 man . 

 the sixth and last column of foot be head by a body of peasant 
 bear ' Minehead ' upon their banner , and the ensign of the three 
 wool - bale and the sailing ship , which be the sign of that ancient 
 borough . they have come for the most part from the wild country which 
 lie to the north of Dunster Castle and skirt the shore of the Bristol 
 Channel . behind they be the poacher and huntsman of Porlock Quay , who 
 have leave the red deer of Exmoor to graze in peace whilst they follow 
 a nobler quarry . they be follow by man from Dulverton , man from 
 Milverton , man from Wiveliscombe and the sunny slope of the Quantocks , 
 swart , fierce man from the bleak moor of Dunkerry Beacon , and tall , 
 stalwart pony rearer and grazier from Bampton . the banner of 
 Bridgewater , of Shepton Mallet , and of Nether Stowey sweep past we , with 
 that of the fisher of Clovelly and the quarryman of the Blackdowns . in 
 the rear be three company of strange man , giant in stature , though 
 somewhat bow with labour , with long tangle beard , and unkempt hair 
 hang over their eye . these be the miner from the Mendip hill and 
 from the Oare and Bagworthy valley , rough , half - savage man , whose eye 
 roll up at the velvet and brocade of the shout citizen , or fix 
 themselves upon their smile dame with a fierce intensity which scar 
 the peaceful burgher . so the long line roll in until three squadron 
 of horse and four small cannon , with the blue - coat dutch cannonier as 
 stiff as their own ramrod , bring up the rear . a long train of cart 
 and of waggon which have follow the army be lead into the field 
 outside the wall and there quarter . 

 when the last soldier have pass through the Shuttern Gate , Monmouth and 
 his leader ride slowly in , the Mayor walk by the King 's charger . 
 as we salute they all face round to we , and I see a quick flush of 
 surprise and pleasure come over Monmouth 's pale face as he note our 
 close line and soldierly bear . 

 ' by my faith , gentleman , ' he say , glance round at his staff , ' our 
 worthy friend the Mayor must have inherit Cadmus 's dragon tooth . where 
 raise ye this pretty crop , Sir Stephen ? how come ye to bring they 
 to such perfection too , even , I declare , to the hair powder of the 
 grenadier ? ' 

 ' I have fifteen hundred in the town , ' the old wool - worker answer 
 proudly ; ' though some be scarce as discipline . 

 these man come from Wiltshire , and the officer from Hampshire . as to 
 their order , the credit be due not to I , but to the old soldier Colonel 
 Decimus Saxon , whom they have choose as their commander , as well as to 
 the captain who serve under he . ' 

 ' my thank be due to you , Colonel , ' say the King , turn to Saxon , 
 who bow and sink the point of his sword to the earth , ' and to you 
 also , gentleman . I shall not forget the warm loyalty which bring you 
 from Hampshire in so short a time . would that I could find the same 
 virtue in high place ! but , Colonel Saxon , you have , I gather , see 
 much service abroad . what think you of the army which hath just pass 
 before you ? ' 

 ' if it please your Majesty , ' Saxon answer , ' it be like so much 
 uncarde wool , which be rough enough in itself , and yet may in time come 
 to be weave into a noble garment . ' 

 ' Hem ! there be not much leisure for the weaving , ' say Monmouth . ' but 
 they fight well . you should have see they fall on at Axminster ! we hope 
 to see you and to hear your view at the council table . but how be this ? 
 have I not see this gentleman 's face before ? ' 

 ' it be the Honourable Sir Gervas Jerome of the county of Surrey , ' quoth 
 Saxon . 

 ' your Majesty may have see I at St. James 's , ' say the baronet , 
 raise his hat , ' or in the balcony at Whitehall . I be much at Court 
 during the latter year of the late king . ' 

 ' yes , yes . I remember the name as well as the face , ' cry Monmouth . 
 ' you see , gentleman , ' he continue , turn to his staff , ' the courtier 
 begin to come in at last . be you not the man who do fight Sir Thomas 
 Killigrew behind Dunkirk House ? I think as much . will you not attach 
 yourself to my personal attendant ? ' 

 ' if it please your Majesty , ' Sir Gervas answer , ' I be of opinion 
 that I could do your royal cause well service at the head of my 
 musqueteer . ' 

 ' so be it ! so be it ! ' say King Monmouth . set spur to his horse , he 
 raise his hat in response to the cheer of the troop and canter down

 window upon he , his staff , and his escort . we have join in his train , 
 as command , so that we come in for our share of this merry crossfire . 
 one rise as it flutter down be catch by Reuben , who , I observe ,

 glance up , I catch sight , of the smile face of our host 's daughter 
 peep down at we from a casement . 

 ' well catch , Reuben ! ' I whisper . ' at trick - track or trap and ball you 
 be ever our good player . ' 

 ' ah , Micah , ' say he , ' I bless the day that ever I follow you to the 
 war . I would not change place with Monmouth this day . ' 

 ' has it go so far then ! ' I exclaim . ' why , lad , I think that you 
 be but open your trench , and you speak as though you have carry 
 the city . ' 

 ' perhaps I be over - hopeful , ' he cry , turn from hot to cold , as a 
 man doth when he be in love , or hath the tertian ague , or other bodily 
 trouble . ' God know that I be little worthy of she , and yet-- ' 

 ' set not your heart too firmly upon that which may prove to be beyond 
 your reach , ' say I. ' the old man be rich , and will look high . ' 

 ' I would he be poor ! ' sigh Reuben , with all the selfishness of a 
 lover . ' if this war last I may win myself some honour or title . who 
 know ? other have do it , and why not I ! ' 

 ' of our three from Havant , ' I remark , ' one be spur onwards by 
 ambition , and one by love . now , what be I to do who care neither for 
 high office nor for the face of a maid ? what be to carry I into the 
 fight ? ' 

 ' our motif come and go , but yours be ever with you , ' say Reuben . 
 ' honour and duty be the two star , Micah , by which you have ever 
 steer your course . ' 

 ' faith , Mistress Ruth have teach you to make pretty speech , ' say I , 
 ' but methink she ought to be here amid the beauty of Taunton . ' 

 as I speak we be rid into the market - place , which be now crowd 
 with our troop . round the cross be group a score of maiden clothe in

 off to the different centre where food have be provide for they . the 
 King and his chief officer take up their quarter in the Castle , while 
 the Mayor and rich burgess find bed and board for the rest . as to

 encamp in the street and castle ground , while the remainder take up 
 their dwelling among the waggon in the field outside the city , where 
 they light up great fire , and have sheep roasting and beer flow as 
 merrily as though a march on London be but a holiday outing . 



 Chapter XXI . of my hand - grip with the Brandenburger 

 King Monmouth have call a council meeting for the evening , and summon 
 Colonel Decimus Saxon to attend it , with whom I go , bear with I 
 the small package which Sir Jacob Clancing have give over to my keeping . 
 on arrive at the Castle we find that the King have not yet come out

 fine room with lofty window and a noble ceiling of carved woodwork . at

 which Monmouth have formerly wear . here be assemble the principal 
 chief of the army , with many of the inferior commander , town officer , 
 and other who have petition to offer . Lord Grey of Wark stand silently 
 by the window , look out over the countryside with a gloomy face . Wade 
 and Holmes shake their head and whisper in a corner . Ferguson strode 
 about with his wig awry , shout out exhortation and prayer in a

 which appear to be shroud in many oath , and which be greet with

 calvinistic philosophy and its relation to statecraft . a few plain

 little flesh upon it , be more than I can verstehen . how have all thing 
 go with you ? ' 

 ' as of old , ' say Saxon . ' More blow than thaler , and great need of 
 a surgeon than of a strong - box . when do I see you last , friend ? be 
 it not at the onfall at Nurnberg , when I lead the right and you the left 
 wing of the heavy horse ? ' 

 ' Nay , ' say Buyse . ' I have meet you in the way of business since then . 
 have you forget the skirmish on the Rhine bank , when you do flash your 
 snapphahn at I ? sapperment ! have some rascally schelm not stab my 
 horse I should have sweep your head off as a boy cut thistle mit a

 if I remember aright , but do afterwards brain the sentry with your

 other . ' 

 ' some such base offer be indeed make I , ' say the German sternly . ' to 
 which I answer that , though I sell my sword , I do not sell my honour . 
 it be well that cavalier of fortune should show that an engagement be 
 with they -- how do ye say it?--unbreakable until the war be over . then by 
 all mean let he change his paymaster . warum nicht ? ' 

 ' true , friend , true ! ' reply Saxon . ' these beggarly Italians and Swiss 
 have make such a trade of the matter , and sell themselves so freely , 
 body and soul , to the long purse , that it be well that we should be 
 nice upon point of honour . but you remember the old hand - grip which no 
 man in the Palatinate could exchange with you ? here be my captain , Micah 
 Clarke . let he see how warm a north german welcome may be . ' 

 the Brandenburger show his white tooth in a grin as he hold out his

 suddenly bent his whole strength upon it , and squeeze my finger

 ' Donnerwetter ! ' he cry , laugh heartily at my start of pain and 
 surprise . ' it be a rough prussian game , and the english lad have not 
 much stomach for it . ' 

 ' truly , sir , ' say I , ' it be the first time that I have see the 
 pastime , and I would fain practise it under so able a master . ' 

 ' what , another ! ' he cry . ' why , you must be still pringle from the 
 first . Nay , if you will I shall not refuse you , though I fear it may 
 weaken your hold upon your sword - hilt . ' 

 he hold out his hand as he speak , and I grasp it firmly , thumb to 
 thumb , keep my elbow high so as to bear all my force upon it . his own 
 trick be , as I observe , to gain command of the other hand by a great 
 output of strength at the onset . this I prevent by myself put out 
 all my power . for a minute or more we stand motionless , gaze into each 
 other 's face . then I see a bead of sweat trickle down his forehead , and

 limp and slack while my own tighten ever upon it , until he be force

 ' Teufel und hexerei ! ' he cry , wipe away the blood which ooze from 
 under his nail , ' I might as well put my finger in a rat - trap . you 
 be the first man that ever yet exchange fair hand - grip with Anthony 
 Buyse . ' 

 ' we breed brawn in England as well as in Brandenburg , ' say Saxon , who 
 be shake with laughter over the german soldier 's discomfiture . ' why , 
 I have see that lad pick up a full - size sergeant of dragoon and throw 
 he into a cart as though he have be a clod of earth . ' 

 ' strong he be , ' grumble Buyse , still wring his injure hand , ' strong 
 as old Gotz mit de iron grip . but what good be strength alone in the 
 handling of a weapon ? it be not the force of a blow , but the way in 
 which it be geschlagen , that make the effect . your sword now be heavy 
 than mine , by the look of it , and yet my blade would bite deeply . eh ? be 
 not that a more soldierly sport than kinderspiel such as hand - grasping 
 and the like ? ' 

 ' he be a modest youth , ' say Saxon . ' yet I would match his stroke 
 against your . ' 

 ' for what ? ' snarl the German . 

 ' for as much wine as we can take at a sitting . 

 ' no small amount , either , ' say Buyse ; ' a brace of gallon at the least . 
 well , be it so . do you accept the contest ? ' 

 ' I shall do what I may , ' I answer , ' though I can scarce hope to strike

 ' Henker take your compliment , ' he cry gruffly . ' it be with sweet

 ' I must bruise my own headpiece to regain my soldierly credit , ' he

 ' well strike ! ' ' a brave stroke ! ' cry the spectator . ' it be proof 
 steel thrice weld , and warrant to turn a sword - blade , ' one remark ,

           " when thy star be in trine , 
            between darkness and shine , 
            Duke Monmouth , Duke Monmouth , 
            beware of the Rhine ! " 

 Thy star in trine ! what tomfoolery be this ? ' 

 ' if it please your Majesty , ' say I , ' I have reason to believe that the 
 man who send you this message be one of those who be deeply skilled 
 in the art of divination , and who pretend from the motion of the 
 celestial body to foretell the fate of man . '

 be an astrological term , which signifieth when your natal planet shall

 question of every housewife . ' 

           ' and tell by Venus and the moon , 
            who steal a thimble or a spoon . ' 
 mutter Saxon , quote from his favourite poem .

 I bow and take up my post as order , while the council - man and

 from my central post by the door I could see the line of face on

 ' if naebody else will gie an opeenion , ' cry the fanatical doctor ,

 in the cause and slave in it , much endure and suffer mony thing 
 at the hond o ' the froward , whereby my ain speerit hath plentifully

 hiss and scorn into waste place ? '

 ' I shall mak ' mysel ' clear , your Majesty . have we no hear that Argyle 
 be cutten off ? and why be he cutten off ? because he hadna due faith

 child o ' light in favour o ' the bare - legged spawn o ' Prelacy , wha be

 a hum of assent and approval rise up from the more puritan member of

 ' you , Lord Grey , ' he say , ' be a soldier and a man of experience . what 
 be your advice ? should we halt here or push forward towards London ? ' 

 ' to advance to the East would , in my humble judgment , be fatal to we , ' 
 Grey answer , speak slowly , with the manner of a man who have think

 in horse , and we have none . we can hold our own amongst hedgerow or in

 amid a pack of wolf . again , every step which we take towards London

 which supply our necessity , while it strengthen our enemy by

 unless , therefore , we hear of some great outbreak elsewhere , or of some

 ground and wait an attack . ' 

 ' you argue shrewdly and well , my Lord Grey , ' say the King . ' but how

 ' tell I , Colonel Buyse , ' say the King , master his emotion by a 
 strong effort . ' do you , as a soldier , agree with my Lord Grey ? ' 

 ' ask Saxon , your Majesty , ' the German answer . ' my opinion in a 
 Raths - Versammlung be , I have observe , ever the same as his . ' 

 ' then we turn to you , Colonel Saxon , ' say Monmouth . ' we have in this 
 council a party who be in favour of an advance and a party who wish

 ' I will give my opinion , your Majesty , ' he say at last . ' Feversham and 
 Churchill be make for Salisbury with three thousand foot , and they 
 have push on eight hundred of the Blue Guards , and two or three 
 dragoon regiment . we should , therefore , as Lord Grey say , have to

 Ferguson laugh contemptuously , and breathe out a prayer , but many of 
 the other Puritans nod their head to acknowledge that this be not 
 an unreasonable view to take of it . 

 ' on the other hand , sire , ' Saxon continue , ' it appear to I that to 
 remain here be equally impossible . your Majesty 's friend throughout

 blow . the rustic would flock off to their wife and home . such an 
 example be catch . I have see a great army thaw away like an icicle 
 in the sunshine . once go , it be no easy matter to collect they again . 
 to keep they we must employ they . never let they have an idle minute . 
 drill they . March they . exercise they . work they . preach to they . make 
 they obey God and their Colonel . this can not be do in snug quarter . 
 they must travel . we can not hope to end this business until we get 
 to London . London , then , must be our goal . but there be many way of 
 reach it . you have , sire , as I have hear , many friend at Bristol 
 and in the Midlands . if I might advise , I should say let we march round 
 in that direction . every day that pass will serve to swell your force 
 and improve your troop , while all will feel something be astirre . 
 should we take Bristol -- and I hear that the work be not very 
 strong -- it would give we a very good command of shipping , and a rare 
 centre from which to act . if all go well with we , we could make 
 our way to London through Gloucestershire and Worcestershire . in the 
 meantime I might suggest that a day of fast and humiliation be call to 
 bring down a blessing on the cause . ' 

 this address , skilfully compound of worldly wisdom and of spiritual 
 zeal , win the applause of the whole council , and especially that of King 
 Monmouth , whose melancholy vanish as if by magic . 

 ' by my faith , Colonel , ' say he , ' you make it all as clear as day . 
 of course , if we make ourselves strong in the West , and my uncle be 
 threaten with disaffection elsewhere , he will have no chance to hold 
 out against we . should he wish to fight we upon our own ground , he must 
 need drain his troop from north , south , and east , which be not to be 
 think of . we may very well march to London by way of Bristol . ' 

 ' I think that the advice be good , ' Lord Grey observe ; ' but I should 
 like to ask Colonel Saxon what warrant he hath for say that Churchill 
 and Feversham be on their way , with three thousand regular foot and 
 several regiment of horse ? ' 

 ' the word of an officer of the Blues with whom I converse at 
 Salisbury , ' Saxon answer . ' he confide in I , believe I to be one 
 of the Duke of Beaufort 's household . as to the horse , one party pursue 
 we on Salisbury Plain with bloodhound , and another attack we not 
 twenty mile from here and lose a score of trooper and a cornet . ' 

 ' we hear something of the brush , ' say the King . ' it be bravely do . 
 but if these man be so close we have no great time for preparation . ' 

 ' their foot can not be here before a week , ' say the Mayor . ' by that time 
 we might be behind the wall of Bristol . ' 

 ' there be one point which might be urge , ' observe Wade the lawyer . ' we 
 have , as your Majesty most truly say , meet with heavy discouragement in 
 the fact that no nobleman and few commoner of repute have declare for 
 we . the reason be , I opine , that each doth wait for his neighbour to 
 make a move . should one or two come over the other would soon follow . 
 how , then , be we to bring a duke or two to our standard ? ' 

 ' there be the question , Master Wade , ' say Monmouth , shake his head 
 despondently . 

 ' I think that it might be do , ' continue the Whig lawyer . ' mere 
 proclamation address to the commonalty will not catch these gold 
 fish . they be not to be angle for with a naked hook . I should 
 recommend that some form of summon or writ be serve upon each of they , 
 call upon they to appear in our camp within a certain date under pain 
 of high treason . ' 

 ' there spake the legal mind , ' quoth King Monmouth , with a laugh . ' but 
 you have omit to tell we how the say writ or summon be to be 
 convey to these same delinquent . ' 

 ' there be the Duke of Beaufort , ' continue Wade , disregard the King 's 
 objection . ' he be President of Wales , and he be , as your Majesty know , 
 lieutenant of four english county . his influence overshadow the whole 
 West . he hath two hundred horse in his stable at Badminton , and a 
 thousand man , as I have hear , sit down at his table every day . why 
 should not a special effort be make to gain over such a one , the more so 
 as we intend to march in his direction ? ' 

 ' Henry , Duke of Beaufort , be unfortunately already in arm against his 
 sovereign , ' say Monmouth gloomily . 

 ' he be , sire , but he may be induce to turn in your favour the weapon 
 which he hath raise against you . he be a protestant . he be say to be a 
 Whig . why should we not send a message to he ? Flatter his pride . appeal 
 to his religion . coax and threaten he . who know ? he may have private 
 grievance of which we know nothing , and may be ripe for such a move . ' 

 ' your counsel be good , Wade , ' say Lord Grey , ' but methink his Majesty 
 hath ask a pertinent question . your messenger would , I fear , find 
 himself swing upon one of the Badminton oak if the Duke desire to 
 show his loyalty to James Stuart . where be we to find a man who be wary 
 enough and bold enough for such a mission , without risk one of our 
 leader , who could be ill - spar at such a time ? ' 

 ' it be true , ' say the King . ' it be well not to venture it at all 
 than to do it in a clumsy and halting fashion . beaufort would think that 
 it be a plot not to gain he over , but to throw discredit upon he . but 
 what mean our giant at the door by sign to we ? ' 

 ' if it please your Majesty , ' I ask , ' have I permission to speak ? ' 

 ' we would fain hear you , Captain , ' he answer graciously . ' if your 
 understanding be in any degree correspondent to your strength , your 
 opinion should be of weight . ' 

 ' then , your Majesty , ' say I , ' I would offer myself as a fitting 
 messenger in this matter . my father bid I spare neither life nor limb 
 in this quarrel , and if this honourable council think that the Duke 
 may be gain over , I be ready to guarantee that the message shall be 
 convey to he if man and horse can do it . ' 

 ' I will warrant that no well herald could be find , ' cry Saxon . ' the 
 lad hath a cool head and a staunch heart . ' 

 ' then , young sir , we shall accept your loyal and gallant offer , ' say 
 Monmouth . ' be ye all agree , gentleman , upon the point ? ' a murmur of 
 assent rise from the company . 

 ' you shall draw up the paper , Wade . offer he money , a seniority amongst 
 the duke , the perpetual Presidentship of Wales -- what you will , if you 
 can but shake he . if not , sequestration , exile , and everlaste infamy . 
 and , hark ye ! you can enclose a copy of the paper draw up by Van 
 Brunow , which prove the marriage of my mother , together with the 
 attestation of the witness . have they ready by to - morrow at daybreak , 
 when the messenger may start . ' ( note H , Appendix . ) 

 ' they shall be ready , your Majesty , ' say Wade . 

 ' in that case , gentleman , ' continue King Monmouth , ' I may now dismiss 
 ye to your post . should anything fresh arise I shall summon ye again , 
 that I may profit by your wisdom . here we shall stay , if Sir Stephen 
 Timewell will have we , until the man be refresh and the recruit 
 enrol . we shall then make our way Bristolwards , and see what luck 
 await we in the North . if Beaufort come over all will be well . 
 Farewell , my kind friend ! I need not tell ye to be diligent and 
 faithful . ' 

 the council rise at the King 's salutation , and bow to he they begin 
 to file out of the Castle hall . several of the member cluster round 
 I with hint for my journey or suggestion as to my conduct . 

 ' he be a proud , froward man , ' say one . ' speak humbly to he or he will 
 never hearken to your message , but will order you to be scourge out of 
 his presence . ' 

 ' Nay , nay ! ' cry another . ' he be hot , but he love a man that be a 
 man . speak boldly and honestly to he , and he be more like to listen to 
 reason . ' 

 ' speak as the Lord shall direct you , ' say a Puritan . ' it be his message 
 which you bear as well as the King 's . ' 

 ' entice he out alone upon some excuse , ' say Buyse , ' then up and away 
 mit he upon your crupper . Hagelsturm ! that would be a proper game . ' 

 ' leave he alone , ' cry Saxon . ' the lad hath as much sense as any of 
 ye . he will see which way the cat jump . come , friend , let we make our 
 way back to our man . ' 

 ' I be sorry , indeed , to lose you , ' he say , as we thread our way 
 through the throng of peasant and soldier upon the Castle Green . ' your 
 company will miss you sorely . Lockarby must see to the two . if all go 
 well you should be back in three or four day . I need not tell you that 
 there be a real danger . if the Duke wish to prove to James that 
 he would not allow himself to be tamper with , he can only do it by 
 punish the messenger , which as lieutenant of a county he hath power 
 to do in time of civil commotion . he be a hard man if all report be 
 true . on the other hand , if you should chance to succeed it may lay the 
 foundation of your fortune and be the mean of save Monmouth . he 
 need help , by the Lord Harry ! never have I see such a rabble as this 
 army of his . Buyse say that they fight lustily at this ruffle at 
 Axminster , but he be of one mind with I , that a few whiff of shot and 
 cavalry charge would scatter they over the countryside . have you any 
 message to leave ? ' 

 ' none , save my love to my mother , ' say I. 

 ' it be well . should you fall in any unfair way , I shall not forget his 
 Grace of Beaufort , and the next of his gentleman who come in my way 
 shall hang as high as Haman . and now you have good make for your chamber , 
 and have as good a slumber as you may , since to - morrow at cock - crow 
 begin your new mission . ' 



 Chapter XXII . of the News from Havant 

 have give my order that Covenant should be saddle and bridle by

 ' three guess , Clarke ! ' he cry . ' what would you most desire ? ' 

 ' letter from Havant , ' say I eagerly .

 a woman 's hand among they . sink I , if I can understand what you have 
 be do all your life . 

           " how can youthful heart resign 
            lovely woman , sparkle wine ? " 

 but you be so lose in your news that you have not observe my 
 transformation . '

 to my little box , which a fair friend of mine in town pack for I ,

 chance to find its way , however , to the Bruton inn , and the good woman

 which I can say from my heart that I never transgress . I always carry a 
 corkscrew , and I never forget to kiss the landlady . '

 mortimer shall measure sword with I for this . I shall write villain

 ' no , ' he answer , laugh . ' many have live with I for year and 
 would give I a certificate for temper . but this be too much . Sir Edward

 go to he should aught befall I , so that he be not averse to

 sit up in bed smoking , with my letter litter about in front of 
 I .

 have , as I say , do Mortimer some turn which he might remember , 
 though it do not become I to remind he of they . this mistress 
 Butterworth be mine old wet - nurse , and it hath be the custom of the 
 family to provide for she . I could not bear the thought that in the ruin

 between she and hunger . my only request to Mortimer , therefore , make on

 I promise that should I prosper I would return whatever he should 
 disburse . the mean - hearted villain wring my hand and swear that it 
 should be so . how vile a thing be human nature , Clarke ! for the sake of 
 this paltry sum he , a rich man , hath break his pledge , and leave this 
 poor woman to starve . but he shall answer to I for it . he think that 
 I be on the Atlantic . if I march back to London with these brave boy 
 I shall disturb the tenor of his sainted existence . meanwhile I shall 
 trust to sun - dial , and off go my watch to Mother Butterworth . Bless 
 her ample bosom ! I have try many liquor , but I dare bet that the 
 first be the most healthy . but how of your own letter ? you have be 
 frown and smile like an April day . ' 

 ' there be one from my father , with a few word attach from my mother , ' 
 say I. ' the second be from an old friend of mine , Zachariah Palmer , the 
 village carpenter . the third be from Solomon Sprent , a retired seaman , 
 for whom I have an affection and respect . ' 

 ' you have a rare trio of newsmen . I would I know your father , Clarke , he 
 must , from what you say , be a stout bit of british oak . I speak even now 
 of your know little of the world , but indeed it may be that in your 
 village you can see mankind without the varnish , and so come to learn 
 more of the good of human nature . varnish or none , the bad will ever 
 peep through . now this carpenter and seaman show themselves no doubt for 
 what they be . a man might know my friend of the court for a lifetime , 
 and never come upon their real self , nor would it perhaps repay the 
 search when you have come across it . sink I , but I wax philosophical , 
 which be the old refuge of the ruin man . give I a tub , and I shall 
 set up in the Piazza of Covent Garden , and be the Diogenes of London . I 
 would not be wealthy again , micah ! how go the old lilt?-- 

           " our money shall never indite we 
            or drag we to Goldsmith Hall , 
            no pirate or wreck can affright we . 
            we that have no estate 
            fear no plunder or rate , 
            nor care to lock gate . 
            he that lie on the ground can not fall ! " 

 that last would make a good motto for an almshouse . ' 

 ' you will have Sir Stephen up , ' say I warningly , for he be carol 
 away at the pitch of his lung . 

 ' never fear ! he and his ' prentice be all at the broad - sword exercise

 stamp , and swing his sword , and cry , " ha ! " on the down - cut . mistress

 with our gallant Colonel at his inn , and he hath tell I enough of your

 ' this one be somewhat grave , ' say I. 

 ' Nay , I be in the humour for grave thing . have at it , if it contain the 
 whole platonic philosophy . ' 

 '' tis from the venerable carpenter who hath for many year be my 
 adviser and friend . he be one who be religious without be sectarian , 
 philosophic without be a partisan , and love without be weak . ' 

 ' a paragon , truly ! ' exclaim Sir Gervas , who be busy with his eyebrow 
 brush . 

 ' this be what he saith , ' I continue , and proceed to read the very 
 letter which I now read to you . 

 ' " have hear from your father , my dear lad , that there be some chance 
 of be able to send a letter to you , I have write this , and be 
 now send it under the charge of the worthy John Packingham , of 
 Chichester , who be bind for the West . I trust that you be now safe 
 with Monmouth 's army , and that you have receive honourable appointment 
 therein . I doubt not that you will find among your comrade some who 
 be extreme sectary , and other who be scoffer and disbeliever . 
 be advise by I , friend , and avoid both the one and the other . for the 
 zealot be a man who not only defend his own right of worship , wherein 
 he hath justice , but wish to impose upon the conscience of other , 
 by which he fall into the very error against which he fight . the mere 
 brainless scoffer be , on the other hand , low than the beast of 
 the field , since he lack the animal 's self - respect and humble 
 resignation . " ' 

 ' my faith ! ' cry the Baronet , ' the old gentleman hath a rough side to 
 his tongue . ' 

 ' " let we take religion upon its broad base , for the truth must be 
 broad than aught which we can conceive . the presence of a table doth 
 prove the existence of a carpenter , and so the presence of a universe 
 prove the existence of a universe maker , call he by what name 
 you will . so far the ground be very firm beneath we , without either 
 inspiration , teaching , or any aid whatever . since , then , there _ must _ be 
 a world Maker , let we judge of his nature by his work . we can not observe 
 the glory of the firmament , its infinite extent , its beauty , and the 
 Divine skill wherewith every plant and animal hath its want care for , 
 without see that he be full of wisdom , intelligence , and power . we 
 be still , you will perceive , upon solid ground , without have to call 
 to our aid aught save pure reason . " ' 

 ' " have get so far , let we inquire to what end the universe be make , 
 and we put upon it . the teaching of all nature show that it must be to 
 the end of improvement and upward growth , the increase in real virtue , 
 in knowledge , and in wisdom . nature be a silent preacher which hold 
 forth upon week - day as on Sabbaths . we see the acorn grow into the oak , 
 the egg into the bird , the maggot into the butterfly . shall we doubt , 
 then , that the human soul , the most precious of all thing , be also 
 upon the upward path ? and how can the soul progress save through the 
 cultivation of virtue and self - mastery ? what other way be there ? there 
 be none . we may say with confidence , then , that we be place here to 
 increase in knowledge and in virtue . " ' 

 ' " this be the core of all religion , and this much need no faith in 
 the acceptance . it be as true and as capable of proof as one of those 
 exercise of Euclid which we have go over together . on this common 
 ground man have raise many different building . Christianity , the creed 
 of Mahomet , the creed of the Easterns , have all the same essence . the 
 difference lie in the form and the detail . let we hold to our own 
 christian creed , the beautiful , often - profess , and seldom - practise 
 doctrine of love , but let we not despise our fellow - man , for we be all 
 branch from the common root of truth . " ' 

 ' " man come out of darkness into light . he tarry awhile and then 
 pass into darkness again . Micah , lad , the day be pass , mine as 
 well as thine . let they not be waste . they be few in number . what say 
 Petrarch ? ' to he that enter , life seem infinite ; to he that 
 depart , nothing . ' let every day , every hour , be spend in further the 
 Creator 's end -- in get out whatever power for good there be in you . 
 what be pain , or work , or trouble ? the cloud that pass over the sun . 
 but the result of work well do be everything . it be eternal . it live 
 and wax strong through the century . pause not for rest . the rest 
 will come when the hour of work be past . " ' 

 ' " may God protect and guard you ! there be no great news . the Portsmouth 
 garrison hath march to the West . Sir John Lawson , the magistrate , hath 
 be down here threaten your father and other , but he can do little 
 for want of proof . Church and Dissent be at each other 's throat as 
 ever . truly the stern law of Moses be more enduring than the sweet word 
 of Christ . Adieu , my dear lad ! all good wish from your grey - head 
 friend , zachariah PALMER . " '

 ' " Master Clarke . Sir,--When last we be in company I have run in under

 prize , which prove to be in proper trim alow and aloft-- " '

 ' proceed , then , ' quoth the Baronet . 

 ' " have also read she the article of war , I explain to her the 
 condition under which we be to sail in company on life 's voyage , 
 namely : " ' 

 ' " first . she to obey signal without question as soon as receive . " ' 

 ' " second . she to steer by my reckoning . " ' 

 ' " Third . she to stand by I as true consort in foul weather , battle , or 
 shipwreck . " ' 

 ' " fourth . she to run under my gun if assail by picaroon , 
 privateero , or garda - costa . " ' 

 ' " Fifth . I to keep she in due repair , dry - dock she at interval , 
 and see that she hath her allowance of coat of paint , streamer , and 
 bunting , as befit a saucy pleasure boat . " ' 

 ' " Sixth . I to take no other craft in tow , and if any be now attach , 
 to cut their hawser . " ' 

 ' " Seventh . I to revictual her day by day . " ' 

 ' " Eighth . should she chance to spring a leak , or be blow on her beam 
 end by the wind of misfortune , to stand by she and see she pump out 
 or right . " ' 

 ' " ninth . to fly the protestant ensign at the peak during life 's voyage , 
 and to lay our course for the great harbour , in the hope that mooring

 up for eternity . " ' 

 ' " ' twas close on eight - bell before these article be sign and 
 seal . when I head after you I could not so much as catch a glimpse

 ' " farewell , mate ! in action , take an old sailor 's advice . keep the 
 weather - gauge and board ! tell that to your admiral on the day of battle . 
 Whisper it in his ear . say to he , ' keep the weather - gauge and board ! ' 
 tell he also to strike quick , strike hard , and keep on strike . that be

 Sir Gervas have be chuckle to himself during the reading of this

 '' tis but a few line , ' I answer . ' he be ever short of speech . but

 ' " Postscriptum ( from my mother).--I trust that you will remember what I
