 our religion at present . ' 

 ' Early Christians , ' I suggest , and we both laugh as we settle down 
 to sleep . 



 Chapter XXIII . of the Snare on the Weston Road 

 just after sunrise I be awake by one of the Mayor 's servant , who 
 bring word that the Honourable Master Wade be await I downstairs . 
 have dress and descend , I find he seat by the table in the 
 sitting - room with paper and wafer - box , seal up the missive which I 
 be to carry . he be a small , worn , grey - face man , very erect in his 
 bearing and sudden in his speech , with more of the soldier than of the 
 lawyer in his appearance . 

 ' so , ' say he , press his seal above the fastening of the string , ' I 
 see that your horse be ready for you outside . you have well make your way 
 round by Nether Stowey and the Bristol Channel , for we have hear that 
 the enemy 's horse guard the road on the far side of Wells . here be your 
 packet . ' 

 I bow and place it in the inside of my tunic . 

 ' it be a write order as suggest in the council . the Duke 's reply may 
 be write , or it may be by word of mouth . in either case guard it well . 
 this packet contain also a copy of the deposition of the clergyman at 
 the Hague , and of the other witness who see Charles of England marry 
 Lucy Walters , the mother of his Majesty . your mission be one of such 
 importance that the whole success of our enterprise may turn upon it . 
 see that you serve the paper upon Beaufort in person , and not through 
 any intermediary , or it might not stand in a court of law . ' 

 I promise to do so if possible . 

 ' I should advise you also , ' he continue , ' to carry sword and pistol as 
 a protection against the chance danger of the road , but to discard your 
 head - piece and steel - front as give you too warlike an aspect for a 
 peaceful messenger . ' 

 ' I have already come to that resolve , ' say I. 

 ' there be nothing more to be say , Captain , ' say the lawyer , give I 
 his hand . ' may all good fortune go with you . keep a still tongue and a 
 quick ear . watch keenly how all thing go . Mark whose face be gloomy and 
 whose content . the Duke may be at Bristol , but you have good make for his 
 seat at Badminton . our sign of the day be tewkesbury . ' 

 thank my instructor for his advice I go out and mount Covenant , 
 who paw and champ at his bit in his delight at get start 
 once more . few of the townsman be stir , though here and there 
 a night - bonnet head star out at I through a casement . I take the 
 precaution of walk the horse very quietly until we be some distance 
 from the house , for I have tell Reuben nothing of my intended journey , 
 and I be convince that if he know of it neither discipline , nor even 
 his new tie of love , would prevent he from come with I . Covenant 's 
 iron - shod foot rang sharply , in spite of my care , upon the cobblestone , 
 but look back I see that the blind of my faithful friend 's room 
 be undrawn , and that all seem quiet in the house . I shake my bridle , 
 therefore , and ride at a brisk trot through the silent street , which 
 be still strew with fade flower and gay with streamer . at the 
 north gate a guard of half a company be station , who let I pass upon 
 hear the word . once beyond the old wall I find myself out on the 
 country side , with my face to the north and a clear road in front of I . 

 it be a blithesome morning . the sun be rise over the distant hill , 
 and heaven and earth be ruddy and golden . the tree in the wayside 
 orchard be full of swarm of bird , who chatter and sing until the 
 air be full of their piping . there be lightsomeness and gladness in 
 every breath . the wistful - eyed red Somerset kine stand along by the 
 hedgerow , cast great shadow down the field and gaze at I as 
 I pass . farm horse lean over wooden gate , and snort a word of 
 greeting to their glossy - coat brother . a great herd of snowy - fleece 
 sheep stream towards we over the hillside and frisk and gambol in 
 the sunshine . all be innocent life , from the lark which sing on high 
 to the little shrew - mouse which run amongst the ripen corn , or the 
 martin which dash away at the sound of my approach . all alive and all 
 innocent . what be we to think , my dear child , when we see the beast 
 of the field full of kindness and virtue and gratitude ? where be this 
 superiority of which we talk ? 

 from the high ground to the north I look back upon the sleep town , 
 with the broad edging of tent and waggon , which show how suddenly 
 its population have outgrow it . the Royal Standard still flutter 
 from the tower of St. Mary Magdalene , while close by its beautiful 
 brother - turret of St. James bore aloft the blue flag of Monmouth . as 
 I gaze the quick petulant roll of a drum rise up on the still morning 
 air , with the clear ringing call of the bugle summon the troop from 
 their slumber . beyond the town , and on either side of it , stretch a 
 glorious view of the Somersetshire down , roll away to the distant 
 sea , with town and hamlet , castle turret and church tower , woode coombe 
 and stretch of grain - land -- as fair a scene as the eye could wish to rest 
 upon . as I wheel my horse and speed upon my way I feel , my dear , that 
 this be a land worth fight for , and that a man 's life be a small 
 thing if he could but aid , in however trifle a degree , in work out 
 its freedom and its happiness . at a little village over the hill I fall 
 in with an outpost of horse , the commander of which ride some distance 
 with I , and set I on my road to Nether Stowey . it seem strange to 
 my Hampshire eye to note that the earth be all red in these part -- very 
 different to the chalk and gravel of Havant . the cow , too , be mostly 
 red . the cottage be build neither of brick nor of wood , but of some 
 form of plaster , which they call cob , which be strong and smooth so 
 long as no water come near it . they shelter the wall from the rain , 
 therefore , by great overhanging thatch . there be scarcely a steeple in 
 the whole country - side , which also seem strange to a man from any other 
 part of England . every church hath a square tower , with pinnacle upon 
 the top , and they be mostly very large , with fine peal of bell . 

 my course run along by the foot of the beautiful Quantock Hills , where 
 heavy - woode coombe be scatter over the broad heathery down , deep 
 with bracken and whortle - bush . on either side of the track steep 
 wind glen slop downward , line with yellow gorse , which blaze 
 out from the deep - red soil like a flame from ember . peat - colour 
 stream splash down these valley and over the road , through which 
 Covenant plough fetlock deep , and shy to see the broad - back trout 
 dart from between his fore foot . 

 all day I ride through this beautiful country , meet few folk , for 
 I keep away from the main road . a few shepherd and farmer , a 
 long - legged clergyman , a packman with his mule , and a horseman with a 
 great bag , whom I take to be a buyer of hair , be all that I can recall . 
 a black jack of ale and the heel of a loaf at a wayside inn be all my 
 refreshment . Near Combwich , Covenant cast a shoe , and two hour be 
 waste before I find a smithy in the town and have the matter set right . 
 it be not until evening that I at last come out upon the bank of the 
 Bristol Channel , at a place call Shurton Bars , where the muddy Parret 
 make its way into the sea . at this point the channel be so broad that 
 the Welsh mountain can scarcely be distinguish . the shore be flat 
 and black and oozy , fleck over with white patch of sea - bird , but 
 far to the east there rise a line of hill , very wild and rugged , 
 rise in place into steep precipice . these cliff run out into the 
 sea , and numerous little harbour and bay be form in their break 
 surface , which be dry half the day , but can float a good - sized boat at 
 half - tide . the road wind over these bleak and rocky hill , which be 
 sparsely inhabit by a wild race of fisherman , or shepherd , who come 
 to their cabin door on hear the clatter of my horse 's hoof , and 
 shoot some rough west - country jest at I as I pass . as the night draw 
 in the country become bleaker and more deserted . an occasional light 
 twinkling in the distance from some lonely hillside cottage be the only 
 sign of the presence of man . the rough track still skirt the sea , and 
 high as it be , the spray from the breaker drift across it . the salt 
 prinkle on my lip , and the air be fill with the hoarse roar of the 
 surge and the thin piping of curlew , who flit past in the darkness 
 like white , shadowy , sad - voice creature from some other world . the 
 wind blow in short , quick , angry puff from the westward , and far out on 
 the black water a single glimmer of light rise and fall , toss 
 up , and then sink out of sight , show how fierce a sea have rise in 
 the channel . 

 rid through the gloaming in this strange wild scenery my mind 
 naturally turn towards the past . I think of my father and my mother , 
 of the old carpenter and of Solomon Sprent . then I ponder over Decimus 
 Saxon , his many - faced character have in it so much to be admire and 
 so much to be abhor . do I like he or no ? it be more than I could 
 say . from he I wander off to my faithful Reuben , and to his love 
 passage with the pretty Puritan , which in turn bring I to Sir Gervas 
 and the wreck of his fortune . my mind then wander to the state of the 
 army and the prospect of the rising , which lead I to my present mission 
 with its peril and its difficulty . have turn over all these 
 thing in my mind I begin to doze upon my horse 's back , overcome by the 
 fatigue of the journey and the drowsy lullaby of the wave . I have just 
 fall into a dream in which I see Reuben Lockarby crown King of 
 England by Mistress Ruth Timewell , while Decimus Saxon endeavour to 
 shoot he with a bottle of Daffy 's elixir , when in an instant , 
 without warn , I be dash violently from my horse , and leave lie 
 half - conscious on the stony track . 

 so stunned and shake be I by the sudden fall , that though I have a dim 
 knowledge of shadowy figure bend over I , and of hoarse laughter 
 sound in my ear , I could not tell for a few minute where I be nor 
 what have befall I . when at last I do make an attempt to recover my 
 foot I find that a loop of rope have be slip round my arm and my 
 leg so as to secure they . with a hard struggle I get one hand free , and 
 dash it in the face of one of the man who be hold I down ; but 
 the whole gang of a dozen or more set upon I at once , and while some 
 thump and kick at I , other tie a fresh cord round my elbow , and 
 deftly fasten it in such a way as to pinion I completely . find 
 that in my weak and daze state all effort be of no avail , I lay 
 sullen and watchful , take no heed of the random blow which be still 
 shower upon I . so dark be it that I could neither see the face of 
 my attacker , nor form any guess as to who they might be , or how they 
 have hurl I from my saddle . the champing and stamping of a horse hard 
 by show I that Covenant be a prisoner as well as his master . 

 ' Dutch Pete 's get as much as he can carry , ' say a rough , harsh voice . 
 ' he lie on the track as limp as a conger . ' 

 ' ah , poor pete ! ' mutter another . ' he will never deal a card or drain a 
 glass of the right Cognac again . ' 

 ' there you lie , mine goot vriend , ' say the injure man , in weak , 
 quaver tone . ' and I will prove that you lie if you have a flaschen 
 in your pocket . ' 

 ' if Pete be dead and bury , ' the first speaker say , ' a word about 
 strong water would bring he to . give he a sup from your bottle , 
 Dicon . ' 

 there be a great gurgling and suck in the darkness , follow by a 
 gasp from the drinker . ' gott sei gelobt , ' he exclaim in a strong 
 voice , ' I have see more star than ever be make . have my kopf not 
 be well hoop he would have knock it in like an ill - stave cask . he 
 shlag like the kick of a horse . ' 

 as he speak the edge of the moon peep over a cliff and throw a flood 
 of cold clear light upon the scene . look up I see that a strong rope 
 have be tie across the road from one tree trunk to another about eight 
 foot above the ground . this could not be see by I , even have I be 
 fully awake , in the dusk ; but catch I across the breast as Covenant 
 trot under it , it have sweep I off and dash I with great force to 
 the ground . either the fall or the blow which I have receive have cut I 
 badly , for I could feel the blood trickle in a warm stream past my 
 ear and down my neck . I make no attempt to move , however , but wait in 
 silence to find out who these man be into whose hand I have fall . 
 my one fear be lest my letter should be take away from I , and my 
 mission render of no avail . that in this , my first trust , I should be 
 disarm without a blow and lose the paper which have be confide to 
 I , be a chance which make I flush and tingle with shame at the very 
 thought . 

 the gang who have seize I be rough - bearded fellow in fur cap and 
 fustian jacket , with buff belt round their waist , from which hung 
 short straight whinyard . their dark sun - dry face and their great 
 boot mark they as fisherman or seaman , as might be guess from their 
 rude sailor speech . a pair knelt on either side with their hand upon 
 my arm , a third stand behind with a cocked pistol point at my head , 
 while the other , seven or eight in number , be help to his foot the 
 man whom I have strike , who be bleed freely from a cut over the eye . 

 ' take the horse up to Daddy Mycroft 's , ' say a stout , black - bearded man , 
 who seem to be their leader . ' it be no mere dragoon hack,(Note 
 I. Appendix ) but a comely , full - blooded brute , which will fetch sixty 
 piece at the least . your share of that , Peter , will buy salve and 
 plaster for your cut . ' 

 ' ha , houndsfoot ! ' cry the Dutchman , shake his fist at I . ' you 
 would strike Peter , would you ? you would draw Peter 's blood , would you ? 
 tausend Teufel , man ! if you and I be together upon the hillside we 
 should see vich vas the petter man . ' 

 ' slack your jaw tackle , Pete , ' growl one of his comrade . ' this fellow 
 be a limb of Satan for sure , and doth follow a calling that none but a 
 mean , snivel , baseborn son of a gun would take to . yet I warrant , 
 from the look of he , that he could truss you like a woodcock if he have 
 his great hand upon you . and you would howl for help as you do last 
 Martinmas , when you do mistake Cooper Dick 's wife for a gauger . ' 

 ' truss I , would he ? Todt und Holle ! ' cry the other , whom the blow 
 and the brandy have drive to madness . ' we shall see . take that , thou 
 deyvil 's spawn , take that ! ' he run at I , and kick I as hard as he 
 could with his heavy sea - boot . 

 some of the gang laugh , but the man who have speak before give 
 the Dutchman a shove that send he whirling . ' none of that , ' he say 
 sternly . ' we will have british fair - play on british soil , and none of your 
 cursed longshore trick . I will not stand by and see an Englishman 
 kick , d'ye see , by a tub - belly , round - starne , schnapps - swill , 
 chicken - hearted son of an Amsterdam lust - vrouw . hang he , if the skipper 
 like . that be all above board , but by thunder , if it be a fight that you 
 will have , touch that man again . ' 

 ' all right , Dicon , ' say their leader soothingly . ' we all know that 
 Pete be not a fight man , but he be the good cooper on the coast , eh , 
 Pete ? there be not his equal at stave , hooping , and bumping . he will 
 take a plank of wood and turn it into a keg while another man would be 
 think of it . ' 

 ' oh , you remember that , Captain Murgatroyd , ' say the Dutchman sulkily . 
 ' but you see I knock about and shlagge , and bully , and call 
 name , and what help have I ? so help I , when the _ Maria _ be in the 
 Texel next , I will take to my old trade , I will , and never set foot on she 
 again . ' 

 ' no fear , ' the Captain answer , laugh . ' while the _ Maria _ bring in 
 five thousand good piece a year , and can show her heel to any cutter 
 on the coast , there be no fear of greedy Pete lose his share of she . 
 why , man , at this rate you may have a lust - haus of your own in a year or 
 two , with a trimmed lawn , and the tree all clip like peacock , and 
 the flower in pattern , and a canal by the door , and a great bounce 
 house - wife just like any burgomeister . there be many such a fortune be 
 make out of Mechlin and Cognac . ' 

 ' Aye , and there be many a broken kopf get over Mechlin and Cognac , ' 
 grumble my enemy . ' Donner ! there be other thing beside lust - house 
 and flower - bed . there be lee - shore and nor'-wester , beak and 
 preventive . ' 

 ' and there be where the smart seaman have the pull over the herre buss , 
 or the skulk coaster that work from Christmas to Christmas with all 
 the danger and none of the little picking . but enough say ! up with the 
 prisoner , and let we get he safely into the bilbo . ' 

 I be raise to my foot and half carry , half drag along in the 
 midst of the gang . my horse have already be lead away in the opposite 
 direction . our course lie off the road , down a very rocky and rugged 
 ravine which slop away towards the sea . there seem to be no trace of 
 a path , and I could only stumble along over rock and bush as good I 
 might in my fettered and crippled state . the blood , however , have 
 dry over my wound , and the cool sea breeze playing upon my forehead 
 refresh I , and help I to take a clear view of my position . 

 it be plain from their talk that these man be smuggler . as such , 
 they be not likely to have any great love for the Government , or 
 desire to uphold King James in any way . on the contrary , their goodwill 
 would probably be with Monmouth , for have I not see the day before a 
 whole regiment of foot in his army , raise from among the coaster folk ? 
 on the other hand , their greed might be strong than their loyalty , and 
 might lead they to hand I over to justice in the hope of reward . on the 
 whole it would be good , I think , to say nothing of my mission , and to 
 keep my paper secret as long as possible . 

 but I could not but wonder , as I be drag along , what have lead these 
 man to lie in wait for I as they have do . the road along which I have 
 travel be a lonely one , and yet a fair number of traveller bind 
 from the West through Weston to Bristol must use it . the gang could 
 not lie in perpetual guard over it . why have they set a trap on this 
 particular night , then ? the smuggler be a lawless and desperate body , 
 but they do not , as a rule , descend to foot - paddery or robbery . as long 
 as no one interfere with they they be seldom the first to break the 
 peace . then , why have they lain in wait for I , who have never injure 
 they ? could it possibly be that I have be betray ? I be still turn 
 over these question in my mind when we all come to a halt , and the 
 Captain blow a shrill note on a whistle which hang round his neck . 

 the place where we find ourselves be the dark and most rugged spot 
 in the whole wild gorge . on either side great cliff shoot up , which 
 arch over our head , with a fringe of fern and bracken on either lip , 
 so that the dark sky and the few twinkle star be well - nigh hid . 
 great black rock loom vaguely out in the shadowy light , while in 
 front a high tangle of what seem to be brushwood bar our road . at a 
 second whistle , however , a glint of light be see through the branch , 
 and the whole mass be swing to one side as though it move upon a 
 hinge . beyond it a dark wind passage open into the side of the 
 hill , down which we go with our back bow , for the rock ceiling be 
 of no great height . on every side of we sound the throbbing of the 
 sea . 

 pass through the entrance , which must have be dig with great labour 
 through the solid rock , we come out into a lofty and roomy cave , light 
 up by a fire at one end , and by several torch . by their smoky yellow 
 glare I could see that the roof be , at least , fifty foot above we , and 
 be hang by long lime - crystal , which sparkle and gleam with great 
 brightness . the floor of the cave be form of fine sand , as soft and 
 velvety as a Wilton carpet , slop down in a way which show that the 
 cave must at its mouth open upon the sea , which be confirm by the 
 booming and splash of the wave , and by the fresh salt air which 
 fill the whole cavern . no water could be see , however , as a sharp 
 turn cut off our view of the outlet . 

 in this rock - girt space , which may have be sixty pace long and 
 thirty across , there be gather great pile of cask , keg and case ; 
 musket , cutlass , stave , cudgel , and straw be litter about upon 
 the floor . at one end a high wood fire blaze merrily , cast strange 
 shadow along the wall , and sparkle like a thousand diamond among 
 the crystal on the roof . the smoke be carry away through a great 
 cleft in the rock . seat on box , or stretch on the sand round the 
 fire , there be seven or eight more of the band , who spring to their 
 foot and run eagerly towards we as we enter . 

 have ye get he ? ' they cry . ' do he indeed come ? have he attendant ? ' 

 ' he be here , and he be alone , ' the Captain answer . ' our hawser fetch 
 he off his horse as neatly as ever a gull be net by a cragsman . 
 what have ye do in our absence , Silas ! ' 

 ' we have the pack ready for carriage , ' say the man address , a 
 sturdy , weather - beat seaman of middle age . ' the silk and lace be do 
 in these square cover over with sack . the one I have mark " yarn " 
 and the other " jute"--a thousand of Mechlin to a hundred of the shiny . 
 they will sling over a mule 's back . Brandy , schnapp , Schiedam , and 
 Hamburg Goldwasser be all set out in due order . the ' baccy be in the 
 flat case over by the Black Drop there . a plaguey job we have carry 
 it all out , but here it be ship - shape at last , and the lugger float

 five - knot breeze . ' 

 ' any sign of the _ Fairy Queen _ ? ' ask the smuggler . 

 ' none . long John be down at the water 's edge look out for her 
 flash - light . this wind should bring she up if she have round 
 Combe - Martin Point . there be a sail about ten mile to the

 ' a King 's crawl - boat , ' say Captain Murgatroyd , with a sneer . ' we can not 
 hang the gauger until Venables bring up the _ Fairy Queen _ , for after 
 all it be one of his hand that be snackle . let he do his own dirty

 ' Tausend Blitzen ! ' cry the ruffian Dutchman , ' would it not be a kindly 
 grass to Captain Venables to chuck the gauger down the Black Drop ere he

 ' Zounds , man , be you in command or be I ? ' say the leader angrily . 
 ' bring the prisoner forward to the fire ! now , hark ye , dog of a

 ' it will carry he half - way to hell before the breath leave he , ' say 
 one . 

 ' it be an easy death than the Devizes gallow ! ' cry a second . 

 ' Nay , he shall have the gallow first ! ' a third shout . ' it be but his

 ' he hath not open his mouth since we take he , ' say the man who be

 ' I have be wait for a civil question after all this brawl and

 ' I swear that you be mistaken , ' I reply . ' I have never in my life 
 be in these part before . ' 

 ' fine word ! fine word ! ' cry another smuggler . ' Gauger or no , you

 ' your secret be safe with I , ' I answer . ' but if ye wish to murder I ,

 ' my faith ! ' say Murgatroyd . ' this be too tall talk for a gauger . he

 ' where be Gauger Westhouse ? ' he cry ; ' he have leave his mark on my arm . 
 Rat I , if the scar be heal yet . the sun be on our side of the wall

 ' not our man ! ' they cry , with a volley of curse . 

 ' why , this fellow would make two of the gauger , and leave enough over

 ' yes , hang he ! ' say Dutch Pete . ' sapperment ! be our cave to be the

 ' worth a jorum of rumbo , ' cry Dicon . ' by your leave , Captain , I would

 ' we find he lie on the road when we come from Daddy Mycroft 's , ' say

 ' have ye slack the hawser ? ' the Captain ask . 

 ' we cast one end loose and let it hang . ' 

 '' tis well . we must keep he for Captain Venables . but now , as to our 
 other prisoner : we must overhaul he and examine his paper , for so many 
 craft be sail under false colour that we must need be careful . 
 Hark ye , Mister Soldier ! what bring you to these part , and what king 
 do you serve ? for I hear there be a mutiny break out , and two skipper 
 claim equal rating in the old british ship . ' 

 ' I be serve under King Monmouth , ' I answer , see that the propose 
 search must end in the finding of my paper . 

 ' under King Monmouth ! ' cry the smuggler . ' nay , friend , that ring 
 somewhat false . the good King hath , I hear , too much need of his friend 
 in the south to let an able soldier go wander along the sea coast 
 like a cornish wrecker in a sou'-wester . ' 

 ' I bear despatch , ' say I , ' from the King 's own hand to Henry Duke 
 of Beaufort , at his castle at Badminton . Ye can find they in my inner 
 pocket , but I pray ye not to break the seal , lest it bring discredit 
 upon my mission . ' 

 ' Sir , ' cry the gauger , raise himself upon his elbow , ' I do hereby

 ' my horse ! ' I object . 

 ' it need not stop we . I can rig up a handy horse - stall with my spare

 Murgatroyd 's face flush with anger . ' you be a plain speaker , Captain 
 Clarke , ' say he . ' this be no murder . it be justice . what harm do we

 ' he be but a servant , I argue . ' he hath not make the law . it be his 
 duty to enforce it . it be with the law itself that your quarrel be . ' 

 ' you be right , ' say the smuggler gloomily . ' it be with Judge Moorcroft

 I have walk and smoke for an hour or more , when Silas the mate

 I could see by the dim light of the single torch which Murgatroyd hold

 I descend the steep stair which lead down into the low - roof cabin of

 Chapter XXIV . of the welcome that meet I at Badminton

 truly Providence work in strange way , my child , and until a

 ' and Vairmer Brown be mine -- John Brown by the register , but well

 Chapter XXV . of Strange Doings in the Boteler Dungeon 

 ' take down this fellow 's statement , ' say the Duke to his scrivener . 
 ' now , sirrah , it may not be know to you that his gracious Majesty the 
 King hath confer plenary power upon I during these troubled time , 
 and that I have his warrant to deal with all traitor without either 
 jury or judge . you do bear a commission , I understand , in the rebellious 
 body which be here describe as Saxon 's regiment of Wiltshire Foot ? 
 speak the truth for your neck 's sake . ' 

 ' I will speak the truth for the sake of something high than that , your 
 grace , ' I answer . ' I command a company in that regiment . ' 

 ' and who be this Saxon ? ' 

 ' I will answer all that I may concern myself , ' say I , ' but not a 
 word which may reflect upon other . ' 

 ' ha ! ' he roar , hot with anger . ' our pretty gentleman must need stand 
 upon the nicety of honour after take up arm against his king . I 
 tell you , sir , that your honour be in such a parlous state already that 
 you may well throw it over and look to your safety . the sun be sink 
 in the west . Ere it set your life , too , may have set for ever . ' 

 ' I be the keeper of my own honour , your grace , ' I answer . ' as to my

 of your household who may come into his power . this I say , not as a 
 threat , but as a warning , for I know he to be a man who be like to be 
 as good as his word . ' 

 ' your Colonel , as you call he , may find it hard enough to save himself 
 soon , ' the Duke answer with a sneer . ' how many man hath Monmouth with 
 he ? ' 

 I smile and shake my head . 

 ' how shall we make this traitor find his tongue ? ' he ask furiously , 
 turn to his council . 

 ' I should clap on the thumbikin , ' say one fierce - face old soldier . 

 ' I have know a lighted match between the finger work wonder , ' another 
 suggest . ' Sir Thomas Dalzell hath in the scottish war be able to

 idolater of a savage country . '

 send to look to his spiritual need ! ' 

 ' shall we take he to the strong room , your grace ? ' ask the Captain of

 he leave the cell , but presently unlock the door and push his dismal

 thus leave to myself I try to follow Captain Sinclair 's advice as to

 it be a very strange thing , my dear , to sit down in cold blood , and

 have give their last throb , and your soul have speed away upon its final 
 errand . strange and very awesome ! the man who rideth down into the

 sword - hilt can not feel this , for the human mind be such that one emotion

 experience of it , for the mind weaken with disease can but submit

 a young and hale man sit alone in quiet , and see present death hang

 I be seat with my head bow upon my breast , deeply bury in this

 ' Whoy , mun , ' he answer , ' I know as much of this ' ere house as Beaufort

 ' there use to be a hole in the roof . look up and see if you can see 
 aught of it . ' 

 ' it arch high above my head , ' I answer , look upwards ; ' but there 
 be no sign of any opening . ' 

 ' there be one , ' he repeat . ' my brother Roger hath swing himself down

 this kindly visit , short as it be , relieve my mind greatly , for I have

 ' here be your supper , friend , ' say he . ' take it down , appetite or no ,

 of good cheer ! folk may say as much of you . his grace be in a terrible 
 way . he walketh up and down , and biteth his lip , and clencheth his hand

 ' against the militia . but they will find it another thing when they have

 ' I shall do so , your grace . 

 ' tell I , ' he ask , ' how doth Monmouth bear himself in this 
 enterprise ? '

 ' strange , ' he murmur ; ' it be ever the jest at court that he have

 ' my sword ? ' I ask . 

 ' all your property be there . tell Monmouth what I have say , and let he

 ' Pshaw , man ! never fret about that ! I will off to Bristol at daybreak ,

 the path to the right lead through grove and past carp pond for a mile

 all that night I ride hard without draw bridle , through sleep

 ' Aye , and King Monmouth will be on thorn to see you . right about , my

 Chapter XXVI . of the Strife in the Council 

 King Monmouth 's council be assemble at the time of my coming , and my 
 entrance cause the utmost surprise and joy , as they have just hear news 
 of my sore danger . even the royal presence could not prevent several 
 member , among whom be the old Mayor and the two soldier of fortune , 
 from spring to their foot and shake I warmly by the hand . Monmouth 
 himself say a few gracious word , and request that I should be seat 
 at the board with the other . 

 ' you have earn the right to be of our council , ' say he ; ' and lest 
 there should be a jealousy amongst other captain that you should come 
 among we , I do hereby confer upon you the special title of Scout - master , 
 which , though it entail few if any duty in the present state of our 
 force , will yet give you precedence over your fellow . we have hear that 
 your greeting from Beaufort be of the roughest , and that you be in 
 sore strait in his dungeon . but you have happily come yourself on the 
 very heel of he who bear the tiding . tell we then from the beginning 
 how thing have fare with you . ' 

 I should have wish to have limit my story to Beaufort and his 
 message , but as the council seem to be intent upon hear a full 
 account of my journey , I tell in as short and simple speech as I 
 could the various passage which have befall I -- the ambuscado of 
 the smuggler , the cave , the capture of the gauger , the journey in the 
 lugger , the acquaintance with Farmer Brown , my be cast into prison , 
 with the manner of my release and the message wherewith I have be 
 commission . to all of this the council hearken with the uttermost 
 attention , while a muttered oath ever and anon from a courtier or a 
 groan and prayer from a Puritan show how keenly they follow the 
 various phase of my fortune . above all , they give the great heed 
 to Beaufort 's word , and stop I more than once when I appear to be 
 pass over any saying or event before they have due time to weigh 
 it . when I at last finish they all sit speechless , look into each 
 other 's face and wait for an expression of opinion . 

 ' on my word , ' say Monmouth at last , ' this be a young Ulysses , though 
 his Odyssey doth but take three day in the acting . scudery might not be 
 so dull be she to take a hint from these smuggler ' cave and slide 
 panel . how say you , Grey ? ' 

 ' he hath indeed have his share of adventure , ' the nobleman answer , ' and 
 hath also perform his mission like a fearless and zealous messenger . 
 you say that Beaufort give you nought in writing ? ' 

 ' not a word , my lord , ' I reply . 

 ' and his private message be that he wish we well , and would join we 
 if we be in his country ? ' 

 ' that be the effect , my lord . ' 

 ' yet in his council , as I understand , he do utter bitter thing against 
 we , put affront upon the king , and make light of his just claim 
 upon the fealty of his nobility ? ' 

 ' he do , ' I answer . 

 ' he would fain stand upon both side of the hedge at once , ' say King 
 Monmouth . ' such a man be very like to find himself on neither side , but 
 in the very heart of the briar . it may be as well , however , that we 
 should move his way , so as to give he the chance of declare himself . ' 

 ' in any case , as your Majesty remember , ' say Saxon , ' we have determine 
 to march Bristolwards and attempt the town . ' 

 ' the work be be strengthen , ' say I , ' and there be five thousand 
 of the Gloucestershire train - band assemble within . I see the labourer 
 at work upon the rampart as I pass . ' 

 ' if we gain Beaufort we shall gain the town , ' quoth Sir Stephen 
 Timewell . ' there be already a strong body of godly and honest folk 
 therein , who would rejoice to see a protestant army within their gate . 
 should we have to beleaguer it we may count upon some help from within . ' 

 ' Hegel und blitzen ! ' exclaim the german soldier , with an impatience 
 which even the presence of the King could not keep in bound ; ' how can 
 we talk of siege and leaguer when we have not a breaching - piece in the 
 army ? ' 

 ' the Lard will find we the breaching - piece , ' cry Ferguson , in his 
 strange , nasal voice . ' do the Lard no breach the too'er o ' Jericho 
 withoot the aid o ' gunpooder ? do the Lard no raise up the man Robert 
 Ferguson and presairve he through five - and - thairty indictment and 
 twa - and - twenty proclamation o ' the godless ? what be there he canna do ? 
 Hosannah ! Hosannah ! ' 

 ' the Doctor be right , ' say a square - faced , leather - skin English 
 Independent . ' we talk too much o ' carnal mean and worldly chance , 
 without lean upon that heavenly goodwill which should be to we as a 
 staff on stony and broken path . yes , gentleman , ' he continue , raise 
 his voice and glance across the table at some of the courtier , ' ye 
 may sneer at word of piety , but I say that it be you and those like you 
 who will bring down God 's anger upon this army . ' 

 ' and I say so too , ' cry another sectary fiercely . 

 ' and I , ' ' and I , ' shout several , with Saxon , I think , among they . 

 ' be it your wish , your Majesty , that we should be insult at your very 
 council board ? ' cry one of the courtier , spring to his foot with 
 a flushed face . ' how long be we to be subject to this insolence because 
 we have the religion of a gentleman , and prefer to practise it in the 
 privacy of our heart rather than at the street corner with these 
 pharisees ? ' 

 ' speak not against God 's saint , ' cry a Puritan , in a loud stern 
 voice . ' there be a voice within I which tell I that it be well to 
 strike thee dead -- yea , even in the presence of the king -- than to allow 
 thee to revile those who have be bear again . ' 

 several have spring to their foot on either side . hand be lay upon 
 sword - hilt , and glance as stern and as deadly as rapi thrust be

 the angry disputant to resume their seat . 

 ' how now , gentleman ? ' cry the King , his face dark with anger , when 
 silence be at last restore . ' be this the extent of my authority that 
 ye should babble and brawl as though my council - chamber be a Fleet 
 Street pot - house ? be this your respect for my person ? I tell ye that I 
 would forfeit my just claim for ever , and return to Holland , or devote

 know how to deal with he . let each preach to his own , but let he not

 and you , Mr. Joyce , and you also , Sir Henry Nuttall , we shall hold ye

 may await our enterprise in those part . ' 

 the King bow as a sign that the formal meeting be over , and take 
 Lord Grey aside , he converse with he anxiously in a recess . the 
 courtier , who number in their party several English and foreign

 indeed , religious dissension and sectarian heat be in the very air .

 ' the leaven be work , ' quoth he . ' something will come of all this 
 ferment . ' 

 ' I see not what can come of it save disorder and weakness , ' I answer . 

 ' good soldier will come of it , lad , ' say he . ' they be all sharpen 
 themselves , each after his own fashion , on the whetstone of religion . 
 this argue breedeth fanatic , and fanatic be the stuff out of which 
 conqueror be fashion . have you not hear how Old Noll 's army divide 
 into Presbyterians , Independents , Ranters , Anabaptists , Fifth Monarchy 
 man , brownist , and a score of other sect , out of whose strife rise the 
 fine regiment that ever form line upon a field of battle ? 

           " such as do build their faith upon 
            the holy text of sword and gun . " 

 you know old Samuel 's couplet . I tell you , I would rather see they thus 
 employ than at their drill , for all their wrangling and jangling . ' 

 ' but how of this split in the council ? ' I ask . 

 ' ah , that be indeed a graver matter . all creed may be weld together , 
 but the Puritan and the scoffer be like oil and water . yet the Puritan 
 be the oil , for he will be ever atop . these courtier do but stand for 
 themselves , while the other be back up by the pith and marrow of the 
 army . it be well that we be afoot to - morrow . the King 's troop be , I 
 hear , pour across Salisbury Plain , but their ordnance and store be 
 delay they , for they know well that they must bring all they need ,

 friend Buyse , wie geht es ? ' 

 ' Ganz gut , ' say the big German , loom up before we through the 
 darkness . ' but , sapperment , what a cawing and croaking , like a rookery 
 at sunset ! you English be a strange people -- yes , donnerwetter , a very 
 strange people ! there be no two of you who think alike upon any subject 
 under Himmel ! the Cavalier will have his gay coat and his loose word . 
 the Puritan will cut your throat rather than give up his sad - colour 
 dress and his Bible . " King James ! " cry some , " King Monmouth ! " say the 
 peasant . " King Jesus ! " say the Fifth Monarchy man . " no King at all ! "

 set foot on the Helderenbergh at Amsterdam , my head hath be in a whirl 
 with try to understand what it be that ye desire , for before I have 
 get to the end of one man 's tale , and begin to see a little through the 
 finsterniss , another will come with another story , and I be in as evil a

 ' mine eyelid be in truth a little heavy , ' I answer . ' save for an 
 hour or two aboard the lugger , and about as long on a prison couch , I 
 have not close eye since I leave the camp . ' 

 ' we shall fall in at the second bugle call , about eight of the clock , ' 
 say Saxon . ' we shall leave you , therefore , that you may restore 
 yourself after your fatigue . ' with a parting nod the two old soldier 
 stride off together down the crowded Fore Street , while I make the good 
 of my way back to the Mayor 's hospitable dwelling , where I have to repeat 
 my story all over again to the assemble household before I be at last 
 suffer to seek my room . 



 Chapter XXVII . of the Affair near Keynsham Bridge 

 Monday , June 21 , 1685 , break very dark and windy , with dull cloud 
 move heavily across the sky and a constant sputter of rain . yet a 
 little after daybreak Monmouth 's bugle be blow in every quarter 
 of the town , from Tone Bridge to Shuttern , and by the hour appoint the 
 regiment have muster , the roll have be call , and the vanguard be 
 march briskly out through the eastern gate . it go forth in the same 
 order as it enter , our own regiment and the Taunton burgher bring 
 up the rear . Mayor Timewell and Saxon have the ordering of this part of 
 the army between they , and be man who have see much service , they 
 draw the ordnance into a less hazardous position , and place a strong 
 guard of horse , a cannon 's shot in the rear , to meet any attempt of the 
 Royal dragoon . 

 it be remark on all side that the army have improve in order and 
 discipline during the three day ' halt , owe perchance to the example 
 of our own unceasing drill and soldierly bear . in number it have 
 increase to nigh eight thousand , and the man be well fed and light 
 of heart . with sturdy close - locked rank they splash their way through 
 mud and puddle , with many a rough country joke and many a lusty stave 
 from song or hymn . Sir Gervas ride at the head of his musqueteer , whose 
 befloure tail hang limp and lank with the water dripping from 
 they . Lockarby 's pikeman and my own company of scythesman be mostly 
 labourer from the country , who be harden against all weather , and 
 plod patiently along with the rain - drop glistening upon their ruddy 
 face . in front be the Taunton foot ; behind , the lumber train of 
 baggage waggon , with the horse in the rear of they . so the long line 
 wind its way over the hill . 

 at the summit , where the road begin to dip down upon the other side , a 
 halt be call to enable the regiment to close up , and we look back 
 at the fair town which many of we be never to see again . from the dark 
 wall and house roof we could still mark the flapping and flutter of 
 white kerchief from those whom we leave behind . Reuben sit his horse 
 beside I , with his spare shirt streaming in the wind and his great 
 pikemen all agrin behind he , though his thought and his eye be 
 too far away to note they . as we gaze , a long thin quiver of sunshine

 Magdalene tower , with the Royal standard which still wave from it . the 
 incident be hail as a happy augury , and a great shout spread from 
 rank to rank at the sight of it , with a waving of hat and a clattering 
 of weapon . then the bugle blow a fanfare , the drum strike up a point 
 of war , Reuben thrust his shirt into his haversack , and on we march 
 through mud and slush , with the dreary cloud bend low over we , and 
 buttress by the no less dreary hill on either side . a seeker for 
 omen might have say that the heavens be weep over our ill - fat 
 venture . 

 all day we trudge along road which be quagmire , over our ankle 
 in mud , until in the evening we make our way to Bridgewater , where we 
 gain some recruit , and also some hundred pound for our military 
 chest , for it be a well - to - do place , with a thriving coast trade 
 carry on down the River Parret . after a night in snug quarter we set 
 off again in even bad weather than before . the country in these part 
 be a quagmire in the dry season , but the heavy rain have cause the 
 fen to overflow , and turn they into broad lake on either side of the 
 road . this may have be to some degree in our favour , as shield we 
 from the raid of the King 's cavalry , but it make our march very 
 slow . all day it be splash and swash through mud and mire , the 
 rain - drop shin on the gun - barrel and dripping from the heavy - footed 
 horse . past the swollen Parret , through Eastover , by the peaceful 
 village of Bawdrip , and over Polden Hill we make our way , until the 
 bugle sound a halt under the grove of Ashcot , and a rude meal be 
 serve out to the man . then on again , through the pitiless rain , past 
 the woode park of Piper 's Inn , through Walton , where the flood be 
 threaten the cottage , past the orchard of Street , and so in the 
 dusk of the evening into the grey old town of Glastonbury , where the 
 good folk do their good by the warmth of their welcome to atone for the 
 bitterness of the weather . 

 the next morning be wet still and inclement , so the army make a short 
 march to Wells , which be a good - sized town , well lay out , with a fine 
 cathedral , which hath a great number of figure carve in stone and 
 place in niche on the outer side , like that which we see at Salisbury . 
 the townsfolk be strong for the protestant cause , and the army be so 
 well receive that their victual cost little from the military chest . on 
 this march we first begin to come into touch with the Royal horse . More 
 than once when the rain mist clear we see the gleam of arm upon the 
 low hill which overlook the road , and our scout come in with report 
 of strong body of dragoon on either flank . at one time they mass 
 heavily upon our rear , as though plan a descent upon the baggage . 
 Saxon , however , plant a regiment of pike on either side , so that they 
 break up again and glint off over the hill . 

 from Wells we march upon the twenty - fourth to Shepton Mallet , with the 
 ominous sabre and helmet still twinkle behind and on either side of 
 we . 

 that evening we be at Keynsham Bridge , less than two league from 
 Bristol as the crow fly , and some of our horse ford the river and 
 push on almost to the wall . 

 by morning the rain cloud have at last clear , so Reuben and I ride 
 slowly up one of the slop green hill which rise behind the camp , in 
 the hope of gain some sight of the enemy . our man we leave litter 
 about upon the grass , try to light fire with the damp stick , or 
 lay out their clothe to dry in the sunshine . a strange - look band 
 they be , coat and splash with mud from head to heel , their hat 
 all limp and draggle , their arm rust , and their boot so wear that 
 many walk barefoot , and other have swathe their kerchief round 
 their foot . yet their short spell of soldiering have change they 
 from honest - face yokel into fierce - eye , half - shaven , gaunt - cheek 
 fellow , who could carry arm or port pike as though they have do 
 nought else since childhood . 

 the plight of the officer be no well than that of the man , nor 
 should an officer , my dear , when he be upon service , ever demean 
 himself by partake of any comfort which all can not share with he . let 
 he lie by a soldier 's fire and eat a soldier 's fare , or let he hence , 
 for he be a hindrance and a stumbling - block . our clothe be pulp , our

 gain his sleep , hath ever be a mystery to I , for day after day

 remove . at his saddle - bow he bear with he the great flour dredger 
 which we see he use at Taunton , and his honest musqueteer have their 
 head duly dust every morning , though in an hour their tail would 
 be as brown as nature make they , while the flour would be trickle in

 the Baronet , but our comrade prove the victor . 

 ' there be a time when I be call plump Reuben , ' quoth my friend , as

 October . I would , Micah , that you would wring I out and hang I to dry 
 upon one of these bush . ' 

 ' if we be wet , King James 's man must be wetter , ' say I , ' for at least 
 we have have such shelter as there be . '

 sword - belt on Monday , two on Tuesday , one yesterday , and one to - day . I 
 tell you , I be thaw like an icicle in the sun . '

 all in importance , and become a man of weight and substance . ' 

 ' I have more substance and weight ere I begin trail over the 
 countryside like a Hambledon packman , ' quoth he . ' but in very truth and

 support it . I feel as if she be my own high self , my loftier

 ' but have you speak to the old man ? ' I ask . ' be you indeed 
 betroth ? '

 no time for such foolery , and he bid I wait until King Monmouth be

 call such thing foolery fifty year ago , when he go a - court 
 himself . ' 

 ' at least he do not refuse you , ' say I. ' it be as good as a promise 
 that ; should the cause be successful , you shall be so too . ' 

 ' by my faith , ' cry Reuben , ' if a man could by his own single blade 
 bring that about , there be none who hath so strong an interest in it as

 raise his eye to his master 's daughter , and the old man be ready to

 ' and how take he this mild intimation ? ' I ask . 

 ' as a rat take a rat trap . snarl out some few word of godly hatred , 
 and so slunk away . ' 

 ' on my life , lad , ' say I , ' you have be have as many adventure in 
 your own way as I in mine . but here we be upon the hill - top , with as 
 fair an outlook as man could wish to have . ' 

 just beneath we run the Avon , curve in long bend through the 
 woodland , with the gleam of the sun strike back from it here and

 on the further side the peaceful , many - hue country , rise and fall

 a road run along the Somersetshire bank of the Avon , and down this two

 for a hundred pace , the dense throng be split asunder , and the Royal 
 Guards come pour through the rent , and swerve off to right and leave 
 through hedge and over ditch , stab and hack at the flee

 hell blast which have break so suddenly upon we . 

 as the Blues retire we observe that a single officer bring up the

 steadily grow great , yet he make no effort to quicken his pace ,

 ' this path , ' cry he eagerly . ' it bring we out beyond the grove , and 
 be in the hollow all the way . ' 

 ' lead the horse until we get on well ground , ' I answer . ' we may 
 just cut he off if we be lucky . ' 

 there be no time for another word , for we hurry off down the uneven

 he be a sun - burn , high - feature man , with black mustachio , mount

 hath tattooed my face with powder until I shall be take for Solomon 
 Sprent 's young brother . '

 ' I would not make sport of a brave man , ' I answer . ' if you will give

 ' willingly , ' say he , scramble down and arrange his ruffled attire .

 King Monmouth should know of this with all speed . '

 the council break up forthwith , every man rid off to his own

 Chapter XXVIII . of the Fight in Wells Cathedral 

 I be fairly tie to the chariot - wheel of history now , my dear child , 
 and must follow on with name and place and date , whether my tale suffer 
 by it or no . with such a drama as this afoot it be impertinent to 
 speak of myself , save in so far as I see or hear what may make these 
 old scene more vivid to you . it be no pleasant matter for I to dwell 
 upon , yet , convince as I be that there be no such thing as chance 
 either in the great or the little thing of this world , I be very sure 
 that the sacrifice of these brave man be not throw away , and that 
 their striving be not as profitless as might at first sight appear . 
 if the perfidious race of Stuart be not now seat upon the throne , and 
 if religion in England be still a thing of free growth , we may , to my 
 thinking , thank these Somerset yokel for it , who first show how small 
 a thing would shake the throne of an unpopular monarch . Monmouth 's 
 army be but the vanguard of that which march throe year later into 
 London , when James and his cruel minister be fly as outcast over 
 the face of the earth . 

 on the night of June 27 , or rather early in the morning of June 28 , we 
 reach the town of Frome , very wet and miserable , for the rain have come 
 on again , and all the road be quagmire . from this next day we push 
 on once more to Wells , where we spend the night and the whole of the 
 next day , to give the man time to get their clothe dry , and to recover 
 themselves after their privation . 

 in the forenoon a parade of our Wiltshire regiment be hold in the 
 Cathedral Close , when Monmouth praise it , as it well deserve , for the 
 soldierly progress make in so short a time . 

 as we return to our quarter after dismiss our man we come upon a 
 great throng of the rough Bagworthy and Oare miner , who be assemble 
 in the open space in front of the Cathedral , listen to one of their 
 own number , who be address they from a cart . the wild and frenzied 
 gesture of the man show we that he be one of those extreme sectary 
 whose religion run perilously near to madness . the hum and groan 
 which rise from the crowd prove , however , that his fiery word be 
 well suited to his hearer , so we halt on the verge of the multitude 
 and hearken to his address . a red - bearded , fierce - faced man he be , 
 with tangle shaggy hair tumble over his gleam eye , and a hoarse 
 voice which resound over the whole square . 

 ' what shall we not do for the Lord ? ' he cry ; ' what shall we not do for 
 the Holy of Holies ? why be it that his hand be heavy upon we ? why be it 
 that we have not free this land , even as Judith free Bethulia ? Behold , 
 we have look for peace but no good come , and for a time of health , and 
 behold trouble ! why be this , I say ? truly , brother , it be because we 
 have slight the Lord , because we have not be wholehearte towards 
 Him . Lo ! we have praise he with our breath , but in our deed we have 
 be cold towards he . ye know well that Prelacy be an accursed thing -- a 
 hissing and an abomination in the eye of the Almighty ! yet what have 
 we , his servant , work for he in this matter ? have we not see 
 Prelatist church , church of form and of show , where the creature be 
 confound with the Creator -- have we not see they , I say , and have we 
 not forborne to sweep they away , and so lend our sanction to they ? there 
 be the sin of a lukewarm and back - slide generation ! there be the cause 
 why the Lord should look coldly upon his people ! lo ! at Shepton and at 
 Frome we have leave such church behind we . at Glastonbury , too , we have 
 spar those wicked wall which be rear by idolatrous hand of old .

 ' shall we pluck it down , then , until no one stone be leave upon another ? ' 

 ' yes , yes ! ' they shout . 

 ' now , at once ? ' 

 ' yes , yes ! '

 the riot have now change into open mutiny among these marshman and

 I can say nothing of what take place to right or leave of I during the

 in the meantime all the troop have assemble and the mutiny be swiftly

 a parade of our whole force be hold in the field outside the town upon

 ' what say you , Wade ! ' he cry . ' be we never to see a smile on that sad

 chapter XXIX . of the great Cry from the Lonely House 

 and so our weary marching and counter - marching come at last to an end , 
 and we find ourselves with our back fairly against the wall , and the 
 whole strength of the Government turn against we . not a word come 
 to we of a rise or movement in our favour in any part of England . 
 everywhere the Dissenters be cast into prison and the Church dominant . 
 from north and east and west the militia of the county be on its 
 march against we . in London six regiment of dutch troop have arrive as 
 a loan from the Prince of Orange . other be say to be on their way . 
 the City have enrol ten thousand man . everywhere there be muster 
 and march to succour the flower of the english army , which be 
 already in Somersetshire . and all for the purpose of crush some five 
 or six thousand clodhopper and fisherman , half - armed and penniless , who 
 be ready to throw their life away for a man and for an idea . 

 but this idea , my dear child , be a noble one , and one which a man 
 might very well sacrifice all for , and yet feel that all be well spend . 
 for though these poor peasant , in their dumb , blunder fashion , would 
 have find it hard to give all their reason in word , yet in the inmost 
 heart of they they know and feel that it be England 's cause which they 
 be fight for , and that they be uphold their country 's true 
 self against those who would alter the old system under which she have 
 lead the nation . three more year make all this very plain , and show 
 that our simple unlettered follower have see and judge the sign of 
 the time more correctly than those who call themselves their better . 
 there be , to my thinking , stage of human progress for which the Church 
 of Rome be admirably suit . where the mind of a nation be young , it may 
 be good that it should not concern itself with spiritual affair , but 
 should lean upon the old staff of custom and authority . but England have 
 cast off her swaddling - clothe , and be a nursery of strong , think 
 man , who would bow to no authority save that which their reason and 
 conscience approve . it be hopeless , useless , foolish , to try to drive 
 such man back into a creed which they have outgrow . such an attempt be , 
 however , be make , back by all the weight of a bigoted king with a 
 powerful and wealthy Church as his ally . in three year the nation would 
 understand it , and the King would be fly from his angry people ; but 
 at present , sink in a torpor after the long civil war and the corrupt 
 reign of Charles , they fail to see what be at stake , and turn 
 against those who would warn they , as a hasty man turn on the messenger 
 who be the bearer of evil tiding . be it not strange , my dear , how 
 quickly a mere shadowy thought come to take living form , and grow into 
 a very tragic reality ? at one end of the chain be a king brood over a 
 point of doctrine ; at the other be six thousand desperate man , chivy 
 and chase from shire to shire , stand to bay at last amid the bleak 
 bridgewater marsh , with their heart as bitter and as hopeless as 
 those of hunt beast of prey . a king 's theology be a dangerous thing 
 for his subject . 

 but if the idea for which these poor man fight be a worthy one , what 
 shall we say of the man who have be choose as the champion of their 
 cause ? alas , that such man should have have such a leader ! swing from 
 the height of confidence to the depth of despair , choose his future 
 council of state one day and propose to fly from the army on the 
 next , he appear from the start to be possess by the very spirit 
 of fickleness . yet he have bear a fair name before this enterprise . in 
 Scotland he have win golden opinion , not only for his success , but for 
 the moderation and mercy with which he treat the vanquished . on the 
 continent he have command an english brigade in a way that earn 
 praise from old soldier of Louis and the empire . yet now , when his own 
 head and his own fortune be at stake , he be feeble , irresolute , and 
 cowardly . in my father 's phrase , ' all the virtue have go out of he . ' 
 I declare when I have see he rid among his troop , with his head 
 bow upon his breast and a face like a mute at a burying , cast an 
 air of gloom and of despair all round he , I have feel that , even in 
 case of success , such a man could never wear the crown of the Tudors and 
 the Plantagenets , but that some strong hand , be it that of one of 
 his own general , would wrest it from he . 

 I will do Monmouth the justice to say that from the time when it be at 
 last decide to fight -- for the very good reason that no other course be 
 open -- he show up in a more soldierly and manly spirit . for the first 
 few day in July no mean be neglect to hearten our troop and to 
 nerve they for the come battle . from morning to night we be at work , 
 teach our foot how to form up in dense group to meet the charge of 
 horse , and how to depend upon each other , and look to their officer for 
 order . at night the street of the little town from the Castle Field 
 to the Parret Bridge resound with the praying and the preaching . there 
 be no need for the officer to quell irregularity , for the troop 
 punish they amongst themselves . one man who come out on the street 
 hot with wine be well - nigh hang by his companion , who finally cast 
 he out of the town as be unworthy to fight in what they look 
 upon as a sacred quarrel . as to their courage , there be no occasion to 
 quicken that , for they be as fearless as lion , and the only danger 
 be lest their fiery daring should lead they into foolhardiness . their 
 desire be to hurl themselves upon the enemy like a horde of Moslem 
 fanatic , and it be no easy matter to drill such hot - head fellow 
 into the steadiness and caution which war demand . 

 provision run low upon the third day of our stay in Bridgewater , which 
 be due to our have exhaust that part of the country before , and 
 also to the vigilance of the Royal Horse , who scour the district round 
 and cut off our supply . Lord Grey determine , therefore , to send 
 out two troop of horse under cover of night , to do what they could to 
 refill the larder . the command of the small expedition be give over 
 to Major Martin Hooker , an old Lifeguardsman of rough speech and curt 
 manner , who have do good service in drill the headstrong farmer 
 and yeoman into some sort of order . Sir Gervas Jerome and I ask leave 
 from Lord Grey to join the foray -- a favour which be readily grant , 
 since there be little stirring in the town . 

 it be about eleven o'clock on a moonless night that we sally out 
 of Bridgewater , intend to explore the country in the direction of 
 Boroughbridge and Athelney . we have word that there be no large body 
 of the enemy in that quarter , and it be a fertile district where

 that ride come very clearly before I as I think of it . the dark loom

 ' in the country , ' say I , laugh , ' we have ever the feeling that the

 ' Ventre Saint - Gris ! it be little knowledge or wisdom that I acquire

 ' but , ' say I , ' when you be a wealthy man you must have be of 
 service to some one , for how could one spend so much money and yet none 
 be the well ? ' 

 ' you dear bucolic Micah ! ' he cry , with a gay laugh . ' you will ever 
 speak of my poor fortune with bated breath and in an awestruck voice , as 
 though it be the wealth of the Indies . you can not think , lad , how easy 
 it be for a money - bag to take unto itself wing and fly . it be true that

 we , I feel that in hatch such a poisonous brood our money hath do

 when I have hold my levee , cringe up to my bedside-- ' 

 ' your bedside ! ' I exclaim . 

 ' Aye ! it be the mode to receive in bed , attire in laced cambric 
 shirt and periwig , though afterwards it be permit to sit up in your 
 chamber , but dress _ a la negligence _ , in gown and slipper . the mode 
 be a terrible tyrant , Clarke , though its arm may not extend as far as 
 Havant . the idle man of the town must have some rule of life , so he

 subject to it than myself . I be regular in my irregularity , and 
 orderly in my disorder . at eleven o'clock to the stroke , up come my 
 valet with the morning cup of hippocras , an excellent thing for the 
 qualm , and some slight refection , as the breast of an ortolan or wing 
 of a widgeon . then come the levee , twenty , thirty , or forty of the class

 Chapter XXX . of the Swordsman with the Brown Jacket 

 the sergeant , who be a great raw - boned west - countryman , push the gate

 the room be large and lofty , with long row of ham and salt meat

 ' and what come of it ? ' I ask , for our new acquaintance be laugh

 ' well , my three friend run down their hare , and pull out their

 private sport . " Nay , I be not chase your hare , gentleman , " say I. 
 " what then , fellow ? " ask one of they . " why , marry , I be chase you , "

 ' do they not raise the country on your track ? ' I ask . 

 ' Nay ! when Brown Alice be give her head she fly fast than the news . 
 Rumour spread quick , but the good mare 's stride be quick still . ' 

 ' and here we be within our own outpost , ' quoth Sir Gervas . ' now , mine 
 honest friend -- for honest you have be to we , whatever other may 
 say of you -- will you not come with we , and strike in for a good cause ? 
 Zounds , man ! you have many an ill deed to atone for , I will warrant . 
 why not add one good one to your account , by risk your life for the 
 reform faith ? ' 

 ' not i , ' the highwayman answer , rein up his horse . ' my own skin be 
 nothing , but why should I risk my mare in such a fool 's quarrel ? should 
 she come to harm in the ruffle , where could I get such another ? besides , 
 it matter nothing to she whether Papist or Protestant sit on the 
 throne of England -- do it , my beauty ? ' 

 ' but you might chance to gain preferment , ' I say . ' our Colonel , Decimus 
 Saxon , be one who love a good swordsman , and his word hath power with 
 King Monmouth and the council . ' 

 ' Nay , nay ! ' cry Hector Marot gruffly . ' let every man stick to his own 
 trade . Kirke 's horse I be ever ready to have a brush with , for a party

 to you ! ' 

 ' farewell , farewell ! ' we cry , press his brown horny hand ; ' our 
 thank to you for your guidance . ' raise his hat , he shake his bridle

 ' rat I , if I ever say a word against the thief again ! ' say Sir 
 Gervas . ' I never see a man wield sword more deftly in my life , and he 
 must be a rare hand with a pistol to bring those two tall fellow down

 red - coat ? ' 

 ' surely I can , ' I answer , gaze out over the broad , reedy , 
 dead - colour plain , which extend from the other side of the winding 
 Parret to the distant Polden Hills . ' I can see they over yonder in the 
 direction of Westonzoyland , as bright as the poppy among corn . ' 

 ' there be more upon the left , near Chedzoy , ' quoth Sir Gervas . ' one , 
 two , three , and one yonder , and two other behind -- six regiment of foot

 sign of ordnance too . faith ! Monmouth must fight now , if he ever hop 
 to feel the gold rim upon his temple . the whole of King James 's army 
 hath close upon he . ' 

 ' we must get back to our command , then , ' I answer . ' if I mistake not , 
 I see the flutter of our standard in the market - place . ' we spur our 
 weary steed forward , and make our way with our little party and the

 comrade . before noon the drove of bullock have be change into joint

 furnish the last dinner which many of our man be ever destine to eat . 
 Major Hooker come in shortly after with a good store of provision , but 
 in no very good case , for he have have a skirmish with the dragoon , and 
 have lose eight or ten of his man . he bear a complaint straightway to the 
 council concern the manner in which we have desert he ; but great 
 event be come fast upon we now , and there be small time to inquire 
 into petty matter of discipline . for myself , I freely confess , look 
 back on it , that as a soldier he be entirely in the right , and that 
 from a strict military point of view our conduct be not to be excuse . 
 yet I trust , my dear , even now , when year have weigh I down , that 
 the scream of a woman in distress would be a signal which would draw I 
 to her aid while these old limb could bear I . for the duty which 
 we owe to the weak override all other duty and be superior to all 
 circumstance , and I for one can not see why the coat of the soldier 
 should harden the heart of the man . 



 chapter XXXI . of the Maid of the Marsh and the bubble which rise from 
 the Bog 

 all Bridgewater be in a ferment as we ride in , for King James 's force 
 be within four mile , on the Sedgemoor Plain , and it be likely that 
 they would push on at once and storm the town . some rude work have be 
 throw up on the Eastover side , behind which two brigade be draw 
 up in arm , while the rest of the army be hold in reserve in the 
 market - place and Castle Field . towards afternoon , however , party of 
 our horse and peasant from the fen country come in with the news that 
 there be no fear of an assault be attempt . the royal troop have 
 quarter themselves snugly in the little village of the neighbourhood , 
 and have levy contribution of cider and of beer from the farmer , 
 they show no sign of any wish to advance . 

 the town be full of woman , the wife , mother , and sister of our 
 peasant , who have come in from far and near to see their love one once 
 more . Fleet Street or Cheapside upon a busy day be not more crowded 
 than be the narrow street and lane of the Somersetshire town . 
 Jack - boot , buff - coat trooper ; scarlet militiaman ; brown , 
 stern - faced Tauntonians ; serge - clothe pikemen ; wild , ragged miner ; 
 smockfrocke yokel ; reckless , weather - tan seaman ; gaunt cragsman 
 from the northern coast -- all push and jostle each other in a thick , 
 many - colour crowd . everywhere among they be the country woman , 
 straw - bonnet and loud - tongue , weeping , embrace , and exhort . 
 here and there amid the motley dress and gleam of arm move the dark , 
 sombre figure of a Puritan minister , with sweeping sad - coloured mantle 
 and penthouse hat , scatter abroad short fiery ejaculation and stern 
 pithy text of the old fighting order , which warm the man 's blood like 
 liquor . ever and anon a sharp , fierce shout would rise from the people , 
 like the yelp of a high - spirited hound which be strain at its leash 
 and hot to be at the throat of its enemy . 

 our regiment have be take off duty whenever it be clear that 
 Feversham do not mean to advance , and they be now busy upon the 
 victual which our night - foray have furnish . it be a Sunday , fresh and 
 warm , with a clear , unclouded sky , and a gentle breeze , sweet with the 
 smack of the country . all day the bell of the neighbouring village 
 ring out their alarm , peal their music over the sunlit countryside . 
 the upper window and red - tile roof of the house be crowd with 
 pale - face woman and child , who peer out to eastward , where the 
 splotch of crimson upon the dun - coloured moor mark the position of 
 our enemy . 

 at four o'clock Monmouth hold a last council of war upon the square 
 tower out of which spring the steeple of Bridgewater parish church , 
 whence a good view can be obtain of all the country round . since my 
 ride to Beaufort I have always be honour with a summon to attend , in 
 spite of my humble rank in the army . there be some thirty councillor 
 in all , as many as the space would hold , soldier and courtier , 
 Cavaliers and Puritans , all draw together now by the bond of a common 
 danger . indeed , the near approach of a crisis in their fortune have 
 break down much of the distinction of manner which have serve to 
 separate they . the sectary have lose something of his austerity and 
 become flushed and eager at the prospect of battle , while the giddy man 
 of fashion be hush into unwonted gravity as he consider the danger 
 of his position . their old feud be forget as they gather on the 
 parapet and gaze with set face at the thick column of smoke which 
 rise along the sky - line . 

 King Monmouth stand among his chief , pale and haggard , with the 
 dishevel , unkempt look of a man whose distress of mind have make he

 twitch until it be grievous to watch he . Lord Grey hand his own

 and star long and earnestly at the enemy . 

 ' they be the very man I have myself lead , ' say Monmouth at last , in a 
 low voice , as though utter his thought aloud . ' over yonder at the 
 right I see Dumbarton 's foot . I know these man well . they will fight . 
 have we they with we all would be well . ' 

 ' Nay , your Majesty , ' Lord Grey answer with spirit , ' you do your brave 
 follower an injustice . they , too , will fight to the last drop of their 
 blood in your quarrel . ' 

 ' look down at they ! ' say Monmouth sadly , point at the swarm 
 street beneath we . ' braver heart never beat in english breast , yet do 
 but mark how they brabble and clamour like clown on a Saturday night . 
 compare they with the stern , orderly array of the train battalion . 
 alas ! that I should have drag these honest soul from their little 
 home to fight so hopeless a battle ! ' 

 ' Hark at that ! ' cry Wade . ' they do not think it hopeless , nor do we . ' 
 as he speak a wild shout rise from the dense crowd beneath , who be 
 listen to a preacher who be hold forth from a window . 

 ' it be worthy Doctor Ferguson , ' say Sir Stephen Timewell , who have 
 just come up . ' he be as one inspire , powerfully bear onwards in his 
 discourse . verily he be even as one of the prophet of old . he have 
 choose for his text , " the Lord God of god he knoweth and Israel he 
 shall know . if it be in rebellion or if in transgression against the 
 Lord , save we not this day . " ' 

 ' amen , amen ! ' cry several of the puritan soldier devoutly , while 
 another hoarse burst of shout from below , with the clashing of 
 scythe - blade and the clatter of arm , show how deeply the people be 
 move by the burn word of the fanatic . 

 ' they do indeed seem to be hot for battle , ' say Monmouth , with a more 
 sprightly look . ' it may be that one who have command regular troop , as 
 I have do , be prone to lay too much weight upon the difference which 
 discipline and training make . these brave lad seem high of heart . what 
 think you of the enemy 's disposition , Colonel Saxon ? ' 

 ' by my faith , I think very little of they , your Majesty , ' Saxon answer

 ' if it please your Majesty , there be something in their order which

 friend ? ' 

 ' I think with Major Hollis , your Majesty , that Feversham by his

 a general chorus of approval from the whole council show that the

 ' have any cavalier anything to say against this plan ? ' ask the King .

 at this simple and kindly speech a flush of devotion , mingle in my

 Saxon nod . 

 ' be desirous of see all that I could of the enemy , I clamber up

 ' because you have no beard upon your face . why , granny hath more hair

 answer . ' the cavalier and the butcher become too near of kin , as I

 ' he come on apace , Micah , ' quoth my comrade , who be still stand

 there be indeed something in his lean , black - clothe figure and swift

 ' we have wait some time for you , Master John Derrick , ' say I. ' you 
 must now return with we to the town . ' 

 ' on what ground do you arrest I ? ' he ask , in hoarse , broken tone . 
 ' where be your warranty ? who hath give you a commission to molest 
 traveller upon the King 's highway ? ' 

 ' I have my Colonel 's commission , ' I answer shortly . ' you have be 
 once already to Feversham 's camp this morning . ' 

 ' it be a lie , ' he snarl fiercely . ' I do but take a stroll to enjoy the

 ' it be the truth , ' say Reuben . ' I see you myself on your return . let we 
 see that paper which peep from your doublet . '

 ' I thank you for your kindly protection , ' he reply , in the same white ,

 Ruth , ' he say , present the weapon in his left hand , ' and this ! ' he

 it be do in an instant -- so suddenly that I have neither time to spring

 it be sudden and silent , as though some strange monster of the marsh

 morass be bury the bone of the traitor and the spy . 

 I make my way as good I could through the oozy cling mud to the

 ' I trust that you have not slay he , Micah , ' he say of a sudden ,

 ' a high power than our have judge he , Reuben , ' I answer .

 as it chance , Decimus Saxon have find time to ascend the church tower

 he for the word . ' the blade have glance on a rib , though the lung be 
 slightly tear . we shall hear he back with we to the town . ' 

 ' you hear what he say , ' say Saxon kindly . ' he be a man whose opinion 
 be of weight-- 

           " a skilful leach be well far , 
            than half a hundred man of war . " 

 cheer up , man ! you be as white as though it be your blood and not his 
 which be drain away . where be Derrick ? ' 

 ' drown in the marsh , ' I answer . 

 '' tis well ! it will save we six foot of good hemp . but our position here 
 be somewhat expose , since the Royal Horse might make a dash at we . who 
 be this little maid who sit so white and still in the corner . ' 

 '' tis the guardian of the house . her granny have leave she here . '

 over . ' 

 ' Nay , I must wait for granny , ' she answer , with the tear run down

 ' but how if I take you to granny , little one , ' say I. ' we can not

 I soothe the little trembling thing as good I might , and bear she off 
 with I upon my shoulder . the scythesman have pass the handle of their
