 who promise to restore she to her home when the trouble be over . 



 Chapter XXXII . of the Onfall at Sedgemoor 

 however press our own private grief and need , we have little time 
 now to dwell upon they , for the moment be at hand which be to decide 
 for the time not only our own fate , but that of the protestant cause 
 in England . none of we make light of the danger . nothing less than a 
 miracle could preserve we from defeat , and most of we be of opinion 
 that the day of the miracle be past . other , however , think 
 otherwise . I believe that many of our Puritans , have they see the 
 heaven open that night , and the army of the Seraphim and the Cherubim 
 descend to our aid , would have look upon it as by no mean a 
 wonderful or unexpected occurrence . 

 the whole town be loud with the preaching . every troop or company have 
 its own choose orator , and sometimes more than one , who hold forth 
 and expound . from barrel , from waggon , from window , and even from 
 housetop , they address the crowd beneath ; nor be their eloquence 
 in vain . Hoarse , fierce shout rise up from the street , with broken 
 prayer and ejaculation . man be drunk with religion as with wine . 
 their face be flushed , their speech thick , their gesture wild . Sir 
 Stephen and Saxon smile at each other as they watch they , for they 
 know , as old soldier , that of all cause which make a man valiant in 
 deed and careless of life , this religious fit be the strong and the 
 most enduring . 

 in the evening I find time to look in upon my wound friend , and find 
 he prop up with cushion upon his couch , breathe with some pain , 
 but as bright and merry as ever . our prisoner , Major Ogilvy , who have 
 conceive a warm affection for we , sit by his side and read aloud to he 
 out of an old book of play . 

 ' this wound hath come at an evil moment , ' say Reuben impatiently . 
 ' be it not too much that a little prick like this should send my man 
 captainless into battle , after all our marching and drilling ? I have 
 be present at the grace , and be cut off from the dinner . ' 

 ' your company hath be join to mine , ' I answer , ' though , indeed , 
 the honest fellow be cast down at not have their own captain . have 
 the physician be to see you ? ' 

 ' he have leave even now , ' say Major Ogilvy . ' he pronounce our friend to 
 be do right well , but hath warn I against allow he to talk . ' 

 ' Hark to that , lad ! ' say I , shake my finger at he . ' if I hear a word 
 from you I go . you will escape a rough waking this night , Major . what 
 think you of our chance ? ' 

 ' I have think little of your chance from the first , ' he reply 
 frankly . ' Monmouth be like a ruin gamester , who be now put his 
 last piece upon the board . he can not win much , and he may lose all . ' 

 ' Nay , that be a hard saying , ' say I. ' a success might set the whole of 
 the Midlands in arm . ' 

 ' england be not ripe for it , ' the Major answer , with a shake of his 
 head . ' it be true that it have no fancy either for Papistry or for a 
 Papist King , but we know that it be but a pass evil , since the next 
 in succession , the Prince of Orange , be a protestant . why , then , should 
 we risk so many evil to bring that about which time and patience must , 
 perforce , accomplish between they ? besides , the man whom ye support have 
 show that he be unworthy of confidence . do he not in his declaration 
 promise to leave the choice of a monarch to the Commons ? and yet , in 
 less than a week , he proclaim himself at Taunton Market Cross ! who 
 could believe one who have so little regard for truth ? ' 

 ' Treason , Major , rank treason , ' I answer , laugh . ' yet if we could 
 order a leader as one do a coat we might , perchance , have choose 
 one of a strong texture . we be in arm not for he , but for the old 
 liberty and right of Englishmen . have you see Sir Gervas ? ' 

 Major Ogilvy , and even Reuben , burst out laugh . ' you will find he 
 in the room above , ' say our prisoner . ' never do a famous toast prepare 
 herself for a court ball as he be prepare for his battle . if the 
 King 's troop take he they will assuredly think that they have the 
 Duke . he hath be in here to consult we as to his patch , hosen , and I 
 know not what beside . you have well go up to he . ' 

 ' adieu , then , Reuben ! ' I say , grasp his hand in mine . 

 ' Adieu , Micah ! God shield you from harm , ' say he . 

 ' can I speak to you aside , Major ? ' I whisper . ' I think , ' I go on , 
 as he follow I into the passage , ' that you will not say that your 
 captivity hath be make very harsh for you . may I ask , therefore , that 
 you will keep an eye upon my friend should we be indeed defeat this 
 night ? no doubt if Feversham gain the upper hand there will be bloody 
 work . the hale can look after themselves , but he be helpless , and will 
 need a friend . ' 

 the Major press my hand . ' I swear to God , ' he say , ' that no harm 
 shall befall he . ' 

 ' you have take a load from my heart , ' I answer ; ' I know that I leave 
 he in safety . ' I can now ride to battle with an easy mind . ' with a 
 friendly smile the soldier return to the sick - room , whilst I ascend 
 the stair and enter the quarter of Sir Gervas Jerome . 

 he be stand before a table which be litter all over with pot , 
 brush , box , and a score of the like trifle , which he have either 
 buy or borrow for the occasion . a large hand - mirror be balance 
 against the wall , with rush - light on either side of it . in front of 
 this , with a most solemn and serious expression upon his pale , handsome 
 face , the Baronet be arrange and re - arrange a white berdash cravat . 
 his riding - boot be brightly polished , and the broken seam repair . 
 his sword - sheath , breastplate , and trapping be clear and bright . 
 he wear his gay and new suit , and above all he have don a most 
 noble and impressive full - bottom periwig , which droop down to his

 to his shin spur there be no speck or stain upon he -- a sad set - off 
 to my own state , plaster as I be with a thick crust of the Sedgemoor 
 mud , and disorder from have ride and work for two day without 
 rest or repose . 

 ' split I , but you have come in good time ! ' he exclaim , as I enter . 
 ' I have even now send down for a flask of canary . ah , and here it 
 come ! ' as a maid from the inn trip upstairs with the bottle and

 have in the whole world . it be the only survivor of a goodly family . pay

 buy ribbon for the next holiday . now , curse I if I can get this cravat 
 to fit unwrinkled ! ' 

 ' there be nought amiss with it , ' I answer . ' how can such trifle 
 occupy you at such a time ? ' 

 ' Trifles ! ' he cry angrily . ' trifle ! well , there , it boot not to 
 argue with you . your bucolic mind would never rise to the subtle import 
 which may lie in such matter -- the rest of mind which it be to have 
 they right , and the plaguey uneasiness when aught be wrong . it come , 
 doubtless , from training , and it may be that I have it more than other 
 of my class . I feel as a cat who would lick all day to take the least 
 speck from her fur . be not the patch over the eyebrow happily choose ? 
 Nay , you can not even offer an opinion ; I would as soon ask friend Marot , 
 the knight of the pistol . fill up your glass ! ' 

 ' your company await you by the church , ' I remark ; ' I see they as I 
 pass . ' 

 ' how look they ? ' he ask . ' be they powdered and clean ? ' 

 ' Nay , I have little leisure to observe . I see that they be cut 
 their match and arrange their priming . ' 

 ' I would that they have all snaphance , ' he answer , sprinkle himself 
 with scented water ; ' the matchlock be slow and cumbersome . have you 
 have wine enough ? ' 

 ' I will take no more , ' I answer . 

 ' then mayhap the Major may care to finish it . it be not often I ask help 
 with a bottle , but I would keep my head cool this night . let we go down 
 and see to our man . ' 

 it be ten o'clock when we descend into the street . the hubbub of 
 the preacher and the shouting of the people have die away , for the 
 regiment have fall into their place , and stand silent and stern , 
 with the faint light from the lamp and window play over their dark 
 serrie rank . a cool , clear moon shine down upon we from amidst fleecy 
 cloud , which drift ever and anon across her face . away in the north 
 tremulous ray of light flicker up into the heaven , come and go 
 like long , quiver finger . they be the northern light , a sight 
 rarely see in the southland county . it be little wonder that , come 
 at such a time , the fanatic should have point to they as signal 
 from another world , and should have compare they to that pillar of fire 
 which guide Israel through the danger of the desert . the footpath and 
 the window be crowd with woman and child , who break into shrill 
 cry of fear or of wonder as the strange light wax and wan . 

 ' it be half after ten by St. Mary 's clock , ' say Saxon , as we ride up to 
 the regiment . ' have we nothing to give the man ? ' 

 ' there be a hogshead of Zoyland cider in the yard of yonder inn , ' say

 his horn full . sink I , if they shall fight with nought but cold water 
 in they . ' 

 ' they will feel the need of it ere morning , ' say Saxon , as a score 
 of pikeman hasten off to the inn . ' the marsh air be chill to the 
 blood . ' 

 ' I feel cold already , and Covenant be stamp with it , ' say I. ' might 
 we not , if we have time upon our hand , canter our horse down the 
 line ? ' 

 ' of a surety , ' Saxon answer gladly , ' we could not do well ; ' so 
 shake our bridle we ride off , our horse ' hoof strike fire from 
 the flint - pave street as we pass . 

 behind the horse , in a long line which stretch from the Eastover gate , 
 across the bridge , along the High Street , up the Cornhill , and so past 
 the church to the Pig Cross , stand our foot , silent and grim , save when 
 some woman 's voice from the window call forth a deep , short answer 
 from the rank . the fitful light gleam on scythe - blade or gun - barrel , 
 and show up the line of rugged , hard set face , some of mere child 
 with never a hair upon their cheek , other of old man whose grey beard 
 sweep down to their cros - belt , but all bear the same stamp of a 
 dog courage and a fierce self - contain resolution . here be still 
 the fisher folk of the south . here , too , be the fierce man from the 
 Mendips , the wild hunter from Porlock Quay and Minehead , the poacher 
 of Exmoor , the shaggy marshman of axbridge , the mountain man from the 
 Quantocks , the serge and wool - worker of Devonshire , the grazier of 
 Bampton , the red - coat from the Militia , the stout burgher of Taunton , 
 and then , as the very bone and sinew of all , the brave smockfrocke 
 peasant of the plain , who have turn up their jacket to the elbow , 
 and expose their brown and cord arm , as be their will not when good 
 work have to be do . as I speak to you , dear child , fifty year 
 roll by like a mist in the morning , and I be rid once more down 
 the wind street , and see again the serried rank of my gallant 
 companion . brave heart ! they show to all time how little training it 
 take to turn an Englishman into a soldier , and what manner of man be 
 breed in those quiet , peaceful hamlet which dot the sunny slope of the 
 Somerset and Devon down . if ever it should be that England should 
 be strike upon her knee , if those who fight her battle should have 
 desert she , and she should find herself unarmed in the presence of her 
 enemy , let she take heart and remember that every village in the realm 
 be a barrack , and that her real stand army be the hardy courage and 
 simple virtue which stand ever in the breast of the humblest of her 
 peasant . 

 as we ride down the long line a buzz of greeting and welcome rise now 
 and again from the rank as they recognise through the gloom Saxon 's 
 tall , gaunt figure . the clock be on the stroke of eleven as we return 
 to our own man , and at that very moment King Monmouth ride out from the

 arm speak the ardour of his devoted follower . no bugle be to sound

 its movement . the clatter and shuffle of hundred of move foot come

 and we find ourselves fairly start upon the last journey which many 
 of we be ever to take in this world . 

 our road lie across the Parret , through Eastover , and so along the 
 winding track past the spot where Derrick meet his fate , and the lonely 
 cottage of the little maid . at the other side of this the road become

 so far our enterprise have succeed admirably . we be within half a

 ' be it not glorious ? ' whisper Sir Gervas , as we rein up upon the

 ' you speak as though it wear a cocking - match or a bull - baiting , ' I

 will be much as yesterday 's . zound ! though I have never rise to see

 ' I have tell friend Reuben such few thing as I should desire to be do

 I have know . be there no service of the sort which I can do for you ? ' 

 ' Hum ! ' say he , muse . ' if I go under , you can tell Araminta -- nay , let 
 the poor wench alone ! why should I send her message which may plague 
 she ! should you be in town , little Tommy Chichester would be glad to 
 hear of the fun which we have have in Somerset . you will find he at the

 be Mother Butterworth , too , whom I might commend to your notice . she 
 be the queen of wet - nurse , but alas ! cruel time hath dry up her 
 business , and she hath need of some little nursing herself . '

 say I. ' have you aught else to say ? ' 

 ' only that Hacker of Paul 's Yard be the good for vest , ' he answer . 
 ' it be a small piece of knowledge , yet like most other knowledge it hath 
 be buy and pay for . one other thing ! I have a trinket or two leave 
 which might serve as a gift for the pretty Puritan maid , should our 
 friend lead she to the altar . Od be my life , but she will make he read 
 some queer book ! how now , Colonel , why be we stick out on the moor 
 like a row of heron among the sedge ? ' 

 ' they be order the line for the attack , ' say Saxon , who have ride 
 up during our conversation . ' Donnerblitz ! who ever see a camp so 
 expose to an onfall ? oh for twelve hundred good horse -- for an hour of 
 Wessenburg 's pandour ! would I not trample they down until their camp 
 be like a field of young corn after a hail - storm ! ' 

 ' may not our horse advance ? ' I ask . 

 the old soldier give a deep snort of disdain . ' if this fight be to be 
 win it must be by our foot , ' say he ; ' what can we hope for from such

 ' there be troop to the right of we , ' I answer , peer through the

 ' Aye ! the Taunton burgher and the Frome peasant . our brigade cover

 well comrade , if their zeal do not outrun their discretion . they be 
 on their knee in the mud at this moment . ' 

 ' they will fight none the bad for that , ' I remark ; ' but surely the 
 troop be advance ! ' 

 ' aye , aye ! ' cry Saxon joyously , pluck out his sword , and tie

 very slowly and silently we creep on through the dense fog , our foot

 when our horse come to a sudden halt , and our whole advance be at a

 ' Sancta Maria ! ' cry Saxon , dash forward with the rest of we to find

 ' Forwards , forwards ! ' cry Sir Gervas and I , wave our sword . 

 ' it be no use , gentleman , ' cry a cornet of horse , wring his hand ;

 ' give I room for my horse , and I shall show ye the way across ! ' cry

 ' Nay , sir , for God 's sake ! ' say a trooper , lay his hand upon his

 ' let we see it , then ! ' cry Saxon , push his way through the crowd 
 of horseman . we follow close at his heel , until we find ourselves on

 to this day I have never be able to make up my mind whether it be 
 by chance or by treachery on the part of our guide that this fosse be 
 overlook until we stumble upon it in the dark . there be some who say

 ' for whom be ye ? ' shout a hoarse voice out of the haze .

 the battle be in this state when there rise a cry of ' the King , the

 it be no light matter which have so rouse the soldier 's admiration . out

 but wonderful as be the sight , there be , as ye may guess , my

 what hope be there to describe such a scene as that -- the crashing of

 but if we could claim it as a victory , the army in general could scarce

 and now it be every man for himself . in no part of the field do 
 the insurgent continue to resist . the first ray of the sun shin

 we be pass a small clump of alder bush when a loud manly voice

 to help he , and , indeed , might by our halt attract his pursuer to his

 be ! how be man cozen and cheat by the rare trapping and prance 
 steed , by the empty term of honour and of glory , until they forget 
 in the outward tinsel and show the real ghastly horror of the accursed 
 thing ! think not of the dazzle squadron , nor of the spirit - stir 
 blare of the trumpet , but think of that lonely man under the shadow of

 Redeemer be too lofty for they , and that there be no long any use in

 fellow - worm . what would we say if we read in Holy Writ of our Lord 
 have bless the batter - ram and the catapult of the legion ? 
 would we think that it be in agreement with his teaching ? but there ! 
 as long as the head of the Church wander away so far from the spirit of 
 its teaching as to live in palace and drive in carriage , what wonder 
 if , with such example before they , the low clergy overstep at time

 look back from the summit of the low hill which lie to the westward

 ' More slaughter , ' I say wearily . ' why will they force we to it ? ' 

 Saxon glance keenly from beneath his droop lid at the approach

 ' it be our friend who set the hound upon our track at Salisbury , ' he

 let I see ! this be the seventeenth , be it not ? '

 Rat I , if I have not misgiving that it might prove to be so . they say

 ' she . the she . the woman . your wife , sweetheart , betroth , what you

 promise to be quiet we shall cast free his arm . '

 ' then untie the rope . a brave man in misfortune hath ever my goodwill , 
 strike I dumb else ! Sergeant Gredder be my name , formerly of Mackay 's

 hear that the Privy Council intend to make such an example as will take

 ' it be an evil season , ' say I , ' when man who call themselves Christians

 during the whole of our long ride I find the worthy sergeant a true

 now that , he be in the keeping of a good man who would see that no harm 
 befell he . 



 Chapter XXXIV . of the coming of Solomon Sprent 

 the church of Gommatch be a small ivy - clothe building with a square 
 Norman tower , stand in the centre of the hamlet of that name . its

 Night be now draw in , but a few dim lamp , hang here and there upon

 on Thursday morning , the third day after the battle , we be all

 have be overlook by our guide and scout , we should have be

 which might have alter the result of the campaign.(note K , Appendix . ) 
 should ye hear or read , then , my dear child , that Monmouth 's rise

 have be to success . 

 I do not wish to say too much of the cruelty and barbarity of the 
 victor , for it be not good for your childish ear to hear of such

 noble cause . go now to Milverton , or to Wiveliscombe , or to Minehead , or 
 to Colyford , or to any village through the whole breadth and length of 
 Somersetshire , and you will find that they have not forget what 
 they proudly call their martyr . but where now be Kirke and where be 
 Feversham ? their name be preserve , it be true , but preserve in a 
 county 's hatred . who can fail to see now that these man in punish 
 other bring a far heavy punishment upon themselves ? their sin hath 
 indeed find they out . 

 they do all that wicked and callous - hearted man could do , know well 
 that such deed be acceptable to the cold - blooded , bigote hypocrite 
 who sit upon the throne . they work to win his favour , and they win it . 
 man be hang and cut down and hang again . every cross - road in the

 heap upon these long - suffer man ; yet it be proudly recount in

 at the end of a week or two news come of the fugitive . Monmouth , it

 he be drag , gaunt , unshaven , and tremble , out of a bean - field in

 of Saxon no news have come , good or bad , which encourage I to hope that

 ' scuttle ? '

 the good old seaman stay with I until late in the night , listen to

 late in August the judge start from London upon that wicked journey

 well , my dear grandchild , from night to night through this long and

 three day after our trial we be draw up in North Street in front

 a fishing - boat be lie alongside the brig . by the murky light of the

 chapter xxxvi . of the end of it all 

 and so , my dear child , I come to the end of the history of a 
 failure -- a brave failure and a noble one , but a failure none the less . 
 in three more year England be to come to herself , to tear the fetter 
 from her free limb , and to send James and his poisonous brood fly 
 from her shore even as I be fly then . we have make the error of 
 be before our time . yet there come day when folk think kindly of 
 the lad who have fight so stoutly in the West , and when their limb , 
 gather from many a hangman 's pit and waste place , be bear amid the 
 silent sorrow of a nation to the pretty country burial - ground where 
 they would have choose to lie . there , within the sound of the bell which 
 from infancy have call they to prayer , beneath the turf over which they 
 have wander , under the shadow of those Mendip and Quantock Hills which 
 they love so well , these brave heart lie still and peaceful , like 
 tired child in the bosom of their mother . requiescant - requiescant in 
 pace ! 

 not another word about myself , dear child . this narrative doth 
 already bristle with I be , as though it be an Argus which be a flash 
 of wit , though I doubt if ye will understand it . I set myself to tell ye 
 the tale of the war in the West , and that tale ye have hear , nor will 
 I be coax or cajole into one word far . ah ! ye know well how 
 garrulous the old man be , and that if you could but get to flush with 
 he he would take ye to the war of the Empire , to William 's Court , and 
 to the second invasion of the West , which have a well outcome than the 
 first . but not an inch far will I budge . on to the green , ye young 
 rogue ! have ye not other limb to exercise besides your ear , that ye 
 should be so fond of squat round grandad 's chair ? if I be spar to 
 next winter , and if the rheumatiz keep away , it be like that I may take 
 up once more the break thread of my story . 

 of the other I can only tell ye what I know . some slip out of my 
 ken entirely . of other I have hear vague and incomplete account . 
 the leader of the insurrection get off much more lightly than their 
 follower , for they find that the passion of greed be even strong 
 than the passion of cruelty . Grey , Buyse , Wade , and other buy 
 themselves free at the price of all their possession . Ferguson escape . 
 Monmouth be execute on Tower Hill , and show in his last moment 
 some faint trace of that spirit which spurt up now and again from his 
 feeble nature , like the momentary flash of an expire fire . 

 my father and my mother live to see the protestant religion regain its 
 place once more , and to see England become the champion of the reform 
 faith upon the continent . three year later I find they in Havant much 
 as I have leave they , save that there be more silver hair amongst the 
 brown braid tress of my mother , and that my father 's great shoulder 
 be a trifle bow and his brow furrow with the line of care . hand 
 in hand they pass onwards down life 's journey , the Puritan and the 
 Church woman , and I have never despair of the healing of religious 
 feud in England since I have see how easy it be for two folk to retain 
 the strong belief in their own creed , and yet to bear the heartiest 
 love and respect for the professor of another . the day may come when 
 the Church and the Chapel may be as a young and an eld brother , 
 each work to one end , and each joy in the other 's success . let 
 the contest between they be not with pike and pistol , not with court and 
 prison ; but let the strife be which shall lead the high life , which 
 shall take the broad view , which shall boast the happy and good 
 care - for poor . then their rivalry shall be not a curse , but a blessing 
 to this land of England . 

 Reuben Lockarby be ill for many month , but when he at last recover 
 he find a pardon await he through the interest of Major Ogilvy . 
 after a time , when the trouble be all blow over , he marry the 
 daughter of Mayor Timewell , and he still live in Taunton , a well - to - do 
 and prosperous citizen . thirty year ago there be a little Micah 
 Lockarby , and now I be tell that there be another , the son of the first , 
 who promise to be as arrant a little Roundhead as ever march to the 
 tuck of drum . 

 of Saxon I have hear more than once . so skilfully do he use his hold 
 over the Duke of Beaufort , that he be appoint through his interest to 
 the command of an expedition which have be send to chastise the savage 
 of Virginia , who have work great cruelty upon the settler . there 
 he do so out - ambush their ambush , and out - trick their most cunning 
 warrior , that he hath leave a great name among they , and be still 
 remember there by an indian word which signifieth ' the long - legged 
 wily one with the eye of a rat . ' have at last drive the tribe far 
 into the wilderness he be present with a tract of country for his 
 service , where he settle down . there he marry , and spend the rest of 
 his day in rear tobacco and in teach the principle of war to a 
 long line of gaunt and slab - side child . they tell I that a great 
 nation of exceed strength and of wondrous size promise some day to 
 rise up on the other side of the water . if this should indeed come to 
 pass , it may perhaps happen that these young Saxons or their child 
 may have a hand in the building of it . God grant that they may never 
 let their heart harden to the little isle of the sea , which be and must 
 ever be the cradle of their race . 

 Solomon Sprent marry and live for many year as happily as his 
 friend could wish . I have a letter from he when I be abroad , in which 
 he say that though his consort and he have start alone on the voyage 
 of wedlock , they be now accompany by a jolly - boat and a gig . one 
 winter 's night when the snow be on the ground he send down for my 
 father , who hurry up to his house . he find the old man sit up in 
 bed , with his flask of rumbo within reach , his tobacco - box beside he , 
 and a great brown Bible balance against his updrawn knee . he be 
 breathe heavily , and be in sore distress . 

 ' I have strain a plank , and have nine foot in the well , ' say he . ' it 
 come in quick than I can put it out . in truth , friend , I have not 
 be seaworthy this many a day , and it be time that I be condemn and 
 break up . ' 

 my father shake his head sadly as he mark his dusky face and laboured 
 breathing . ' how of your soul ? ' he ask . 

 ' aye ! ' say Solomon , ' that be a cargo that we carry under our hatch , 
 though we can not see it , and have no hand in the stowing of it . I have be 
 overhaul the sailing order here , and the ten article of war , but I 
 can not find that I have go so far out of my course that I may not hope to 
 come into the channel again . ' 

 ' trust not in yourself , but in Christ , ' say my father . 

 ' he be the pilot , in course , ' reply the old seaman . ' when I have a 
 pilot aboard o ' my ship , however , it be my way always to keep my own 
 weather eye open , d'ye see , and so I will do now . the pilot do not think 
 none the bad of ye for it . so I will throw my own lead line , though I 
 hear as how there be no sounding in the ocean of God 's mercy . say , 
 friend , d'ye think this very body , this same hull o ' mine , will rise 
 again ? ' 

 ' so we be teach , ' my father answer . 

 ' I would know it anywhere from the tattoo mark , ' say Solomon . ' they be 
 do when I be with Sir Christopher in the West Indies , and I would be 
 sorry to part with they . for myself , d'ye see , I have never bear ill - will 
 to any one , not even to the dutch lubber , though I fight three war 
 wi ' they , and they carry off one of my spar , and be hang to they ! 
 if I have let daylight into a few of they , d'ye see , it be all in good 
 part and by way of duty . I have drink my share -- enough to sweeten my 
 bilge - water -- but there be few that have see I cranky in the 
 upper rigging or refuse to answer to my helm . I never draw pay or 
 prize - money that my mate in distress be not welcome to the half of it . 
 as to the Polls , the less say the well . I have be a true consort 
 to my Phoebe since she agree to look to I for signal . those be my 
 paper , all clear and aboveboard . if I be summon aft this very night by 
 the great Lord High Admiral of all , I be not afeare that he will clap I 
 into the bilbo , for though I be only a poor sailor man , I have get his 
 promise in this here book , and I be not afraid of his go back from 
 it . ' 

 my father sit with the old man for some hour and do all that he could 
 to comfort and assist he , for it be clear that he be sink rapidly . 
 when he at last leave he , with his faithful wife beside he , he grasp 
 the brown but waste hand which lie above the clothe . 

 ' I will see you again soon , ' he say . 

 ' yes . in the latitude of heaven , ' reply the die seaman . his 
 foreboding be right , for in the early hour of the morning his wife , 
 bend over he , see a bright smile upon his tan , weather - beat 
 face . raise himself upon his pillow he touch his forelock , as be 
 the habit of sailor - man , and so sink slowly and peacefully back into the 
 long sleep which wake when the night have cease to be . 

 you will ask I doubtless what become of Hector Marot and of the strange 
 shipload which have set sail from Poole Harbour . there be never a word 
 hear of they again , unless indeed a story which be spread some month 
 afterwards by Captain Elias Hopkins , of the Bristol ship _ caroline _ , may 
 be take as bear upon their fate . for Captain Hopkins relate that , 
 be on his homeward voyage from our settlement , he chance to meet 
 with thick fog and a head wind in the neighbourhood of the great cod 
 bank . one night as he be beat about , with the weather so thick that 
 he could scarce see the truck of his own mast , a most strange passage 
 befell he . for as he and other stand upon the deck , they hear to 
 their astonishment the sound of many voice join in a great chorus , 
 which be at first faint and distant , but which presently wax and 
 increase until it appear to pass within a stone - throw of his vessel , 
 when it slowly die away once more and be lose in the distance . there 
 be some among the crew who set the matter down as the doing of the 
 evil one , but , as Captain Elias Hopkins be will not to remark , it be a 
 strange thing that the foul fiend should choose west - country hymn for 
 his nightly exercise , and stranger still that the dweller in the pit 
 should sing with a strong Somersetshire burr . for myself , I have little 
 doubt that it be indeed the _ Dorothy Fox _ which have sweep past in the 
 fog , and that the prisoner , have win their freedom , be celebrate 
 their delivery in true puritan style . whether they be drive on to the 
 rocky coast of Labrador , or whether they find a home in some desolate 
 land whence no kingly cruelty could harry they , be what must remain for 
 ever unknown . 

 Zachariah Palmer live for many year , a venerable and honour old man , 
 before he , too , be call to his father . a sweet and simple village 
 philosopher he be , with a child 's heart in his aged breast . the very 
 thought of he be to I as the smell of violet ; for if in my view of 
 life and in my hope of the future I differ somewhat from the hard and 
 gloomy teaching of my father , I know that I owe it to the wise word 
 and kindly training of the carpenter . if , as he be himself will not to 
 say , deed be everything in this world and dogma be nothing , then his 
 sinless , blameless life might be a pattern to you and to all . may the 
 dust lie light upon he ! 

 one word of another friend -- the last mention , but not the least 
 value . when Dutch William have be ten year upon the english throne 
 there be still to be see in the field by my father 's house a tall , 
 strong - boned horse , whose grey skin be fleck with dash of white . 
 and it be ever observe that , should the soldier be pass from 
 Portsmouth , or should the clank of trumpet or the rattle of drum break 
 upon his ear , he would arch his old neck , throw out his grey - streak 
 tail , and raise his stiff knee in a pompous and pedantic canter . the 
 country folk would stop to watch these antic of the old horse , and then 
 the chance be that one of they would tell the rest how that charger 
 have bear one of their own village lad to the war , and how , when the 
 rider have to fly the country , a kindly sergeant in the King 's troop 
 have bring the steed as a remembrance of he to his father at home . so 
 Covenant pass the last year of his life , a veteran among steed , well 
 feed and care for , and much give , mayhap , to tell in equine language 
 to all the poor , silly country steed the wonderful passage which have 
 befall he in the West . 



 APPENDIX 


 Note a.--hatre of learn among the Puritans . 

 in spite of the presence in their rank of such ripe scholar as John 
 Milton , Colonel Hutchinson , and other , there be among the independent 
 and Anabaptists a profound distrust of learning , which be comment upon 
 by writer of all shade of politic . Dr. South in his sermon remark 
 that ' all learning be cry down , so that with they the good preacher 
 be such as could not read , and the good divine such as could not 
 write . in all their preachment they so highly pretended to the Spirit , 
 that some of they could hardly spell a letter . to be blind with they be 
 a proper qualification of a spiritual guide , and to be book - learn , as 
 they call it , and to be irreligious , be almost convertible term . 
 none save tradesman and mechanic be allow to have the Spirit , and 
 those only be account like St. Paul who could work with their hand , 
 and be able to make a pulpit before preach in it . ' 

 in the collection of loyal ballad reprint in 1731 , the Royalist bard 
 harp upon the same characteristic : 

           ' we will down with university 
             where learning be profess , 
            because they practise and maintain 
             the language of the beast . 
            we will drive the doctor out of door , 
             and part , whate'er they be , 
            we will cry all part and learn down , 
             and heigh , then up go we ! ' 


 note B.--On the Speed of Couriers . 

 it be difficult for we in these day of steam and electricity to realise 
 how long it take to despatch a message in the seventeenth century , even 
 when the occasion be most pressing . thus , Monmouth land at Lyme on 
 the morning of Thursday , the 11th of June . Gregory Alford , the Tory 
 mayor of Lyme , instantly flee to Honiton , whence he despatch a 
 messenger to the Privy Council . yet it be five o'clock in the morning 
 of Saturday , the 13th , before the news reach London , though the 
 distance be but 156 mile . 


 note c.--on the Claims of the Lender of a horse . 

 the difficulty touch upon by Decimus Saxon , as to the claim of the 
 lender of a horse upon the booty gain by the rider , be one frequently 
 discuss by writer of that date upon the usage of war . one 
 distinguish authority say : Praefectus turmae equitum Hispanorum , cum 
 proelio tuba caneret , unum ex equitibus suae turmae obvium habuit ; qui 
 questus est quod paucis ante diebus equum suum in certamine amiserat , 
 propter quod non poterat imminenti proelio interesse ; unde jussit 
 Praefectus ut unum ex suis equis conscenderet et ipsum comitaretur . 
 Miles , equo conscenso , inter fugandum hoste , incidit in ipsum ducem 
 hostilis exercitus , quem cepit et consignavit Duci exercitus Hispani , 
 qui a captivo vicena aureorum millia est consequutus . Dicebat Praefectus 
 partem pretii huju redemptionis sibi debere , quod miles equo suo 
 dimicaverat , qui alias proelio interesse non potuit . Petrinus Bellus 
 affirmat se , cum esset Bruxellis in curia Hispaniarum Regis de hac 
 quaestione consultum , et censuisse , pro Praefecto facere aequitatem quae 
 praecipue respicitur inter milites , quorum controversiae ex aequo et 
 bono dirimendae sunt ; unde ultra conventa quis obligatur ad i d quod 
 alterum alteri prasstare oportet . ' the case , it appear , ultimately go 
 against the horse - lending praefect . 


 note D.--On the Pronunciation of Exquisites . 

 the substitution of the a for the o be a common affectation in 
 the speech of the fop of the period , as may be find in Vanbrugh 's 
 _ relapse _ . the notorious Titus Oates , in his effort to be in the mode , 
 push this trick to excess , and his cry of ' Oh Lard ! Oh Lard ! ' be 
 familiar sound in Westminster Hall at the time when the Salamanca 
 doctor be at the flood of his fortune . 


 note e.--hour - glass in pulpit . 

 in those day it be customary to have an hour - glass station in 
 a frame of iron at the side of the pulpit , and visible to the whole 
 congregation . it be turn up as soon as the text be announce , and a 
 minister earn a name as a lazy preacher if he do not hold out until 
 the sand have cease to run . if , on the other hand , he exceed that 
 limit , his audience would signify by gape and yawn that they have 
 have as much spiritual food as they could digest . Sir Roger L'Estrange 
 ( _ fable _ , Part II . Fab . 262 ) tell of a notorious spin - text who , have 
 exhaust his glass and be half - way through a second one , be at 
 last arrest in his career by a valiant sexton , who rise and depart , 
 remark as he do so , ' pray , sir , be pleased when you have do to 
 leave the key under the door . ' 


 note f.--disturbance at the old Gast House of Little Burton . 

 the circumstance refer to by the Mayor of Taunton in his allusion 
 to the Drummer of Tedsworth be probably too well known to require 
 elucidation . the haunting of the old Gast House at Burton would , 
 however , be fresh at that time in the mind of Somersetshire folk , 
 occur as it do in 1677 . some short account from document of that 
 date may be of interest . 

 ' the first night that I be there , with Hugh Mellmore and Edward Smith , 
 they hear as it be the washing of water over their head . then , 
 take the candle and go up the stair , there be a wet cloth throw 
 at they , but it fall on the stair . they , go up far , there be 
 another throw as before . and when they be come up into the chamber 
 there stand a bowl of water , look white , as though soap have be use 
 in it . the bowl just before be in the kitchen , and could not be carry 
 up but through the room where they be . the next thing be a terrible 
 noise , like a clap of thunder , and shortly afterwards they hear a great 
 scratch about the bedstead , and after that great knock with a 
 hammer against the bed' - head , so that the two maid that be in bed 
 cry out for help . then they run up the stair , and there lie the 
 hammer on the bed , and on the bed' - head there be near a thousand 
 print of the hammer . the maid say that they be scratch and 
 pinch with a hand which have exceed long nail . 

 ' the second night that James Sherring and Thomas Hillary be there , 
 James Sherring sit down in the chimney to fill a pipe of tobacco . he 
 use the tong to lift a coal to light his pipe , and by - and - by the tong 
 be draw up the stair and be cast upon the bed . the same night one 
 of the maid leave her shoe by the fire , and they be carry up into 
 the chamber , and the old man 's bring down and set in their place . 
 as they be go upstairs there be many thing throw at they which 
 be just before in the low room , and when they go down the stair the 
 old man 's breech be throw down after they . 

 ' on another night a saddle do come into the house from a pin in the 
 entry , and do hop about the place from table to table . it be very 
 troublesome to they , until they break it into small piece and throw 
 it out into the roadway . so for some week the haunting continue , 
 with rapping , scratch , movement of heavy article , and many other 
 strange thing , as be attest by all who be in the village , until at 
 last they cease as suddenly as they have begin . ' 


 note g.--monmouth 's progress in the West . 

 during his triumphal progress through the western shire , some year 
 before the rebellion , Monmouth first venture to exhibit upon his 
 escutcheon the lion of England and the lily of France , without the 
 baton sinister . a still more ominous sign be that he venture to touch 
 for the king 's evil . the appended letter , extract from the collection 
 of tract in the British Museum , may be of interest as first - hand 
 evidence of the occasional efficacy of that curious ceremony . 

 ' his grace the Duke of Monmouth honour in his progress in the West of 
 England , in an account of an extraordinary cure of the king 's evil . 

 ' give in a letter from Crewkhorn , in Somerset , from the minister of the 
 parish and many other . 

 ' we , whose name be underwritten , do certify the miraculous cure of 
 a girl of this town , about twenty , by name Elizabeth Parcet , a poor 
 widow 's daughter , who hath languish under sad affliction from that 
 distemper of the king 's evil term the joint evil , be say to be 
 the bad evil . for about ten or twelve year ' time she have in her right 
 hand four running wound , one on the inside , three on the back of her 
 hand , as well as two more in the same arm , one above her hand - wrist , 
 the other above the bending of her arm . she have betwixt her arm - pit a 
 swollen bunch , which the doctor say feed those six run wound . she 
 have the same distemper also on her left eye , so she be almost blind . 
 her mother , despair of preserve her sight , and be not of ability 
 to send she to London to be touch by the king , be miserably poor , 
 have many poor child , and this girl not be able to work , her 
 mother , desirous to have her daughter cure , send to the chirurgeon for 
 help , who tamper with it for some time , but could do no good . she 
 go likewise ten or eleven mile to a seventh son , but all in vain . no 
 visible hope remain , and she expect nothing but the grave . 

 ' but now , in this the girl 's great extremity , God , the great physician , 
 dictate to she , then languish in her miserable , hopeless condition , 
 what course to take and what to do for a cure , which be to go and touch 
 the Duke of Monmouth . the girl tell her mother that , if she could 
 but touch the Duke she would be well . the mother reprove she for her 
 foolish conceit , but the girl do often persuade her mother to go to 
 Lackington to the Duke , who then lie with Mr. Speaks . " certainly , " say 
 she , " I should be well if I could touch he . " the mother slight these 
 press request , but the more she slight and reprove , the more 
 earnest the girl be for it . a few day after , the girl have notice 
 that Sir John Sydenham intend to treat the Duke at White Lodge in 
 Henton Park , this girl with many of her neighbour go to the say 
 park . she be there timely wait the Duke 's come . when first she 
 observe the Duke she press in among a crowd of people and catch 
 he by the hand , his glove be on , and she likewise have a glove to 
 cover her wound . she not be herewith satisfied at the first attempt 
 of touch his glove only , but her mind be she must touch some part 
 of his bare skin , she , weigh his come forth , intend a second 
 attempt . the poor girl , thus between hope and fear , wait his motion . 
 on a sudden there be news of the Duke 's come on , which she to be 
 prepared rent off her glove , that be clung to the sore , in such haste 
 that she break her glove , and bring away not only the sore but the 
 skin . the Duke 's glove , as Providence would have it , the upper part hung 
 down , so that his hand - wrist be bare . she press on , and catch he 
 by the bare hand - wrist with her run hand , cry , " God bless your 
 highness ! " and the Duke say " God bless you ! " the girl , not a little 
 transport at her good success , come and assure her friend that she 
 would now be well . she come home to her mother in great joy , and tell 
 she that she have touch the Duke 's hand . the mother , hear what she 
 have do , reprove she sharply for her boldness , ask how she durst 
 do such a thing , and threaten to beat she for it . she cry out , " oh , 
 mother , I shall be well again , and heal of my wound ! " and as God 
 Almighty would have it , to the wonder and admiration of all , the six 
 wound be speedily dry up , the eye become perfectly well , and the 
 girl be in good health . all which have be discover to we by the 
 mother and daughter , and by neighbour that know she . 

 ' Henry Clark , minister ; Captain James Bale , & c & c. whoever doubt the 
 truth of this relation may see the original under the hand of the 
 person mention at the Amsterdam Coffee House , Bartholomew Lane , Royal 
 Exchange . ' 

 in spite of the uncouth verbiage of the old narrative , there be a touch 
 of human pathos about it which make it worthy of reproduction . 


 note h.--monmouth 's Contention of Legitimacy . 

 Sir Patrick Hume , relate a talk with Monmouth before his expedition , 
 say : ' I urge if he consider himself as lawful son of King Charles , 
 late deceased . he say he do . I ask he if he be able to make out 
 and prove the marriage of his mother to King Charles , and whether he 
 intend to lay claim to the crown . he answer that he have be able 
 lately to prove the marriage , and if some person be not lately dead , 
 of which he would inform himself , he would yet be able to prove it . 
 as for his claim the crown , he intend not to do it unless it be 
 advise to be do by those who should concern themselves and join for 
 the delivery of the nation . ' 

 it may be remark that in Monmouth 's commission to be general , date 
 April 1668 , he be style ' our most entirely beloved and natural son . ' 
 again , in a commission for the government of Hull , April 1673 , he be 
 ' our well - belove natural son . ' 


 note i.--dragooner and Chargers . 

 the dragoon , be really mount infantry , be provide with very 
 inferior animal to the real cavalry . from a letter of Cromwell 's 
 ( ' Squire Correspondence , ' April 3 , 1643 ) , it will be see that a 
 dragooner be worth twenty piece , while a charger could not be obtain 
 under sixty . 

 note j.--battle of Sedgemoor . 

 a curious little sidelight upon the battle be afford by the two 
 follow letter exhibit to the Royal Archaeological Institute by the 
 Rev. C. W. Bingham . 

 ' to Mrs. Chaffin at Chettle House . ' 

 ' Monday , about ye forenoon , July 6 , 1685 . ' 

 ' my dearest creature,--this morning about one o'clock the rebbell fall 
 upon we whil we be in our tent in King 's Sedgemoor , with their 
 whole army .... we have kill and take at least 1000 of they . they be 
 flee into Bridgewater . it be say that we have take all their cannon , 
 but sure it be that most be , if all be not . a coat with star on ' t be 
 take . '' tis run through the back . by some ' tis thought that the Duke 
 rebbell have it on and be kill , but most doe think that a servant wear 
 it . I wish he be call , that the war may be end . it be think 
 he will never be able to make his man fight again . I thank God I be very 
 well without the least hurt , soe be our dorsetshire friend . Prythee 
 let Biddy know this by the first opportunity . I be thyne onely deare , 
 TOSSEY . ' 

 BRIDGEWATER : July 7 , 1685 . 

 ' we have totally rout the enemy of God and the king , and can not hear 
 of fifty man together of the whole rebel army . we pick they up every 
 houre in cornfield and ditch . Williams , the late Duke 's valet de 
 chambre , be take , who give a very ingenious account of the whole 
 affair , which be too long to write . the last word that he say to he 
 be at the time when his army flee , that he be undo and must shift 
 for himself . we think to march with the General this day to Wells , on 
 his way homeward . at present he be 3 mile off at the camp , soe I can not 
 certainly tell whether he intend for Wells . I shall be home certainly 
 on Saturday at farth . I believe my deare Nan would for 500 pound 
 that her Tossey have serve the King to the end of the war . 

 I be thyne , my deare childe , for ever . ' 


 note K.--Lord Grey and the Horse at Sedgemoor . 

 it be only fair to state that Ferguson be hold by many to have be 
 as doughty a soldier as he be zealous in religion . his own account of 
 Sedgemoor be interesting , as show what be think by those who be 
 actually engage on the cause of their failure . 

 ' now besides these two troop , whose officer though they have no great 
 skill yet have courage enough to have do something honourably , have they 
 not for want of a guide meet with the aforesaid obstruction , there be 
 no one of all the rest of our troop that ever advance to charge or 
 approach as near to the enemy as to give or receive a wound . Mr. 
 Hacker , one of our captain , come no soon within view of their camp 
 than he villainously fire a pistol to give they notice of our approach , 
 and then forsook his charge and ride oft with all the speed he could , to 
 take the benefit of a proclamation emit by the king , offer pardon 
 to all such as should return home within such a time . and this he 
 plead at his tryal , but be answer by Jeffreys " that he above all 
 other man deserve to be hang , and that for his treachery to Monmouth 
 as well as his treason to the king . " and though no other of our officer 
 act so villainously , yet they be useless and unserviceable , as never 
 once attempt to charge , nor so much as keep their man in a body . 
 and I dare affirm that if our horse have never fire a pistol , but only 
 stand in a posture to have give jealousy and apprehension to the enemy , 
 our foot alone would have carry the day and be triumphant . but our 
 horse standing scatter and disunite , and fly upon every approach 
 of a squadron of theirs , command by Oglethorpe , give that body of 
 their cavalry an advantage , after they have hover up and down in the 
 field without think it necessary to attack those whom their own fear 
 have disperse , to fall in at last in the rear of our battalion , and to 
 wrest that victory out of their hand which they be grasp at , and 
 stand almost possessed of . nor be that party of their horse above 
 three hundred at most , whereas we have more than enough have they have any 
 courage , and be command by a gallant man , to have attack they 
 with ease both in front and flank . these thing I can declare with 
 more certainty , because I be a doleful spectator of they ; for have 
 contrary to my custom leave attend upon the Duke , who advance with 
 the foot , I betook myself to the horse , because the first of that 
 morning 's action be expect from they , which be to break in and 
 disorder the enemy 's camp . against the time that our battalion should 
 come up , I endeavour whatsoever I be capable of perform , for I 
 not only strike at several trooper who have forsake their station , but 
 upbraided diver of the captain for be want in their duty . but I 
 speak with great warmth to my Lord Grey , and conjure he to charge , and 
 not suffer the victory , which our foot have in a manner take hold of , 
 to be ravish from we . but instead of hearken , he not only as an 
 unworthy man and cowardly poltroon desert that part of the field and 
 forsook his command , but ride with the utmost speed to the Duke , tell 
 he that all be lose and it be more than time to shift for himself . 
 Wherebye , as an addition to all the mischief he have be the occasion 
 of before , he draw the easy and unfortunate gentleman to leave the 
 battalion while they be courageously dispute on which side the 
 victory should fall . and this fall most unhappily out , while a certain 
 person be endeavour to find out the Duke to have beg of he to 
 come and charge at the head of his own troop . however , this I dare 
 affirm , that if the Duke have be but master of two hundred horse , 
 well mount , completely armed , personally valiant , and command 
 by experienced officer , they would have be victorious . this be 
 acknowledge by our enemy , who have often confess they be ready 
 to fly through the impression make upon they by our foot , and must have 
 be beat have our horse do their part , and not tamely look on 
 till their cavalry retrieve the day by fall into the rear of our 
 battalion . nor be the fault in the private man , who have courage 
 to have follow their leader , but it be in those who lead they , 
 particularly my Lord Grey , in whom , if cowardice may be call 
 treachery , we may safely charge he with betray our cause . ' 

 Extract from MS . of Dr. Ferguson , quote in ' Ferguson the plotter , ' an 
 interesting work by his immediate descendant , an advocate of Edinburgh . 


 note l.--monmouth 's Attitude after Capture . 

 the following letter , write by Monmouth to the Queen from the Tower , 
 be indicative of his abject state of mind . 

 ' madam,--i would not take the boldness of writing to your Majesty till 
 I have show the King how I do abhor the thing that I have do , and how 
 much I desire to live to serve he . I hope , madam , by what I have say 
 to the King to - day will satisfy how sincere I be , and how much I detest 
 all those people who have bring I to this . have do this , madam , 
 I think I be in a fitt condition to beg your intercession , which I be 
 sure you never refuse to the distressed , and I be sure , madam , that I 
 be an object of your pity , have be cousene and cheat into this 
 horrid business . do I wish , madam , to live for live sake I would 
 never give you this trouble , but it be to have life to serve the 
 King , which I be able to doe , and will doe beyond what I can express . 
 therefore , madam , upon such an account as I may take the boldness to 
 press you and beg of you to intersaid for I , for I be sure , madam , the 
 King will hearken to you . your prair can never be refuse , especially 
 when it be beg for a life only to serve the king . I hope , madam , by 
 the King 's generosity and goodness , and your intercession , I may hope 
 for my life which if I have shall be ever employ in show to your 
 Majesty all the sense immaginable of gratitude , and in serve of the 
 King like a true subject . and ever be your Majesty 's most dutiful and 
 obedient servant , MONMOUTH . ' 

 the END
